Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Ninian Martin Stephen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of
the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight Commander of the Most Excellent Order of the British Empire, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of
the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: FIFTH PERIOD)
Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 140 of 1982)An Act to amend the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 consequent upon the enactment of the Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982.
Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 70 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Act 1963.
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 77 of 1982)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Air Navigation Legislation (Validation and Interpretation) Act 1982 (Act No. 95 of 1982)An Act to make provision in relation to the exercise of certain powers and the performance of certain functions under regulations and instruments under the Air Navigation Act 1920 and for related purposes.
Appropriation Act (No. I) 1982-83 (Act No. 1160Â£]982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1982-83 (Act No. 117 of 1982)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 (Act No. 118 of 1982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service, in relation to the Parliamentary Departments, of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Asian Development Fund Act 1982 (Act No. lSI of 1982)An Act to authorize a further contribution by Australia to the Asian Development Bank for the purposes of the Asian Development Fund.
Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Act 1982 (Act No. 133 of 1982)An Act to establish a Trust Fund for the purpose of the grant of financial assistance to the States and to the Northern Territory for expenditure on upgrading Australian roads in celebration of the Bicentenary in 1988, and for related purposes.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 126 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 125 of 1982)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 124 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981.
Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 127 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Bank Account Debits Tax Act 1982 (Act No. 141 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks.
Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982 (Act No. 142 of 1982)An Act relating to the collection of a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Berry Fruits) Act 1982 (Act No. 132 of 1982)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain berry fruits.
Bounty (Ships) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 145 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Bounty (Ships) Act 1980.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 154 of 1982)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. ISS of 1982)- An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964.
Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 71 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Act 1959.
Conciliation and Arbitration (Management of Organizations) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 143 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.
Cotton Levy Act 1982 (Act No. 147 of 1982)An Act to impose a levy upon certain cotton produced in Australia.
Cotton Research Act 1982 (Act No. 146 of 1982)An Act to provide for the collection of levy imposed by the Cotton Levy Act 1982 and to establish a Cotton Research Trust Account.
Customs and Excise Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 81 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 137 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 in relation to certain Customs securities, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff Act 1982 (Act No. 113 of 1982)- An Act relating to duties of Customs.
Customs Tariff (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1982 (Act No. 115 of 1982)An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 114 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 consequent upon the enactment of the Customs TariffAct 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 136 of 1982)An Act to repeal section 140fthe Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment (Countervailing Duties) Act 1982 (Act No. 68 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in relation to countervailing duties.
Defence Force (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1982 (Act No. 153 of 1982)An Act to amend certain Acts, and to enact transitional provisions, consequent upon the enactment of the Defence Force Discipline Act 1982, and for other purposes.
Defence Force Discipline Act 1982 (Act No. 1520f 1982)An Act relating to the discipline of the Defence Force and for related purposes.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 109 of 1982)- An Actto amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.1) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. II00f 1982)- An Act to amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.2) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 108 of 1982)- An Act relating to taxes on diesel fuel.
Dried Fruits Export Charges Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. l440f 1982)- An Act to amend the Dried Fruits Export Charges Act 1924.
Drought Assistance (Primary Producers) Act 1982 (Act No. 99 of 1982)An Act to make provison for the grant of financial assistance to primary producers who are affected by drought.
Export Market Development Grants Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 157 of 1982)An Act to amend the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, and for related purposes.
Fertilizers (Subsidy) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 69 of 1982)An Act to amend section 3 of the Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Act 1966 and to amend the Phosphate Fertilizers Bounty Act 1963.
Health Legislation Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 1120f 1982)An Act to amend the Health Insurance Act 1973, the National Health Act 1953 and the Health Insurance Commission Act 1973, and for related purposes.
Income Tax (Companies, Corporate Unit Trusts and Superannuation Funds) Act 1982 (Act No. 102 of 1982)- An Act to impose a tax upon incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Individuals) Act 1982 (Act No. 101 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax upon incomes, other than incomes of companies, of corporate unit trusts and of superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Rates) Act 1982 (Act No. 105 of 1982)An Act to declare the rates of income tax payable upon incomes other than incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 103 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Act 1979.
Income Tax (Rates) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 1040f 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Rates) AC11976.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.4) 1982 (Act No. 76 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.5) 1982 (Act No. 106 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.6) 1982 (Act No. 123 of 1982)- An Act relating to income tax.
Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 97 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Act 1980.
Live-stock Export Charge Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 83 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-slock Export Charge ACI 1977.

Northern Territory (Self-Government) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 130 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Northern Territory (Self-Government) Act 1978.

Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 78 of 1982)- An Act relating to certain remuneration and allowances.
Repatriation Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 100 of 1982)- An Act relating to repatriation and related matters.
Royal Commissions Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 139 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Royal Commissions Act 1902.
Salaries and Wages Pause Act 1982 (Act No. 134 of 1982)An Act to suspend for 12 months the powers of certain authorities in relation to the remuneration of certain persons, and for certain other purposes.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
TAXATION
Mr ARMITAGE-I refer the Prime Minister to the mounting campaign being conducted against his Government's proposed retrospective legislation directed at bottom-of-the-harbour tax avoidance schemes and his assurances that he would not be influenced in this campaign. Is the Prime Minister aware of reports that lobbyists for this campaign have visited him, the Treasurer and Treasury officials to push their case against the Government's measures? Are these reports true? Did the lobbyists include two former senior members of the Prime Minister's own staff? If so, in view of his statements that tax evaders are robbing the Commonwealth, will he now review his employment procedures to ensure that in future he does not again employ persons sympathetic to this objective?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I believe that the last part of the honourable gentleman's question is an undue, unreasonable and scurrilous reflection on people who have been asked to put a point of view on behalf of clients. It does not necessarily mean that it is their point of view at all. I hope all members of this Parliament will accept that all points of view have a right to be put. It does not mean to say that they will be accepted, but they at least have a right to be put. If anyone in this Parliament accepts the proposition that certain views have no right even to be put and argued, then he is saying something very fundamental which I think would be anathema to most of us.
The only people who have approached me are the two gentlemen the honourable member mentioned. It was a relatively brief telephone conversation. I do not believe any comfort would have been given out of that conversation to anyone who employed those lobbyists in that instance. I ought to make the point that a report on the second page of the Melbourne Sun News-Pictorial this morning is not correct. I understand a correcting statement is being issued in relation to that and that the State president of the party in Victoria supports what the Government is doing because he understands the reasons why we are doing it. Although nobody really likes the question of retrospectivity, in this instance and in these circumstances if there is what people regard as an

element of retrospectivity in the legislation, that cannot be avoided.
I make the point that the Government is not creating an obligation; it is only determined to collect funds which were due and which should have been paid in the normal course of events but which were avoided or evaded. As I indicated at a luncheon in Sydney on Friday, in a very real sense the beneficiaries of what happened are in receipt of stolen funds-whether one wants to say stolen from the generality of taxpayers or stolen from the Commonwealth I do not suppose it makes all that much difference-and the Government has every intention of collecting those funds.
COMMONWEALTH GAMES: VISAS AND DEPARTURE TAX
Mr JULL-My question is directed to the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs. With the Commonwealth Games just a few weeks away, what facilities are being made available for the quick processing of overseas visitors who may now wish to come to Brisbane as athletes, officials or spectators? Will the usual delays at overseas posts in issuing visitors' visas prevent perhaps thousands of people from coming to Brisbane for the Games? Is the Minister also aware of criticism being levelled at Australia for charging the $20 departure tax for competitors and officials leaving after the Games? Can this be waived? If not, will the Minister move to incorporate the tax in the price of the ticket as is done in most overseas countries?
Mr HODGES-The honourable member for Bowman has a keen interest in the Commonwealth Games for a number of reasons, not the least of which is the fact that he has a number of the very fine facilities for the Games situated within his electorate. Apart from the generous contribution of $lOm that this Government has made to the provision of facilities, it is government policy to assist the Commonwealth Games organising committee wherever possible. Part of that assistance will be that my Department, at its overseas posts, will issue visas to people promptly. Departmental officers have been made well aware of the dates of the Games and they have been told to ensure that people are not unduly delayed. The period of stay will allow people to come to Australia to view the Games and also to visit friends or relatives here. I recently decided that people in Australia from overseas who wish to take in the Games may have their temporary entry permits extended for that purpose.
Unfortunately competitors and officials, together with visitors, will have to pay the departure tax. The departure tax is required to be paid

Questions without Notice

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 801

by law. The honourable member for Bowman will be only too aware, because he made a study of the tourist industry both in Australia and overseas, that more than 100 countries charge departure tax. On the method of collecting that departure tax in the future, my Department has under consideration options for change so that tax will not be as visible. In many countries in which departure tax is charged the tax is not visible because it is built into the ticket price. We currently have that matter under review.

1982-83 BUDGET
Mr RUDOOCK-I ask the Prime Minister: What does the Budget do that will provide incentives to those making claims for increases in wages, particularly unions, to diminish those claims?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-There is a very substantial incentive in the Budget to moderate wage claims as a result of the totality of the measures that the Government introduced. The tax cuts, especially as they affect lower income people, the rebate for housing loan interest, the family allowance and the family income supplement for the lowest income families put a large number of Australian families in a very much better position than they were before the Budget. I believe it is reasonable, and the Australian Council of Trade Unions has recognised that it is reasonable, to think that this establishes a precondition for wage restraint through the course of this year.
The House might be interested in some figures which I have had taken out showing the position, before and after the Budget, of taxpayers with a dependent spouse and two children. The figures

show how much taxpayers would have to get in increased wages if they were to be as well off through claims on their employers as they are, in fact, as a result of the Budget. A taxpayer on $180 a week who, of course, benefits from the family income supplement is a little over $30 better off as a result of the Budget. If he were to get that income from his employer through increased wage claims, he would need a wage increase of $44 or a fraction under 25 per cent. The same person on $200 a week who is $22.40 better off as a result of the Budget would need a wage adjustment from his employer of nearly $33 to be as well off. This would represent an increase of between 16 per cent and 17 per cent in the wages cost to the employer. Somebody on $300 a week is between $17 and $18 better off as a result of the Budget and would need a wage adjustment from his employer of a little over $26 or between 8 per cent and 9 per cent to be as well off. A taxpayer with a dependent spouse and two children who is on $400 a week is $22.86 better off and would need a wage adjustment of $42 to be as well off through wage claims as he has been as a consequence of the Budget. These very substantial improvements will affect a very large number of Australian families. The improvements are real and significant. It is totally reasonable to ask and to expect that they will be taken into account in wage claims throughout the rest of this year.
1982-83 BUDGET
Mr HAYDEN-I ask the Prime Minister a question which is supplementary to the answer he has just given. Is it his expectation that the trade union movement, which represents a host of families in this country concerned about the depletion of their living standards through government policies in the past several years, should alone bear the burden of economic restraint? If not, what constructive proposals does he have in mind in relation to non-wage income, prices and the failure of his Government to clamp down effectively on tax avoidance and evasion? Furthermore--
Government supporters interjecting-
Mr HAYDEN-Well, the Liberal Party is more successful at it at an official level than any other group in the community, as honourable members will hear this afternoon.
Mr SPEAKER-The Leader of the Opposition will ask his question.
Mr HAYDEN-Does the Prime Minister expect the trade union movement to accept as a credible proposition that wage earning families will be better off as a result of the Budget when a

802 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Questions without Notice

man supporting a wife and two children on average weekly earnings after allowance for inflation adjustment in connection with taxation will be only a little over $3 a week better off this year than last year? Is it a fact that that represents for an average weekly wage earner an improvement of only one per cent in a year when inflation will increase by something like II per cent? In these circumstances does he genuinely expect the trade union movement to accept his sham deals?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The figures are not sham. The figures are real. Once the benefits start flowing from 1 November every Australian family in the categories that benefited from the Budget will know just how real they are. Families will know that the honourable gentleman's rhetoric is quite unreal. I do not think there is very much inducement on any employers at the moment to put up prices unnecessarily or unreasonably. Employers and Australian manufacturers are trying to survive and trying to sell against very tough competition, competition from companies overseas where the wage adjustments have been 5 per cent or 6 per cent a year and where, in many cases, employers and trade unions have willingly accepted a real reduction in living standards so that people can continue to have jobs. It is worth noting that through last year, and more so taking account of hours adjustments through last year, the real increase in wages was 3 per cent to 5 per cent. It is very much more than the rate of inflation. There had been an increase in the year before that. It is just not possible for anyone in this country to expect to go on being paid more in real terms and to work less and to have a sustainable situation within this country. The honourable gentleman does not understand that. I well know the document to which he committed himself last week. On any kind of reading of that document, one can see that it commits him to a minimum of full wage indexation together with a whole host of other and additional benefits through Budgets. If people think that that can be provided without real increases in productivity, without the capacity to sell Australian goods and without the willingness and the capacity to work together so that costs can be kept within bounds, the persons having those views are just living in a fool's paradise. Through this Budget the Government has established the realistic basis for co-operation throughout all parts of this community. Leaving aside the rhetoric of the Leader of the Opposition, I have little doubt that that will be achieved.
NEW SOUTH WALES DROUGHT
Mr SAINSBURY-My question is addressed to the Minister for Primary Industry and refers to

the crippling drought which is affecting many areas of New South Wales including, in particular, my electorate. Is the Minister aware that many small businesses in country towns and villages are now suffering just as much as the farmers? Are there any States in which Commonwealth-State disaster assistance can be extended to heavily affected small business? Can the Minister encourage the New South Wales Government to use Commonwealth and State funds to help alleviate the plight of country small business in drought affected New South Wales?
Mr NIXON-Yes, I am aware of the seriousness of the drought not only in New South Wales but also throughout eastern Australia. Indeed, the drought is getting more serious day by day. The fact of the matter is that assistance for small business is available and is currently being applied in Queensland. That assistance is to the tune of some $30,000 over a seven-year period at an interest rate of 5 per cent and with a repayment holiday period for three years. Similar assistance was granted last year in Western Australia where the activities of small businesses in rural areas were seriously affected because of the drought. In Western Australia, $40,000 was made available for a seven-year period loan with an interest rate of 4 per cent and a two-year repayment holiday. Similar assistance could be granted in New South Wales were the New South Wales Government to apply for it. It could be made part of the core arrangements that are presently in existence and in place in that State. I think it was last year when I invited New South Wales to join small business in the program. It did not do so. It may be that this year, because the drought is running into its fourth year in parts of that State, the New South Wales' Government will recognise the serious effect the drought is having on small businesses in country towns. It may be that New South Wales can be encouraged to apply a similar provision in New South Wales to that which is available in other States. I will certainly do my best to encourage New South Wales to do just that.

Will he, therefore, acknowledge that this Budget rips off taxpayers once again, taking as much as it gives?

Questions without Notice

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 803

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-If the Leader of the Opposition cannot understand that a lift in the basic threshold results in less tax being paid, if he cannot understand that a reduction in the basic rate from 32c to 30c in the dollar represents less tax being paid and if he cannot understand that the lifting of the second threshold to $19,500 results in less tax being paid, I have to confess that I cannot help him.
WAGE INCREASES
Mr CADMAN-The Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations is aware of figures announced recently by the Australian Bureau of Statistics regarding the 10 per cent increase in the cost of living and the 17 per cent increase in wages last year. Can the Minister inform the House whether there has been any assessment of the extent to which a shorter working week in many industries has added to labour costs? What will be the effect of a real increase in wages of about 7 per cent, plus a 35-hour week, on unemployment and inflation?
Mr MACPHEE-I am advised that the effect of moving from a 40-hour week to a 38-hour week so far has been an increase in the hourly rate of pay in the order of 5 per cent to 7 per cent. That would take the movement in average weekly earnings on an hourly rate to about 20 per cent to 23 per cent over roughly the past year-certainly well in excess of cost of living increases. I believe it behoves all members of this House to support the general call for wage restraint and for no further movements in the reduction of hours of work in order that we can improve our international competitiveness. There is simply no question about it-all our major trading partners have done much better than we have.
It is rare for a Liberal Minister of this House to praise the New South Wales Premier, Mr Wran, but I have had my attention drawn to a statement he made in the New South Wales Parliament on 19 August and I congratulate him upon it. The Premier said then, in introducing a Bill called the Remuneration (Special Provisions) Bill 1982, that his Government is firmly committed to a policy of wage restraint and that it will pursue that policy in the determination of New South Wales Public Service salaries. The Premier went on to say:
. . . this legislation is an expression of the need faced by both the Government and the community to practise wage restraint in a time of difficult economic circumstances.
The Premier indicated that his Government will be submitting to the New South Wales tribunal a case which, I assure the House, is the case which this Government presented to the Commonwealth Remuneration Tribunal and subsequently

to the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission. It is, in effect, that it is in the public interest that those bodies should take account of the state of the economy and the effects which their decisions have on salaries generally; that the total maximum increase should be 7 per cent, an amount which has found favour with the Commonwealth Remuneration Tribunal; and that it is no longer appropriate when examining wage levels to have regard for comparative wage justice arguments and the positions of similar office holders in the Commonwealth and State governments. It is worth closing with a quotation from the Premier. He said:
. . . in these circumstances it is essential that governments both exhort and practise wage restraint; that governments give leadership in the public sector so that all sections within the community, employers, employees, unions and indeed all wage-fixing tribunals will act responsibly and exercise moderation and restraint.
I congratulate the Premier on his position. He is following the example set by this Government. I hope that it is followed by the Australian Council of Trade Unions as well.
1982-83 BUDGET
Mr HAYDEN-I ask the Prime Minister a question supplementary to those two which he has already answered on the matter of tax.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman will be out of order if he attempts to ask two supplementary questions. I suggest he start afresh.
Mr HAYDEN-The content or substance of the question is supplementary to those which have been asked, but it is perfectly acceptable for me to ask questions of the Prime Minister on matters of tax even if they are, by their nature, supplementary to things which have been raised already.
Mr SPEAKER-If the honourable gentleman declares it to be a second supplementary question, it will be out of order. If he does not so declare it, it will be in order.
Mr HAYDEN-I will not quibble. I ask the Prime Minister: If his claim is correct that people in Australia will be paying less tax as a result of this Budget, will he explain how it comes about that total tax revenue in this Budget-in excess of 25 per cent of gross domestic product-will be as great as and probably greater than the level of last year, which was a record level of tax collections? I further ask: Does he recognise that in a contracting economy, as acknowledged in the Budget, this means a relatively higher overall tax burden for the community?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Subject to correction by the Treasurer, I understand that tax collections rise by 0.3 per cent in real terms, which

804 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Questions without Notice

is a very small increase indeed. The honourable gentleman seems to be indicating a remarkable fa-
cility for confusing his own fantasies with the facts of the present situation. The facts of the present situation are, if I may repeat them for the advantage of the honourable gentleman, and if other honourable members will forgive me while I repeat them: The basic tax threshold is being increased, and that means that less tax will be paid; the basic tax rate of 32Â¢ in the dollar comes down to 30Â¢, and that means that less tax will be
paid; the next threshold rises to $19,500, and that means that less tax will be paid; there will be a special rebate of $250 for welfare recipients, and that means that less tax will be paid; there is an increase in the spouse rebate for people with dependent children, and that means that less tax will be paid. There are also inducements for people to invest in the shares of their own companies. There are inducements for small investors to invest in Australian equity and for a tax rebate to be provided as a result of that, and those two measures also will mean that less tax will be paid than would otherwise be the case without this Budget. So, all in all, there are very substantial advantages to Australian families.
The Government has quite unashamedly structured its assistance to meet the needs of families, believing that that was the greatest requirement. It has also done a number of other things to assist the elderly. The family income supplement is again a social welfare first for Australia. This Government has introduced it-not a Labor government, not a socialist government. That particular reform is as far-sighted as was the original introduction of family allowances. The Leader of the Opposition might laugh at that reform, but a reform which enables people in this country to say, for the first time in a long while that low income earners on the lowest wages and with families will be significantly better off than welfare recipients, than somebody entirely supported by the welfare system, is a very significant advance indeed. The improvement for the lowest income working families in Australia through that particular reform will be very substantial.
I have indicated the situation of a taxpayer, with a dependent spouse and two children, on $180 a week, being $30 a week better off as a result of the changes. To get that increase through an employer he would have had to negotiate a wage claim of $44 a week, which for lowest income people just would not have been possible. The Leader of the Opposition may continue to say as much as he likes that people are not better off as a result of these changes, but the more he says it the less credible he becomes not only to members

of this House but also to people outside. So I hope very much that he repeats himself ad nauseum so that the more people the better can understand what a lot of nonsense he is talking.
SYDNEY (KINGSFORD-SMITH) AIRPORT
Mr IAN ROBINSON-I ask the Minister for Aviation: Is congestion at Sydney (Kingsford Smith) Airport reaching a crisis stage? Would a runway parallel to the main north-south runway accommodate prop jet, medium jet and commuter aircraft and divert the imminent air traffic crisis facing intrastate, interstate and international passengers travelling into and out of Sydney? What steps can the Government take to overcome the opposition of the New South Wales Government to this urgent development of Sydney Airport?
Mr FIFE-Last week I answered a question similar to that which has just been asked by the honourable member for Cowper. I take the opportunity in answering his question to stress again that there is an urgent need for some additional capacity in the Sydney area. The honourable member for Cowper has asked whether an additional runway parallel to the present north-south runway would overcome the difficulties. The short answer is yes. There are, however, two options within that general proposition. One option would be for the present north-south runway to be duplicated. A second option would be to have a shortened runway, a runway that would extend from the east-west runway south to the end of the present north-south runway. Although no decision has been taken, I think that shortened version would probably be the preferred option because it would satisfy the need and obviously it would be a great deal less expensive.
As I said last week, the Government is concerned to improve the position in the Sydney area. The honourable member asked what the Commonwealth can do to bring its view and the State's view closer together. I have been giving a great deal of consideration to the matter over recent weeks. I hope to be in a position soon to place some options before the Commonwealth Government. Obviously, if an option is approved that option will be discussed with the New South Wales Government. We need to have the cooperation of the State Government in relation to a possible new north-south runway at Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport and also in relation to determining the site for a new airport. The surface facilities are largely the responsibility of State governments and there would be no point in determining a site for a second airport unless the

Questions without Notice

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 805

State Government was party to that decision and was prepared to provide the necessary train and/or road access.
UNEMPLOYMENT
Dr THEOPHANOUS-Is the Prime Minister aware of a report in the National Times of 22 August headed 'Liberal's research shows unemployment no worry'? If so, does this reflect the Government's attitude to unemployment as indicated by the estimated increase of 3 per cent proposed in the Treasurer's Budget Speech? Has this Government abandoned all attempts to come to grips with the unemployment crisis?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-I had not seen that report in the National Times. It falls into the category of weekend newspapers which I do not read, but I thank the honourable gentleman for his information. The impression that the honourable gentleman sought to convey in his question is the reverse of the truth. A great many elements in this Budget are designed to assist the unemployed, and the young unemployed in particular. The very substantial increase in capital works and the increases in welfare housing obviously will add to construction and to employment in ways that would not otherwise be possible. The Australian Bicentennial Roads Development Program will assist local government and other authorities in providing greater work opportunities than would otherwise be the case.
There are a number of areas where we have provided significant assistance. The young people who are most at risk in this country are those who leave school as soon as they get to the end of the compulsory school age. Some years ago a secondary allowance scheme was introduced to encourage low income families to try to keep their kids at school for the last couple of years of schooling. In this last Budget we increased the means test for the secondary allowance by 44 per cent as well as increasing the actual allowance very considerably. The Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme allowances also have been considerably increased. As well, further adjustments have been made in the TEAS means test. This is obviously designed to make it easier, especially for lower income families, to keep young Australians in education or in training in the present climate. These are not the only things that are necessary.
The employment programs under the Department of Employment and Industrial Relations are being significantly expanded and a very large number of training places and a larger number of apprenticeships will be supported by the Commonwealth this year. Funds in these areas will have risen to a record $250m by the end of the

year. A large part of this is designed to assist, support and be helpful to young Australians.
There are other areas where I think this community could do a great deal to help young people, especially at school. I was at a forum yesterday morning where about 100 people under the Queen Elizabeth Trust arrangements had been brought to Canberra for a symposium for a week. They are mostly academically inclined, brighter and more intelligent students. I asked how many of them thought that the students who had manual skills, as opposed to intellectual skills, got a fair go in their own schools. A very large number of these young Australians believe that they did not get a fair go in their schools. Attention was drawn to this point by the Schools Commission in a report about two years ago. That has significantly influenced the money that we are spending on the school to work transition programs. I must say that the bias in many secondary schools, both government and, I would suggest, private, towards the academically inclined is a very substantial bias. Those with skills of a different kind, such as manual skills, often are ignored, pushed aside or given the feeling of frustration at those schools. This is an area where I would have thought all honourable members of this Parliament would show a concern at the opportunities that are offered LO young Australians. If the particular point that I am making is to be overcome it will involve a change of attitude by school authorities and by many teachers and many hundreds of thousands of schools throughout Australia.
DROUGHT RELIEF
Mr BAUME-My question is to the Prime Minister and follows an earlier question from the honourable member for Eden-Monaro on the impact of drought on small business. What action has the Government taken to assist all those affected by the current severe drought in New South Wales, and in my electorate in particular which has been declared drought affected for the second time in three years? What also has been done about the Victorian Premier's request for financial assistance for drought relief?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Commonwealth is very concerned about the increasing severity of the drought, not just in New ,South Wales but also in parts of Queensland and Victoria and indeed in significant parts of every State of the Commonwealth. I cannot recall in a long while a drought that at this point was as widespread as it now is. Many of the cropping areas, the wheat areas, are already a write-off for this season; for some that is a write-off for the second

806 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Questions without Notice

year in a row. The pastoral areas are facing great difficulty. Many of them could be saved by a substantial change in the weather pattern over the next two or three weeks, but there is no sign of
that coming. The concern that is being felt over vast parts of the whole Australian continent is very great indeed.
Once the relatively small base figures that need
to be met by States resources are met, the Commonwealth provides $3 for every $1 that is provided by the States. Over the last two years the Commonwealth has provided $100m or more to the States for drought relief. Because of the concerns that we feel, the Minister for Primary Industry has asked his Department to examine immediately the measures that are provided and supported by the States and by the Commonwealth. This information will be brought back before the Commonwealth Government very
shortly.
I have written to the State Premiers today asking them to support an urgent meeting of the Australian Agricultural Council, which the Minister for Primary Industry will convene, so that drought assistance measures right across the Commonwealth can be examined against the background of need and, in particular, the necessity to maintain the viability of the breeding flocks across Australia. There is not the slightest doubt that, as things stand at the moment, there will be a very substantial reduction in the sheep and beef herds in Australia. That reduction, in addition to the disasters that have occurred in the cropping areas, will have a substantial impact throughout the whole economy. In these circumstances governments need to assess and to re-examine the basic core measures that have had support. Some States, for example in relation to those core measures, have been more generous than others and have been prepared to support larger carry-on loans than other States.
These things need to be examined. I hope the States will examine these matters urgently because not only do we need adequate agreement between the Commonwealth and the States as to the kinds of measures that ought to be supported and promoted, but also do the States which have been administratively responsible for giving assistance to farmers hurt by the drought need to make sure that they can do so very quickly and very speedily with the minimum of administrative delays. I hope that all these things can be reassessed at the meeting that will be held. Before that meeting occurs the Commonwealth will be reexamining the measures that it believes can or should be supported. Whenever a statement can be made as a result of that and as a result of the

meeting it will be made. But we are looking for the fullest possible co-operation of the States, the Premiers and their Ministers in relation to this matter.

QUESTION TIME: SUPPLEMENTARY QUESTIONS
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-Mr Speaker, I ask you to help the

808 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Question Time: Supplementary Questions

House by expanding on a ruling you gave at Question Time. I make the request seriously because it is necessary that we understand the principles upon which the Standing Orders are applied so that there can be consistency, at least on our part, in understanding your probable responses. You will recall that at Question Time I asked a second question but in alternate order, as is the practice, of the Prime Minister and I mentioned that it was supplementary in respect of the matter which had been raised; that is, concerning taxation. You ruled that if I were to declare it to be a supplementary question it would be out of order. I note that Standing Order 151 declares:
Questions may be asked without notice. At the discretion of the Speaker supplementary questions may be asked to elucidate an answer.
That seems to mean, if we take up what seems to be the import of your ruling, Mr Speaker, that whether one declares a succeeding question, asked alternately as is the practice in this House, to be a supplementary question does not matter. Should the question be supplementary, in the sense that it seeks to extract more information or is an elucidation of an answer given earlier, you will rule it out of order. If that is to be the practice I suggest it is an extraordinary situation. By its implication-I presume this would not be your wish-it declares that in future a Minister, having given an answer to a question no matter how provenly misleading it may be, or wrong in fact, or just as a matter of natural and proper curiosity requiring more information, you, Mr Speaker, would not allow a further question, whether declared as supplementary, which sought to extract more information or an elucidation based on the answer given earlier. Mr Speaker, can you clarify this matter because it is very important to the proper functioning of this House? If I am correct in my interpretation of the implications of what you have put then you are imposing an extraordinary restriction on the Opposition. If I am not correct, can you concisely and substantially explain exactly what is the import of that ruling?
Mr SPEAKER-The Standing Orders formerly were specific and allowed only one supplementary question. The honourable gentleman asks me for my directions so he ought to listen and hear. The Standing Orders formerly stated that a question would be out of order if it were more than one supplementary question. In other words, one supplementary question was permitted and not otherwise. This gave rise to debate in the House in that an honourable member would ask a question not declaring it to be a supplementary question and some other honourable member

would challenge the question as being out of order on the ground that it really was supplementary, whether so declared or not. That gave rise to arguments in this House as to whether a question was, in fact, a second supplementary question. In order to overcome the challenge from the floor of the House the Standing Orders were amended to allow supplementary questions-plural-to be asked and that was left in the discretion of the Speaker. My interpretation is that the practice of the House hitherto will remain where a member declares himself to be asking a second supplementary question. That is what the honourable gentleman did. He asked one question, received the next subsequent call from the Opposition side and said: 'I ask a supplementary question'. He received the next call from the Opposition side and said: 'I ask a second supplementary question'. I then told him that if he persisted in calling it a second supplementary question I would rule it out of order. I adhere to that ruling; I think it is a correct ruling. That has been the intention of the House during the period I have been here.
Mr HAYDEN-According to the dictionary a supplement is 'something that is added to to overcome deficiencies'. So, Mr Speaker, you are giving a fairly restrictive interpretation. I think, with respect, we ought to clarify this point because it could be--
Mr SPEAKER-The question has been clarified very simply. If the honourable gentleman persists in asking a question, which he declares to be a second supplementary question, I will rule him out of order. I will not allow other honourable members on the floor of the House to raise a point of order and allege what is not declared to be a supplementary question to be, in fact, on an interpretation, a second supplementary question.
Mr HAYDEN-Mr Speaker, what you are really saying is that I can ask a supplementary question as often as I like so long as I do not tell anyone it is supplementary.
Mr SPEAKER-That is the fact.
Mr HAYDEN-With respect, Mr Speaker, I think it is a silly quibble about semantics.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman may think it is a silly quibble about semantics. He is entitled to that view. As I have indicated to him before, any view, no matter how outrageous or how far it departs from the Standing Orders or practice of this House, can be stated. I have merely to rule on it. That is my only responsibility.
Sir James Killen-Mr Speaker, I raise a point of order. I do not wish to be mean on the point. If

Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 809

the Leader of the Opposition would look at House of Representatives Practice he would find the decisions that have been made. I find the position quite clear. The Standing Orders were amended to enable the discretion to rest entirely with Mr Speaker. That is the position today. Otherwise Question Time would develop into a raging debate.
JOINT COMMITTEE ON FOREIGN AFFAIRS AND DEFENCE
Mr DOBIE (Cook)-On behalf of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence, I present the Committee's report on Namibia, together with the transcript of evidence.
Ordered that the report be printed.
Mr DOBIE-by leave-This report on Namibia is tabled at a time when Namibia is very much in the news. For some weeks now there have been reports that the South West Africa People's Organisation and South Africa are on the verge of agreement on a settlement which will lead to independence for the territory after some 62 years of administration by South Africa following its occupation of the former German territory during the First World War. If agreement is reached on a settlement, and Namibia proceeds to independence early in 1983, then will end one of the longest running disputes to have occupied the United Nations since its formation in 1945.
Between the two world wars Namibia was administered by South Africa under a League of Nations mandate. However, South Africa refused to convert the mandate into a United Nations trusteeship agreement after World War II and has continued to administer the territory in defiance of repeated United Nations demands to withdraw and turn the territory over to UN administration prior to its independence. South African refusal to leave the territory led the South West Africa People's Organisation-SWAPO-to begin a guerrilla campaign against South Africa in the mid-I 960s. The guerrilla war has increased in intensity over the years and has cost several thousand lives. Although in recent years South Africa has acknowledged Namibia's right to independence, so far it has not been able to agree on acceptable terms for a transition to independence, despite almost continuous negotiations involving the United Nations, a group of Western countries known as the Contact Group and SWAPO.
The major obstacle to agreement in the current negotiations appears to be South Africa's demand that a settlement in Namibia be accompanied by a withdrawal of Cuban troops from Angola. Cuban troops were invited into Angola in 1975 by that

country's Popular Movement for the Liberation of Angola-MPLA-Government to counter a South African incursion and to help the MPLA in its civil war with the other major group in Angola, the National Union for the Total Independence of Angola, UNITA. Cuban troops have remained since and as recently as three weeks ago were engaged in large scale operations against UNITA forces. South Africa argues that the continued presence of an estimated 20,000 Cuban troops in
Angola during a Namibian transition to independence could encourage SWAPO to continue its guerilla war should it lose independence elections. South Africa is also concerned at what it sees as a growing Soviet influence in the region. It appears to be using the issue of Namibian independence to try to reduce that influence by making a Cuban withdrawal its condition for an end to the guerrilla war.
SWAPO has rejected the South African linkage of a Cuban withdrawal from Angola with the issue of Namibian independence and Angola has stated that it will not ask Cuban forces to leave until Namibia is independent and until South African cross-border raids stop. Whether South Africa or SWAPO and Angola will modify their positions on the Cuban issue remains to be seen. South Africa is under Western pressure to agree to a settlement while SWAPO is under similar pressure from the front-line states. The front-line states, particularly Angola, would like the Namibia issue settled so they can concentrate on their growing economic problems. Front-line states such as Zimbabwe, Tanzania, Zambia and Botswana are also concerned that the longer the guerrilla war continues in Namibia, the greater the opportunity for unrest and a possible increase in Soviet influence in the region.
Of the six front line states, even the two most Soviet aligned countries, Angola and Mozambique, are showing signs of disillusionment with the Soviet Union. Their experience generally is that Soviet military aid for independence struggles has not been followed through with satisfactory post-independence relationships. Soviet development aid is considered inadequate and often ill suited to their needs. African trade with the Soviet bloc frequently results in major deficits for the countries concerned. Both Angola and Mozambique in recent months have been seeking closer economic links with the West. While the front line states would like a Namibia settlement and are impressing this on SWAPO some analysts have argued that SWAPO is also under pressure from the Soviet Union, as its main backer and supplier of weapons, to delay negotiations in order to continue destabilisation of the region.

810 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs

This would give the Soviet Union continued justification for maintaining its presence, including what are generally considered to be its surrogate forces, the Cubans.
In the current negotiations South Africa and SWAPO are reported to have reached agreement
on nearly all issues except that of the linkage of a Cuban troop withdrawal with a settlement. Involving, as it does, a third party, Angola, this broadens the question of a Namibia settlement into a regional settlement. The Committee, in its report, takes the view that this will only further
delay Namibian independence. Angola, unless strongly pressured by fellow front line states, is unlikely to agree to give up the Cuban presence unless it obtains guarantees from South Africa that it will cease its raids into Angola and cease supporting UNITA. The South African demand broadens the Namibia issue and could make a solution more difficult.
The Committee also considers that Walvis Bay should be part of an independent Namibia, and that its retention by South Africa after independence could continue to cause tensions in the area. Walvis Bay is the only deep water port on the Namibian coast. Its continued retention by South Africa would provide South Africa with a degree of economic influence over Namibia which would be unacceptable to most independent countries. Continued retention of the port and its surrounding territory by South Africa would ignore claims for the incorporation of the port based on considerations of geography, history, economics and ethnic origin, as well as political and, equally importantly, moral considerations.
In the chapter on Australia and Namibia, the Committee details the attitudes of present and previous governments concerning Namibia, outlines Australian aid and examines areas of possible international competition between Australia and an independent Namibia, particularly in the export of diamonds and uranium. The Committee endorses Australian participation in a United Nations transitional assistance group presence in Namibia in the period prior to independence. It argues that while Australia does not have a major direct role to play in the Namibia dispute and while it does not have a direct strategic interest in Namibia, the region of which Namibia is a part is of strategic concern. Instability in the southern Africa region, in its effect on the major powers, could be of considerable importance to Australia. Therefore, a Namibia settlement is in Australia's interests, even apart from the equally valid considerations of morality and justice. Namibia has a sound economic basis for independence and the sooner independence is

granted the sooner will its people be able to live in peace, equality and personal dignity. Before I conclude I pay a tribute to Mr John Vanderwyk, the Committee's acting secretary, Mrs Maureen Weeks, its research officer and to alI members of the staff who have alIowed us to complete this report under very difficult circumstances.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan)-by leave-I join with the Chairman of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence in expressing thanks to the various members of the Committee staff for the work they have put into the preparation of this report. I also say that a tribute must be paid to the members of the sub-committee in the previous Parliament as a substantial proportion of the work on this report was done in the previous Parliament. The work done by the Committee in this Parliament has largely been a matter of bringing the report up to date. Therefore, the judgments in it reflect to a considerable degree those of previous committees and previous Committee members.
I would like to highlight a number of points about the Committee's report. The first is that I think that when the report is printed and the general public gets to read it people will find that the understanding within Australia of the historical record of this issue has been substantialIy advanced. Basically, the report deals with the historical record and only secondarily with recommendations on what contemporary policies ought to be. The report is generally supportive of the direction of government policy. It tends to emphasise the need in the minds of the majority of the Committee's members for a certain amount of delicacy and moderation in the negotiating process. I think-if I can express one personal reservation-that occasionally the recommendations are a little too placatory in this regard. Nevertheless, they are reasonably welI argued points.
One matter that is contained in the report, which will be increasingly important over the next few months and, therefore, bears particular reading, is an analysis of the claims by the various representatives of Namibian independence movements and South Africa to the Walvis Bay area. Readers will find on page 192 of the report a map which gives the location of Walvis Bay-how central it is to the advancement of the Namibian economy after independence, how very distant it is from South Africa and what an absurdity it would be if independence were to be granted and that particular area not included within Namibia shortly thereafter. There is a very real possibility that that could become a running sore and a source of continuing dispute between Namibia

Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 811

and South Africa. Much of the advantage that would be gained by Namibian independence in strategic terms as opposed solely to social justice considerations could well be lost if the South Africans do not change their current position on the issue.
The Committee takes up this point and advises,
in one of its recommendations, that it acknowledges the legal argument presented by South Africa in its claims to Walvis Bay but notes also shifting interpretations of international law in relation to such claims. It expresses the opinion that, whatever in Australia's view the legal position might be, it should be subordinated to political, moral and other considerations based on geography, history, economics and ethnic origin.
Further, the Committee has advised the South African Government that it does not consider that South Africa's concern at the unlikely possibility of a substantial Soviet base at Walvis Bay outweighs the value to the West of a resolution of disputes in the region. Honourable members may well be aware that South Africa seeks to convince the Western powers that are assisting in the negotiation of Namibian independence that for Walvis Bay not to remain in South African hands would encourage a substantial Soviet naval presence in the area and a threat to Western sea lanes.
I think the first point that needs to be made on
that claim is that suggestions that Soviet bases are developing in the area often prove to be something of a chimera. When they are genuine, as was the case in Berbera in Somalia, they were short lived. In the case of Alexandria in Egypt, they were also short lived. The Soviets have been notably unsuccessful in converting any other of their friendly relations with other Indian Ocean powers or, in this case, a southern Atlantic power, into meaningful substantial military strategic positions. What is absolutely certain, as the Chairman pointed out, is that, if there is not a resolution of this particular issue, the instability in that area that will be created will be very much to the detriment of the interests of the West, Australia and, I suspect, South Africa. If the Walvis Bay issue is not settled, the possibility of South Africa maintaining peaceful and sensible relationships with Namibia will be pretty short lived indeed and many of the advantages, as I said before, in strategic as opposed to social justice terms of a resolution of the conflict will be lost. A further point that the Committee detailed, I think particularly effectively, is the relationship between the development of the Namibian economy and Australia. The Chairman briefly passed over the question of diamonds and uranium. In some areas Australia is a direct competitor. Of course, we on this side of

the House would probably take Australia out of one area of competition over a period. That may well be very considerably to the advantage of the Namibian economy.
On the general economic question, there is a very substantial analysis of the record in labour relations and in economic exploitation of the Namibian area and its people over a considerable period of South African rule. It has been a substantial and very damaging exploitation. There have been reforms recently, particularly in the treatment of labour in Namibia, which have been welcomed by the Committee but, as it points out, the long term consequences of that exploitation will be very severe and will poison race relations probably for some considerable time. The Committee notes too that there is justification in charges of exploitation and excessive repatriation of profits by foreign companies. It disputes the argument that the desire to continue that exploitation is a substantial reason in South Africa deliberately or unduly delaying independence and says that the South African view on that I think rightly-could be seen more as a product of strategic and military calculation than a desire to continue economic exploitation. Nevertheless, the effect of the continued administration of Namibia by South Africa unquestionably results in these trends continuing as it is South African policy that they should continue.
The Committee takes one view with which I do not entirely agree; that is, that little purpose is served in raking over the past in regard to economic relations in order to consider the question of reparations. I still think there is some value in discussing that even though the likelihood of South Africa paying reparations is remote because that, at least, is a useful bargaining tool that emphasises further the moral case that the South West Africa People's Organisaton-SWAPO-has for a rapid conclusion of an agreement on independence. The Committee expresses to SWAPO on a number of occasions throughout the report the view that compromises ought to be made with South Africa to achieve independence as quickly as possible. I think that some of us on this side of the House would be a little concerned that perhaps too much emphasis is placed on SWAPO not giving up its claims, for the purposes of negotiation, to be the sole authoritative negotiating body of the South West African people, the Namibian people. Nevertheless there has been an indication by SWAPO representatives in the past that they are prepared to do that for the period of negotiations and let an election take its course. I do not think

812 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs

that there would be too many people on the Committee who did not believe that in the first postindependence election SWAPO would be successful in much the same way as Mugabe was in Zimbabwe. As I said at the outset, the report is a useful historical record which I think will add substantially to public understanding of the issues in a complicated and important area.
DISTINGUISHED VISITORS

JOINT COMMITTEE ON FOREIGN AFFAIRS AND DEFENCE
Debate resumed.
Mr SHIPTON (Higgins)-by leave-The report on Namibia by the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence follows one tabled two years earlier on Zimbabwe. The Committee, first through its Sub-committee on Southern Africa and then through the Sub-committee on Middle Eastern and African affairs, has been closely following events in the southern Africa region for some years. It is a bipartisan report and has the approval of members of both sides of the Parliament, who are in agreement on issues in the report. That is a point which is often missed in the reporting of the tabling of such reports in the Parliament. The Committee is concerned that instability in the southern Africa region may have wider repercussions, particularly for relations between the major powers. At the moment the focus of instability in the region is Namibia and the tabling of this report is timely.
The territory of Namibia has been administered by South Africa for some 60 years. For many years South Africa wanted to incorporate Namibia into its own territory but for the past few years it has acknowledged the territory's right to become an independent nation. However, despite years of almost continuous negotiations no formula for independence acceptable to both South Africa and the South West Africa People's Organisation-SWAPO-has yet emerged. The current round of negotiations, known as the three phase settlement proposals, appear to be the most promising yet. Reports that a settlement is near have appeared over the past few weeks, although they have become more cautious in their forecasts since the South African raid into Angola about two weeks ago. That raid is seen by some as yet

another delaying tactic on the part of South Africa while other commentators view it as part of manoeuvres on the part of South Africa and SWAPO to gain maximum advantage prior to a ceasefire and settlement.
Whether the three-phase settlement plan achieves success will become apparent in the coming weeks and months. But, whatever the outcome the Committee's report has appeared at what may well be a turning point in the affairs of the Territory. The Committee's report is a comprehensive one. It traces the history of the Namibia dispute from its origins in a League of Nations mandate to the present. It considers a number of political, social and economic issues, including the controversy over the status of Walvis Bay, and examines Australian involvement and interest in the territory.
Earlier I said that the Namibia dispute was important in terms of its effects on the stability of the southern Africa region. I did not mean to imply by that statement that the strategic issues are the only reasons for the Committee's interest, Namibia is important also in terms of international moral issues-the right of peoples to govern themselves, to be free and to regard themselves as equals whatever their racial or ethnic origin. It is unfortunate that the South African administration of Namibia has been in terms of that country's racial policies, and it is only since about 1977 that South Africa has begun dismantling its apartheid policies in Namibia. South Africa has acknowledged international opinion and moved to abolish discrimination in that territory. In South Africa itself today I believe that recognition ought to be given to the efforts to effect change by Prime Minister Botha and I believe he should receive more international support for what he is trying to achieve. If he is successful he will show that managed change can take place peacefully for the benefit of all South Africans. It is to be hoped that Namibia will soon become an independent nation and that the divisions based on race, which have been a feature of South Africa's administration of the territory, will become a thing of the past.
EMPLOYMENT OPPORTUNITIES IN RURAL AND PROVINCIAL AUSTRALIA
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MilIar)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the honourable member for Ballarat (Mr Mildren) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:

Employment Opportunities in Australia

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 813

The Government's failure to protect existing employment and promote new employment opportunities in rural and provincial Australia.

More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr MILDREN (Ballarat) (3.8)-Throughout the length and breadth of rural and provincial Australia there is unanimous concern for the future of employment and hence the economic viability of these areas. That this ailing Government has failed even those people whom it is reputed it serves most of all will not be forgotten by the people who live outside the metropolitan cities of this nation. There is a worry-indeed, a major concern-among all sections of the community in these areas about the appalling shallowness and limitations of the Fraser Government's attitude to manpower planning. We have heard recently about the fate of the Government's decision to sell the Bendigo Ordnance Factory following the report of the now infamous razor gang's recommendations.
Following the resounding victory of the State Australian Labor Party candidate, Mr David Kennedy, over the incumbent Liberal at the election earlier this year, the Federal Government recognised that its tenancy in Bendigo was about to be terminated. The recent piece of amateur theatrics which we witnessed in this House will not reassure the people of Bendigo and will not engender in them trust in the Federal Government or their local member. There is no doubt they will treat him as he deserves and cast him into the political scrap heap. The possibilities for sound usage of a resource such as the Ordnance Factory would have stimulated economic activity in Bendigo. Had the Government had an adequate policy of manpower planning it would have enabled the Ordnance Factory to engage more systematically in commercial work. As has been pointed out in the joint judgment of Justices Gavan Duffy, Evatt and McTiernan, as quoted in the minority report of the Select Committee on the Government Clothing and Ordnance Factories, the full usage of the specialist staff of that factory engaged in the manufacture of naval and military equipment would have a very important effect not only upon employment but also for the future of our defence arm. The justices stated:
Consequently the sales of clothing . . .
Referring to an earlier decision-
outside the regular military forces are not to be regarded as the main or essential purpose of this part of the business but as

incidents in the maintenance for war purposes of an essential part of the munitions branch of the defence arm.
How much more significant is the maintenance of a factory engaged in the manufacture of heavy ordnance? At any time in the history of conservative governments at this Federal level they could have, had they been concerned, established a policy that would have enabled a factory, such as the Ordnance Factory, to be maintained at the level that I indicated. Conservative governments have been in office for 30 years out of the last 33 years. In a provincial city such as Bendigo such a policy would have been crucial to the maintenance of jobs and the economic welfare of that community.
While the situation in Bendigo is of particular significance, given the Government's crude attempts to sell off people's assets to its friends, the failure of the Government to articulate an adequate manpower policy is placing the future of other rural and provincial areas in similar jeopardy. The Government has relied upon the concurrence of market forces both nationally and internationally and the beneficence of climatic conditions to see the country through. Hence when difficult times arise the chickens come home to roost. In the country these chickens show all the signs of neglect. I represent a provincial electorate. In each of the four areas depending upon manufacturing industry-namely, Ballarat, Maryborough, Kyneton and Daylesforddependence upon positive government initiatives is crucial to their future.
The central highlands region of Victoria is the area second most dependent upon tariff protection for manufacturing industry. Each of these centres has a number of industries that are extremely important for maintaining jobs and ensuring a flow of cash into the community. According to the last released figures from the Commonwealth Employment Service, in 1981 Ballarat had approximately 9.5 per cent of its work force registered for work. Since then, though repeated representations have been made to have these figures released, we have no current data to enable local planning to take place. That is a disgrace. The same situation occurs throughout rural and provincial Australia. The Government does not care about rural and provincial Australia. Therefore it is necessary to infer from data gained from the Department of Social Security the gross picture of unemployment in these areas. In June 1981 in the Ballarat region there were 2,139 unemployment benefit recipients. By June 1982 the figure had risen to 2,80 I-an increase of 662 poor souls who have been cast adrift by this Government. That is an increase of 30.9 per cent in a 12-month period. The Government has not given any indication of

814 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Employment Opportunities in Australia

concern for these people. We do not know the breakdown of unemployment in the region according to sex and age. Likewise, we have no
breakdown according to occupation. It would be interesting to find whether unemployment benefits correspond with the actual registered figure for the unemployed. If they do, the figure would go up from 3,315 to 4,641-an increase of 40.3 per cent. It is disgusting! We know that there is an upsurge of concern from welfare agencies in finding the capacity to cope with the demands on their services and in spreading the available resources far beyond their limit.
The picture I am painting is not just confined in its applicability to Ballarat and its regions. It is relevant to rural and provincial Australia generally. When the Government assumes in its Budget that there will be no growth in output and, to all intents and purposes, no productivity growth it is admitting that employment will deteriorate and that the ranks of the unemployed will be swollen once again. We can expect a further fall in employment throughout the nation of at least 65,000 during 1982-83. Mr Deputy Speaker, as you would know, many of these people will be in provincial and rural Australia. By the second half of 1982-83 we will be looking at unemployment rates nationally of between 8 per cent and 10 per cent. This means a depressed army of unemployed in excess of 600,000 people. Without an unemployment policy how can this nation possibly cope with the social and economic catastrophe that is in our midst today?
In rural and provincial areas the impact will be greater still. In cities such as Portland, Townsville, Geelong, Ballarat and Bendigo the buffers for these people just do not exist. We require programs for employment. The recently
appointed mayor of Ballarat, Councillor Don Woodward, referred to local government's responsibility to create employment. How much more important is it for the Federal Government to have a job creation program? It is rather ironic that whilst there is a vast army of unemployed there are areas of employment where specialised personnel are required. Had the Government been concerned to create jobs it would have created the necessary machinery to do so. It is a fact that there is a shortage of workers with the necessary skills to use some of the most modern farm machinery. There is also a shortage of shearers. Again, oddly enough, if the country only had the number of shearers indicated by the figures from the Australian Bureau of Statistics it would take two years to shear the flock. In other words, the Government is not even concerned to have adequate figures on which to plan.

There are many areas in which job opportunities could be created in rural areas if the Government were concerned to do so. Let us take an example which will be of concern to Tasmanians. There is much scope for increased jobs in the fishing industry if the Government were concerned to see that Australia exploited its 200-mile zone rather than give it to foreign interests. The Government has made much of the training programs that it has developed. Oddly, there is a lot of double talk about those programs. While the amount of money allocated for manpower and training programs increased in 1981-82 by 21 per cent this actually represented a fall of 38 per cent in real terms over the level of expenditure on manpower and training programs in 1975-76. So much for the Government's great concern. In that time unemployment has increased by at least 50 per cent. There are grave problems in these training programs because they are not geared to job creation. The Government cannot countenance any government becoming involved in job creation. Under the Special Youth Employment Training Program only 12 per cent of the subsidised positions represented additional positions.
Whilst I applaud the dedication of people in my electorate of Ballarat who have conscientiously worked to try to make these programs succeed there are problems for rural and provincial areas that must be addressed by the Government if jobs are to be gained by those young people at the end of their training. I am appalled, as are my colleagues and the people of this country, by the lack of Government policy, to protect jobs in rural and provincial Australia; the Government should demand that adequate policies also be developed to ensure that jobs are created in those areas for all those who want them.
MrCHAPMAN (Kingston) (3.21)-The matter of public importance proposed by the honourable member for Ballarat (Mr Mildren) this afternoon states:
The Government's failure to protect existing employment and promote new employment opportunities in rural and provincial Australia.
This is nothing more than a fraud. As I have just indicated, this matter of public importance refers to rural and provincial Australia. We have heard very little about the broad expanse of rural and provincial Australia.
