Employment Opportunities in Australia

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 815

has served his electorate, and indeed this nation, very effectively for the last 10 years. As far as the protection of jobs even in that provincial area to which he referred is concerned the honourable member for Ballarat ought to be reminded that the Federal Government has recently established a contract worth $40m for the supply of howitzer guns to the Australian Army which will keep the Bendigo Ordnance Factory to which he referred in work until 1989. So much for his criticism of the Government with regard to the Bendigo Ordnance Factory.
The other factor which warrants some reference is the honourable member for Ballarat's repetition of the Australian Labor Party's unquestioning faith that increasing government intervention and government control is the panacea to all our problems. Over time that has been demonstrated to be a quite false faith. Let me return the debate to the particular matter of public importance that has been proposed, something which the honourable member for Ballarat failed to do. The first thing that the honourable member had to establish, and completely failed to do, was that unemployment in rural and provincial Australia was in a far worse situation than for Australia as a whole and, in particular, the capital cities. Of course the case is quite the contrary. The current national average for unemployment is 6.6 per cent. With the exception of rural New South Wales and rural Tasmania the other nonmetropolitan areas in Australia have unemployment rates significantly below that national average. That clearly gives the lie to the claim that unemployment is in a worse situation in provincial and rural Australia than it is in our capital cities.
I turn now to the particular problem of unemployment. We need to recognise that crucial to the prosperity of rural and provincial areas and, therefore, to employment prospects in those areas, is the health of the farming and mining industries. The honourable member for Ballarat completely ignored that aspect in his remarks here this afternoon.
Mr Bourchier-You would not expect the Labor Party to support primary industry.
Mr CHAPMAN-We would not expect the Labor Party to support primary industry. Its philosophy is totally opposed to it. The most significant aspect of this prosperity in comparison with other sectors of the economy, is that the mining and primary industries are export industries and therefore have to compete for the sale of their goods on world markets. Therefore, the mining and rural industries are directly affected

by our terms of trade. Quite simply, those industries cannot pass on the extra costs to which they are subjected by arbitrarily increasing prices. Indeed, in the current world economy, quite the reverse is the case. The prices of rural and mining commodities on world markets are falling quite significantly. Therefore the gross incomes of those industries in Australia are also falling significantly.
The other problem with regard to provincial and rural areas is the drought which is affecting wide areas of Australia, and the impact of that on the farming industry in particular is quite significant. Our farmers are a resilient lot and they have demonstrated over the years that they can cope with droughts and they can even cope with international marketing problems for fairly extended periods. But they can do that only if, during those periods, their cost structure does not seriously escalate. Any increase in the cost of inputs, whether those inputs be capital or labour, must be kept to a minimum if rural and mining industries are to survive in the current climate of international economic recession combined with the effects of drought.
The most effective contribution that the Government can make in that situation is to continue its fight against inflation. To the extent that it has been allowed to fight inflation which has been influenced by factors beyond its control, this Government has done so successfully over the last six or seven years. That has been achieved in particular by restraint on government spending and by control of the money supply. The extent to which inflation remains a problem is due to the rapid escalation of wage rates. Over a long period we have seen the level of wages increase well in excess of increases in productivity, and inflation is an inevitable consequence. More especially, over the last two years, average weekly earnings increased by about 17 per cent per year. Not only have we seen wages increase far in excess of productivity increases but also have wages increased far in excess of the rate of inflation. That has the consequence offulther fuelling inflation.
As well as this indirect detrimental impact on rural industry through its effect on inflation, the escalation of wages also has a direct impact on the cost structure of rural and mining industries. Market forces apply on world markets to the products of farming and mining industries. Unless market forces also apply to the wage rates paid in those industries, those market forces will affect the employment levels of those industries. That is to say, if the influence of international market forces is not reflected in the wages structure in the farming and mining industries, which have suffered the

816 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Employment Opportunities in Australia

effects of those forces on their returns, that influence will, quite simply, be reflected in the employment structure; in other words, unemployment
will result.
The Federal Government is not responsible for
the international economic recession and falling commodity prices on international markets. The Federal Government is not responsible for the drought which is gripping Australia and threatening farm outputs. Nor is the Federal Government responsible for the rapid escalation of wages which has flowed through the economy, including the farming and mining sectors. Indeed, the Government has argued strongly against it and sought wage restraint. It is therefore absolute nonsense to charge the Government with the failure to protect existing employment opportunities and promote new employment opportunities in rural and provincial Australia. Of the three factors I mentioned in relation to this problem, the wage structure is the only aspect which domestically we can do anything about.
Rural workers had a wage structure foisted upon them by their industrial representatives in the union movement, which cannot be sustained in the light of those two other factors, that is, the international market recession and the widespread drought. Quite simply, if the gross domestic product in general is falling and rural and mining income is falling even more significantly, employment will not be maintained if wages continue to increase. In contrast with past experience, some trade unions, I notice now, are recognising this factor. The Australian Workers Union has sought to share in the metal industry round of wage negotiations and benefits. It has supported this effort in the recent past by industrial action. However, the hours side of this claim has not been pressed over recent months; and that is to some extent a recognition of the declining rural situation and the declining capacity of rural industry to afford those extra benefits. I welcome that recognition on the part of the Australian Workers Union, particularly in relation to pastoral workers. I urge other industrial organisations to follow the lead that it has given. Furthermore, if the concern of the Labor Party about employment opportunities in rural and provincial Australia is genuine, surely it also ought to be arguing for market forces to apply more specifically to wage rates in rural areas so that employment opportunities can be maintained in those areas.
The other aspect of importance to rural and provincial areas and the farming and mining industries that produce the bulk of the wealth from those areas and, therefore provide employment opportunities, is the cost of capital inputs. More

particularly, that relates to machinery, buildings, fences and other items. Of course, if the costs of these capital inputs are lower those industries have a greater capacity to provide jobs. A major factor in the cost of these items to rural and mining industries is the excessive protection given to some areas of secondary industry, whether by tariff or quota protection. This protection costs consumers throughout Australia about $6,OOOm per year. Some of that cost is borne by the economically efficient manufacturing industries, but the great bulk of the cost of that protection is borne by the rural and mining industries. That $6,OOOm cost of protection is severly detrimental
to the capacity of those industries to provide employment opportunities in rural and provincial Australia.
An input of basic importance to these industries is steel. It is used directly in those industries and, of course, as a raw material for machinery and other equipment. The utter hypocrisy of the Labor Party lies in its real lack of concern about employment in rural and provincial centres, and especially in those sectors of industry that provide the jobs in those areas. That lack of concern is clearly demonstrated by its support for increased protection for the steel industry and its support
for the plea of the Broken Hill Propriety Co. Ltd for increased protection.
On 6 July, the Deputy Leader of the Opposition, the honourable member for Kingsford Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen), advocated greater protection for the Australian steel industry. He thereby advocated increased costs for the rural and mining industries and in turn, advocated a reduction in the employment capacity of those industries. The attitude of the industrial wing and the political wing of the Labor movement to two of the key issues affecting the prosperity of rural and mining industries in the current climate and hence to their capacity to provide jobs-namely, the wages structure and the protection of manufactured inputs-demonstrates the proposal of this matter of public importance for discussion to be a total sham; and, of course, it should be rejected as such.
In contrast, the Government has demonstrated its concern for employment in these areas. It has once again enhanced its job training programs and particularly emphasised the importance of those programs in rural and provincial areas. Indeed, if we look at the provision of various training programs in rural areas, we find, for example, under the school to work transition scheme that some 39.6 per cent of programs are provided in non-metropolitan areas, a provision far in excess of the population of those areas. The proportion

Employment Opportunities in Australia

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 817

of trainees-34.7 per cent-once again demostrates the Government's recognition of the importance of providing support in this way in rural areas. The Community Youth Support Scheme is another very important program that provides support to young unemployed people. Fortyseven per cent of all grants approved are for nonmetropolitan areas and some $7m of the funds provided for the Community Youth Support Scheme also go to non-metropolitan areas compared with some $9m for metropolitan areas. The Government's concern for employment prospects in those areas is clearly demonstrated by those programs.
Basically, the problem of unemployment gets back to our cost structure. Unless we develop a wages structure which is rational and which recognises the capacity of these industries to produce and obtain a return for what they produce on world markets, quite simply unemployment will increase and job opportunities will not be created. As I said at the outset, the Government clearly recognises that this is a crucial part of the problem. For six or seven years it has argued for wage restraint. It has given an even more positive lead in that regard in the last two or three months. Mr FREE (Macquarie) (3.36)-There could be no more important matter for discussion in this House today than that of the Government's failure to protect and provide jobs in rural and provincial Australia. The honourable member for Kingston (Mr Chapman) might not like to be reminded that it was the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) of the Government which he supports who said to the people of Australia that only under a Liberal Government will there be jobs for all those who want to work and that it was the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee) of the same Government who, on the 12th of this month, said to the people, in effect, 'forget it'. That Minister, in his Press release of that day, said:
The severity of the labour market difficulties facing Australian is evident from the unemployment figures for July 1982 which were released today by the Australian Bureau of Statistics. When account is taken of seasonal influences, there was a further rise in unemployment in July. This confirms other evidence that the overall demand for labour is at a low level.

As the shadow Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations-the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke)-has so tellingly pointed out on so many occasions, the true situation is far worse than this bland admission from this bland Minister. Employment growth has now collapsed completely. Consequently unemployment has risen sharply, at a frightening rate, with people of all ages and in all localities across the country being affected. The impact of the Government's failure is hurting most in provincial and rural Australia. As the honourable member for Ballarat (Mr Mildren) said during his speech, the precise local figures on registered unemployed are now unobtainable. The Government saw to that in April of last year when the razor gang recommended that the collection of figures by local Commonwealth Employment Service offices be dropped. It was a neat solution to a sticky political problem. When one has broken one's promises on jobs and the unemployment problem is getting out of hand, hide the figures. That is the one area where this deceitful Government can always be counted on. When the going sets tough it will run away and hide every time.
The local unemployment figures have proved no exception to the Government's consistent refusal to face up to the consequences of its disastrous policies. Nevertheless in various ways some figures will always surface. I have obtained regional figures for the city of Penrith, a centre which I represent and which is on the outskirts of Sydney. I must admit that the figures are third hand but that is the way these figures come out these days. If the version that I have been given is correct, it shows a frightening pattern. It shows an increase for Penrith of 36 per cent in registrations from June 1981 to June 1982-from 1,311 to 1,784.
Across the country in a mid-year period, at a time when unemployment ought to have fallen, it has climbed at an alarming rate. At a time when the graph should have dipped, it has taken off at a large and consistent angle of elevation. One can obtain some idea at least of the extent of unemployment in rural and provincial centres from the monthly totals of people receiving unemployment benefit from the district offices of the Department of Social Security. I shall present some statistics from an office serving part of my electorate and two others nearby in central western New South Wales:At the end of July 1982, 1,661 people were receiving unemployment benefit through the Lithgow office of the Department of Social Security. That figure represents an increase of 25 per cent over the May 1981 figure. In Bathurst 697 people were receiving unemployment benefit, an

818 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Employment Opportunities in Australia

increase of 20 per cent over May 1981. In Orange 3,912 people were receiving unemployment benefit, an increase of 19 per cent over the May figure for the previous year. I stress that these figures represent just the beneficiaries, not the total unemployed or the registrations. The totals do not include the numbers swelling the ranks of the hidden unemployed, those people who have given up the struggle of looking for non-existent jobs in these country centres.
When we talk merely in terms of figures we run the terrible risk of forgetting that we are talking about people, not just statistics. They are real people, people with commitments, people with hopes, often people with families who naturally have a desire to see their children get on in the world. They are young school leavers who are
eager to work but are faced with the prospect of having to get out of town because there is nothing there for them. They are people who are looking for jobs that do not exist because of the policies of this Government. They are people who are thrown out of work by the miserable policies of this miserable Government. We have all met them or their representatives at our electorate offices. What does one tell an unemployed person when he or she comes to one's electorate office looking for help? I wish the Minister were in the House to give me an answer because I have not been given one. He cannot give me one because he is not here, but I suspect I will not have an answer as long as this contemptible Government remains in office. What do I tell the next wave of school leavers when they come on to the job scene at the end of the year? What do I say to the local officers of the Department of Employment and Industrial Relations who are doing an excellent job in an impossible employment situation? How about some answers from this Government for a change?
I well remember the despair of men who were sacked when a cement works closed some years ago in the Kandos district of New South Wales. Those sackings meant a huge loss to the district. Over 80 wage packets went straight out of the local economy. But more than that were the problems that they faced and the problems facing many more in rural and provincial Australia because those problems are in many ways different from the problems facing people out of work in the cities. I am not saying that people unemployed in the cities do not have enormous problems, they certainly do. But at least they are part of a larger job market and have public transport systems available to them to help them explore that market. This is not so for someone who is out of work in the country. In many cases, especially in smaller centres, those people have to get out of

town. An unemployed person in such a situation may have to leave a town and a community which have provided the sum total of his social and personal contacts. He may have to leave a town where his children are happily settled in at a school. Hardest of all, he may have to try to sell a home which represents the major family investment and the fruit of years of work. It is especially hard in small centres, for who would possibly want to buy a home in a small town which has just lost a major local industry, perhaps its only local industry?
Let us look briefly at some case studies of local industries in the centres I have mentioned. Until early in July of this year, Robin's Shoe Factory in Bathurst employed over 100 people. In July 60 of those 100 people were dismissed, a result I believe of this Government's policy on footwear imports. On the growth side, just to demonstrate how rare and yet how feeble are the instances of job growth under this Government, honourable members will remember that the Email factory at Bankstown in Sydney closed recently with the loss of 300 jobs, the aim being to consolidate the industry at Orange. I have been told that this consolidation will result in a mere 30 additional jobs in Orange-l 0 per cent of the jobs lost and 0.76 per cent of those already receiving benefit in Orange.
I also want to mention employment at the Lithgow Small Arms Factory. This factory, like the Bendigo Ordnance Factory referred to by my colleague the honourable member for Ballarat, is a government undertaking. It was started by a Labor government. Over the years its work force has declined and this concerns me especially because people from my district travel to work at that factory. From 1970 to 1981 the factory's work force declined by over 35 per cent-from 1,525 to 988. In May of this year the staff was told that 60 jobs would go at the end of the month because orders expected from the Department of Defence had not arrived. Union representatives from that factory contacted me on 4 May. I asked them to form a delegation to come to Canberra. I was told that they had also contacted the honourable members for Calare (Mr MacKenzie). They were advised that he would not be able to meet them until June-after the date of the dismissals-and that he did not think it would be a good idea for them to come to Canberra. Neverthless, they did come. They met, of course, with members of the Australian Labor Party, including shadow Ministers. But they were surprised to find also that a meeting had been arranged by the honourable member for Calare with

Employment Opportunities in Australia

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 819

the Minister. As a result of that meeting, a statement was issued. It said that the orders would arrive after all, that there would be no retrenchments but that during the year 60 jobs would still go by attrition. Furthermore, the Minister indicated that he could give no guarantees beyond May 1982. Those are ominous words indeed. But at least those dismissals were averted through, I believe, pressure from the Labor Party and its excellent candidate in Calare, Alderman David Simmons. This example is an illustration of what a government can do if it is put under pressure. That kind of action from a government that really cares can do an enormous amount to assist people in the country. Through its own industries, through selective stimuli a government can do a great deal.

In Victoria it is the same in metropolitan and non-metropolitan areas. Generally speaking, throughout Australia other regional areas had unemployment rates below the national average of 6.6 per cent. That destroys the Labor Party's basic argument that the Government has somehow or other failed in rural and regional areas.
One can look at the proposals put up by the two Opposition speakers. The honourable member for Ballarat (Mr Mildren) seemed to be of the view that somehow or other we could just have a short term policy, that we could first turn on something like job creation and that would solve our problems.

Mr Bourchier-Tell us about the RED scheme.
Mr LLOYD-As the honourable member for Bendigo reminds us all, it was the previous Labor Government which ended the Regional Employment Development Scheme because it was not seen as being a success as it was too expensive. It completely ignored the point that Labor governments and politicians ignore all the time-that we have to have long term policies if we are to create development and employment in these areas. All their long term policies are anti-development and anti-employment in rural and regional areas in Australia.
Mr Milton-Absolute nonsense.
Mr LLOYD-If the honourable member would like to sit here and listen for a moment I will go into some detail on this matter. I will just speak on the subject of the honourable member for Ballarat for a moment. He was here today criticising this Federal Government. What about the Government in his own State of Victoria? At the present time the McCain Aust Pty Ltd factory in Ballarat is threatening to move to Tasmania because of lack of assistance from a Victorian Labor Government. This same Victorian Labor Government is threatening to wipe out payroll tax incentives for decentralised industries in Victoria, completely altering the whole of the policies of the charges of the State Electricity Commission of Victoria which were built up over many years. It will now penalise people who use electricity in country and regional parts of Victoria. So much for the rhetoric, so much for the action that is taking place under a Labor government in Victoria at the present time.
Let us have a look at what the honourable member for Macquarie (Mr Free) had to say. The honourable member for Macquarie based his argument on the failure of this Government to face up to the disastrous consequences of its economic policy. What a cheek for anybody on the Labor side to mention economic policy in relation to employment levels and international competitiveness in this country. Let us have a look at those Labor policies. They are inflationary, big government expenditure, very high protection policies. They, are anti-rural, anti-development and antiinternational trade. They are all of the things which are opposite to what is needed for rural and regional employment in this country.
Let us get back to something sensible in this debate, that is, if we are to have employment in rural and regional areas we have to have long term policies based on that which will create employment in those areas. First of all we must deal with the

820 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Employment Opportunities in Australia

agricultural industries that are there. Flowing through from that we need a system for the processing of those agricultural products. Next to that we need to provide for the mining industries that are in rural and regional areas. Beyond that we need to provide special forms of assistance to encourage those industries to develop and to encourage people to go to them. They have to be long term. Confidence has to be built up. Industries have to be built on the basis of internationally competitive trade. There has to be some encouragement for investors, both within Australia and outside, to come and invest. Let us look at the Labor Party in relation to agricultural policy. How much support has it given for any of the stabilisation or underwriting schemes that are necessary for long term projections, long term investment in rural industry?
Mr Mildren~ The floor price for wool.
Mr LLOYD~ We introduced the floor price. I very clearly remember being in this Parliament in 1973 and 1974 when the Labor Cabinet was trying to reduce the floor price for wool, a price that had been introduced by us several years earlier. What support has the Labor Party given for the new depreciation allowance or for the investment allowance? The Labor Party took away the superphosphate and nitrogenous fertiliser bounties. It messed this around again in the Senate the other day. It removed the fuel freight subsidy equalisation scheme. It reduced the rural telephone concessions. It halved in one year the money going toward rural roads in this country. What a mockery to have Labor Party members here today talking about this Government not assisting rural and regional employment. When the Labor Party was in government~that is the test~~
Mr Bourchier~ They have the same policies now.
Mr LLOYD~ Those policies have not changed. People should always remember on the one hand what the Labor Party says in its rhetoric and on the other hand what it does. The honourable member for Bendigo has made the point that we should first look at the State Labor Government in Victoria at the present time. If one looks at the special assistance that is being made in this Budget to assist in these areas one can indicate and show the special concern of this Government. What about mining? Golly, oil exploration almost ceased in this country during the period of the Labor Government. There was so much uncertainty and confusion about the Connor proposals for investment in this country. Since 1976 the employment potential of the mining industry

in this country~nearly all of it in rural and regional areas~has been phenomenal. We have worked very hard also on the necessary international trade policies. I always remember the Labor Government and 1974 when the Japanese Government shut the door on any beef exports from Australia. There was not one whimper from the Labor Government at that time to maintain our trade with that country. Fortunately, with ourselves back in government and good relations with the Government of Japan we now have quite a trade going again.
We can look at the other things that we are
doing as a government. We have established a special processing committee with the Japanese Government to encourage more processing of primary products in this country. The honourable member for Calare (Mr MacKenzie) whom Opposition members mentioned a moment ago, has provided considerable assistance in the development of wool scouring in regional areas of this country providing more employment in rural and regional areas. We can come to the matter of food processing. There is a natural movement of food processing out of capital cities to regional cities
because of the environmental protection problems in our major cities. We have had tremendous movement~certainly in my electorate~towards
food processing in regional towns. I say to the Labor Opposition: The people who work in those food processing industries are their own worst enemies with regard to their future employment because of the way they are pricing our processed foods out of the international market and are forcing people to stay in business to sell their commodities raw overseas rather than processed.
I spent some time during the recess looking at the prawn fishing industry in northern Australia. There has been a tremendous movement in the export of what is known as bulk packed prawns to other countries, particularly Japan, as distinct from fully processed prawns with the resultant loss of hundreds and hundreds of people in the prawn processing industry because the cost of labour in this country is too high. The cost of a woman working in a fruit cannery in the Goulburn Valley is twice what it is in California. Yet somehow or other we are expected to compete. Thousands of jobs have been lost in the abattoir industry of Australia. Yet the Australasian Meat Industry Employees Union is on strike in Victoria for $65 a week extra~at a time when that industry has already lost thousands of employees whose own actions have significantly encouraged that to happen. Yet members of that union are doing this. It is so suicidal.
Mr Bourchier~Backed up by the ALP.

Petitions

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 821

Mr LLOYD-They are their own worst enemies. As the honourable member for Bendigo has said, they are given every encouragement by the Labor Party. The Labor Party will destroy employment opportunities in rural and regional Australia.
Finally, without going through all the other factors, including tourism, which this Government has boosted and which is now quite significant in country areas, I say that industry in rural and regional Australia is basically export or import replacement oriented. To survive it must be internationally competitive. The policies of the Labor Government are the opposite to international competitiveness, as they were shown to be when in practice during the term of the Labor Government in the mid-l 970s.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired. The discussion is concluded.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Taxation
To the Right Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that whereas the current rates of personal income tax are onerous and destructive of personal incentive, particularly since the cessation of income tax indexation.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Australian Government formerly call upon the Parliament assembled to legislate such changes to the current taxation law as will-
raise the minimum level at which income tax is first payable to at least $4,500.00,
proportionately increase the figures at which the higher rates of tax attract,
allow taxpayers to claim the full amount for each item within the Concessional Expenditure Rebate, and
adjust the rates of tax payable so that no higher rate of tax is payable than fifty percent of income earned.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Bradfield, Mr Cadman, Mr Donald Cameron and Mr Sinclair.
Petitions received.
Excise on Wine
To the Right Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
that any sales tax or excise on Australian wine would adversely affect employment in the wine industry, place a disproportionate burden on grapegrowers and ensure a larger future surplus of grapes;
the reduction in the demand for wine will also threaten the existence of many wine producers and reduce the range, quality and variety of wines offered in the market.

your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Australian Government will reject any proposal for a sales tax or excise on Australian wine;
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Baume, Mr Chapman and Mr Les Johnson.
Petitions received.
Interest Rates
To the Right Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of we the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
The heart-rending loss of homes by home purchasers, due to an expanding holocaust of interest rates caused by, and to quote the manager of a leading bank (26.04.81) 'with the growing competition for savings, depositors are looking for higher interest returns on deposits. In order to retain our depositors and to meet the increased costs payable on deposits and operating expenses, it is necessary to raise income from loans of all types, including House Loans'.
Therefore it becomes transparently clear that the constant edging up of interest rates for investors by banks, lending institutions, and banking consortiums are collectively responsible for the monetary explosion, lack of employment and all that that entails and is causing grave social disruption to hundreds of thousands. Behind it all, the absence of control that is now politically essential in the interest of justice and fair distribution of burden.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that your House urge all politicians to unite nationally to do all within their power to reduce interest rates across the board.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Dr Blewett.

Petitions received.
Proposed Student Loans Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that the introduction of a comprehensive student loans scheme will:
I. Restrict Access to Education-- People from poorer backgrounds will be deterred from studying by the prospect of incurring large debts. These people, if they enrol, will be forced to amass loan debts. Richer students do not face this handicap.

2. Undermind the Tertiary Education Assistance SchemeTEAS should cover the basic living costs of students. Currently the scheme only assists 38% of full-time students and the maximum level of TEAS is a mere $49.67. Government statements indicate TEAS will be further downgraded if loans are introduced
3. Disadvantage Women-Women students would be disadvantaged by a loans scheme as they have a lesser ability to repay loan debts, because on average women earn less and have a higher unemployment rate.
4. Disadvantage Mature-Age Students-Usually mature age students forego income to study and already have large financial commitments. They could not return to study if it meant incurring substantial debts.
5. Distort Educational Choices-Mounting loan debts would force students into studying shorter courses or not continuing with post-graduate study.
6. Result in Massive Attrition Rates-'Students will attempt to undertake extensive part-time work or overload courses to minimise their loans. Increased failure rates will result forcing students to "drop-out" rather than continuing study and increase their debts.
7. Cause Administrative & Financial DifficultiesOverseas experience shows high default rates will occur on student loans. Government money will be channelled away from TEAS to paying off banks. The government will also bear the administrative burden of pursuing defaulters.
Therefore, the proposed student loans scheme will have disasterous effects on tertiary education, which we believe benefits the whole community, not just the individual student.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that:
The House of Representatives will reject the introduction of a student loans scheme. And your petitioners as in duty bound shall every humbly pray.
by Mr Wilson and Mr Young.

Home Deposit Assistance Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
(I) The restrictive income test of the Home Deposit Assistance Scheme penalises applicants who are forced by rising housing prices and high interest rates to become two-income families;
(2) the limit on the value of the house be reintroduced to ensure that the wealthy would not be eligible to apply for the grant;
(3) the interest rate tax rebate is too low and takes too long to recompense to be of any real value to low-income earners, who cannot afford the repayments of a high interest loan; and
(4) more people should be made eligible for the loan so that the building industry may be assisted out of its present slump.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House request the Government to take positive steps to stimulate the waning building industry and genuinely assist home buyers by raising, or abolishing, the income test, re-introducing a limit on the value of the house and increasing the tax rebate of the Home Deposit Assistance Scheme.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Telephone Services for Pensioners
To the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the indiscriminate denial of the telephone rental concession to pensioners who share accommodation with others whose weekly income is greater than $104.15 is causing many needy pensioners unnecessary hardship.
The vast majority of pensioners who share accommodation do so out of necessity, i.e. to help in defraying household expenses or to provide a home for their children. They do not derive significant financial gain through sharing accommodation.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will devise a Household Income Test which will reflect the true financial position of such pensioners and will direct Telecom Australia to extend the telephone rental concession to all pensioners not deriving significant financial gains through sharing accommodation with others.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.

Moreton Island: Sand Mining
To the Hounourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
( I) That Moreton Island has been listed on the Register of the National Estate because of its exceptional scientific, ecological, heritage and recreational values; and
(2) That any sand mining of Moreton Island will result in the destruction of the exceptional scientific, ecological, heritage and recreational values of the Island; and
(3) That Moreton Island has been the subject of detailed investigation resulting in the adoption of a Management Plan by the Brisbane City Council the main aims of which are to conserve in its natural state the quality of its scenery and natural environment and to enhance public enjoyment of it for recreation purposes; and
(4) That Moreton Island represents the last significant area of wilderness close to Brisbane; and
(5) That there is an acute deficiency of National Parks within a 150 kilometre radius of Brisbane; and
(6) That Moreton Island contains Aboriginal Relics of immense scientific and cultural importance; and
(7) That the Island represents a major potential tourist resource specifically because of its unspoiled natural scenic attractions; and
(8) That public opinion actively demostrated over a number of years clearly indicates that most citizens of Brisbane are strongly opposed to any sand mining on the Island, and desire that the whole of Moreton Island with the exception only of existing Townships, Tourist Resort, Airstrips and Lighthouse Reserve should be declared a National Park.
Your Petitioners, therefore, Humbly Pray that the Parliament of Australia will: (a) accept the wishes of the Petitioners and the citizens of Brisbane generally and take whatever action is necessary and available to it to prevent sand mining of any description taking place on Moreton Island; and (b) request the Government of the State of Queensland to take immediate action to declare the whole of Moreton Island to be a National Park with the exception only of existing Townships, Tourist Resort, Airstrips and Lighthouse Reserve.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.

Slaughter of Marine Mammals
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
I. Since the Australian Whale Protection Act 1980 provides for the banning of importation into Australia of all whale by-products, and

2. the Prime Minister has stated that he and his Government support the international banning of whaling,
3. then consistent with (I) and (2), this Government should immediately legislate to ban the importation of all marine mammal by-products, including the harp seal of Canada, and
4. through the Australian delegate in the United Nations General Assembly, the Prime Minister should state his Government's support for an international ban on the slaughter of any marine mammal for commercial reasons.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that consistent with legislation on whaling, this Government should ban the importation of marine mammal by-products and support an international ban on slaughter of any marine mammal.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. That continued use of animal ingredients in cosmetic products, and the inhumane use of animals in scientific rsearch for cosmetic products is abhorrent and barbaric.

Service Pensions
To the Honourable the Speaker and members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned electors of the Division of McArthur respectfully showeth-the old age pension is granted, according to the Constitution of the Land, Federal Parliament, and the people, conditionally to all citizens, who were Non-serving persons in the Forces, on reaching 65 years of age.
We of the Defence Forces, past and present, are entitled to the above mentioned Pension five years earlier, i.e. at 60 years of age.
An Unconditional Pension is granted to people who did not risk their life and limbs at any war.
We wish to ask the Minister for Veteran's Affairs, WHY, we ex Defence Forces are not entitled to a NO MEANS TEST at 65 years of age?
This means that a non serving Citizen, on retirement at 65 years of age, only has to wait 5 years to be free of the Means Test, as against the veterans waiting 10 years for the same privilege.
Those people who are interested, regarding this matter, and wish to draw this subject to our present Federal Government's attention, please sign below.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that you, our Honourable Member, will bring this matter to the Minister for Veterans Affairs attention and your Petitioners, as in duty bound, will ever pray.
by MrBaume.

Great Northern Highway, Kimberley Region
To the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled.We the undersigned residents of the Kimberley humbly petition the House of Representatives to urgently allocate funds for the immediate upgrading of the Great Northern Highway in the Kimberley to an all weather sealed highway and pray that the following matters might be considered in relation thereto:
(a) The grave hardship caused to the many residents of the Kimberley due to the Great Northern Highway annually being impassable by road transport resulting in the towns of Kununurra, Wyndham, Halls Creek and Fitzroy Crossing and

the community of Turkey Creek being without adequate food supplies for periods of sometimes in excess of one month.
(b) That this portion of the Great Northern Highway is the last portion which is unsealed and which does not provide an all weather link and we are, therefore, the only residents of Australia who are on a major highway system which is regularly inundated.
(c) Current allocations and plans do not provide for the upgradiQg of this highway to be completed in under six years.
(d) There is great defence and strategic importance in having an all weather highway system into the North of Western Australia.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Campbell.
Petition received.
Pensions of Italian Immigrants
To the Speaker and Honourable Members of the House of Representatives of the Australian Parliament assembled.
The petition of the undersigned residents of Australia respectfully showeth: That the Australian Government take urgent. steps to formulate and approve a Social Security Agreement with the Italian Government based on the rights and the needs of Italian immigrant workers in Australia.
Moreover, with the present petition we request that the following important question related to the pensions of Italian immigrants be urgently resolved within such an agreement; in particular we request:
I. That Italian Immigrants who have gained the right to Australian pensions and are no longer resident in Australia be given the opportunity to apply for and obtain the pension without having to return to Australia.
2. That periods of residence in Australia of less than ten years presently required by the Australian legislation be considered for some entitlement to the Australian pension.
3. That the issue concerning the unfair reduction applied to the pension of Australian residents who are receiving an Italian pension be resolved.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.
Petition received.
TARIFF PROPOSALS
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce) (4.3)-1 move:
Excise Tariff Proposals No.3 (1982) and Customs Tariff (Coal Export Duty) Proposals (1982).
Excise Tariff Proposals No. 3 (1982) and Customs Tariff (Coal Export Duty) Proposals (1982) which I have just tabled formally place before Parliament alterations to the Excise Tariff Act 1921 and the Customs Tariff (Coal Export Duty) Act 1975 respectively. These alterations were introduced during the winter recess by Commonwealth of Australia Gazette excise notice No. 1 (1982) under the authority of section 160B of the Excise Act and Gazette notice No.1 (1982)

Customs and Excise Amendment Bill

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 827

under the authority of section 273EA of the Customs Act. Excise Tariff Proposals No. 3 (1982) increases the excise duty on stabilised crude petroleum oil from $186.26 to $205.97 per kilolitre operating on and from 1 July 1982. This alteration followed determination of new import parity prices by the Minister for National Development and Energy (Senator Sir John Carrick). The determination accords with Government policy on import parity pricing for Australian produced crude oil.
In addition, Excise Tariff Proposals No. 3 (1982) increases the excise duty on naturally occurring liquefied petroleum gas from $20.46 to $25.93 per kilolitre operating on and from I July 1982. This change followed a new average wholesale price for naturally occurring LPG being set by the Minister for National Development and Energy in accordance with Government policy. Customs Tariff (Coal Export Duty) Proposals (1982), operating on and from 29 July 1982, exempts from duty all black coal previously dutiable at $1.00 per tonne. This alteration follows the Government's decision announced by the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) on 29 July 1982 to assist Australian coal exporters. I commend the proposals to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr West) adjourned.

manner in which the Government has responded to suggestions for change which have come from within the Department itself, from the ranks of the Customs officers and, indeed, from private enterprise.

Review of decision
"75. Section 162c of the Principal Act is amended-
(a) by ommitting from paragraph (I) (d) "and" (last occurring); and
(b) by adding at the end of sub-section (I) the following word and paragraph:
'; and (f) a decision of a Collector for the purposes of section 61 D.'. ".
The main purpose of the amendments is to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901 by inserting into each of those Acts a scheme of provisions to govern the sale and delivery by 'off airport' duty-free shops of duty-free goods to prospective air travellers. Amendment 5 proposes insertion of the necessary provisions into the Customs Act as a new section 96A. Amendment 15 proposes the insertion of corresponding provisions into the Excise Act as a new section 61 D. The provisions proposed to be inserted will provide authority for imposing on shop proprietors and purchasers conditions directed at ensuring that goods that are sold and delivered to travellers duty-free are, in fact, exported in accordance with the requirements applying.
The conditions that are intended to be imposed will, in effect, restate on a valid legal basis the conditions at present being applied. The purpose of these conditions is to place on the shop proprietors obligations to make travellers aware of the conditions with which they are required to

comply, keep records and furnish returns, produce satisfactory proof of export of goods and accept liability for payment of duty on goods in respect of which satisfactory proof of export is not produced. The provision of a legal basis for imposing conditions on purchasers will mean that such persons who fail to export goods purchased duty free will, in appropriate circumstances, become liable to prosecution.
One of the practices which I am sure would be condemned on both sides of the House but which, unfortunately, raises its head from time to time is when a person obtains an airline ticket for over~ seas travel, takes that airline ticket to a number of duty-free shops established outside airports, makes a number of duty-free purchases, cashes in the airline ticket and, in fact, never exports the goods. In that situation the revenue has been defrauded and the person has gained access to goods at duty free prices. It is the view of the Department, and one with which I entirely concur, that persons who behave in that way shall not only become liable to prosecution but also will in fact be prosecuted with the full force of the law. Therefore, the assistance of the duty-free shop operators in providing information which will enable the apprehension of these offenders is very much sought by the Government.
As the duty-free shops to which the proposed amendments relate have the status of being warehouses licensed for the purposes of the Customs Act, other provisions of a more general nature included in Part V of the Customs Act also have application in relation to the operation and control of such premises. Since the inception of duty-free shops, the general authority contained in section 40AA of the Customs Act and section 61A of the Excise Act to give permission for the movement of goods under continuing Customs control, subject to compliance with specified conditions, has been relied upon to render shop proprietors liable for duty on goods in respect of which satisfactory proof of export is not produced. Legal advice has recently been received, however, that, primarily because the duty-free procedures involve the permission holder relinquishing possession of goods for carriage to the aircraft by the traveller, who is not in the capacity of being an agent or servant of the shop proprietor, those general proyisions do not provide a satisfactory legal basis on which action can be taken to deal with breaches of the requirements or to recover duty in respect of goods not exported. The proposed amendments will not affect the present day to day operations of the duty-free industry as no change in the procedures at present being followed is envisaged.

832 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Customs and Excise Amendment Bill

My colleague the honourable member for Parramatta will no doubt refer to the fact that the Australian duty-free industry is not only growing at a very substantial pace but also forms an integral part of our tourism industry. With purchases in excess of $40m and total investment, including employment, in the industry of around $200m we are dealing with a very big industry. Permissions that have been given to proprietors under section 40AA of the Customs Act and section 61A of the Excise Act will, however, be required to be replaced by permissions given under the proposed new provisions when the necessary regulations are made and promulgated.
I should particularly mention, in view of deep concerns expressed by representatives of the dutyfree industry, that the placing of the proposed new provisions within Part V of the Customs Act will have the effect of rendering all administrative decisions made in pursuance of those provisions reviewable by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. That is the result of discussions between the industry and the Department culminating just two weeks ago in a conference in Canberra at which the industry was well represented by a former honourable senator, Mr Puplick, who presented the basic case on its behalf. The Department, the Minister and the Government have responded and the matters raised in respect of bringing appeals under Part V under the review of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal are now being implemented-a very speedy example of government responding to a proposal for reform which had merit and which is now about to be-
come law.
I turn now to the other proposed amendments that a close examination of the Bill during the period since its introduction has shown to be desirable. These are in the main of a relatively for-
mal nature requiring little explanation. I mention specifically, however, amendments 2, 3, 4, 8, 9 and 10 which are in this category but which are, of necessity, quite lengthy. They relate to certain situations in which the Comptroller-General, before arriving at a belief that a company is not a fit or proper company or a person is not a fit or proper person to hold a warehouse or depot licence, is required to have regard to certain prescribed matters. Included in these matters are any convictions of the company, or key personnel, for certain specified offences committed within 10 years immediately preceding the making of the application for the licence concerned.
Representations have been received from industry interests that, in circumstances relating to a licence that has been renewed on one or more occasions since the initial application for the

licence, regard should be had to only such convictions as may have been imposed during the IO-year period immediately preceding the application for the latest renewal of the licence. The Government sees these representations as being eminently reasonable and proposes that the necessary amendments to clauses 19 and 24 of the Bill should be made. In further response to concerns expressed by industry interests, amendment 12 modifies the right of access and entry to depots, proposed by clause 24 of the Bill, to be given to collectors, by authorising such access and entry only by a collector who is in official uniform or who, on request, produces to the depot proprietor written evidence of his official status.
I commend these amendments to the Committee. I believe them to be reasonable. In the main, they are responses to suggestions emanating from within the Department and from the Customs officers themselves, on the one side, and from private enterprise on the other. I feel some pride in now seeing a co-operation between industry and government on this very important issue. The continuing comprehensive review of the legislation will be maintained. The Government does not claim to have a monopoly on wisdom and I can assure the Committee that any suggestions from the Opposition, from my colleague the honourable member for Parramatta, from the industry and from honourable members will certainly be taken into account by the Government and will be responded to. I commend the amendments to the Committee.

Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (4.31)-1 wish to take up the point raised by the honourable member for Parramatta (Mr John Brown) about the lack of review procedures in this legislation in relation to appeals to the Administrative Appeals Tribunals against Customs officers' decisions. I had some discussions this morning with the Australian Federation of Air Freight Forwarders, the Australian Chamber of Shipping and the Distilled Spirits Industry Council of Australia. Representatives of those three industry groups explained to me that they had had some discussions with the Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Mr Hodgman), who is in the chamber today. Their main part of disagreement with this proposed legislation was that administrative decisions made pursuant to the proposed new section 65A would not be reviewable by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. It was their

Customs and Excise Amendment Bill

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 835

view that that was quite necessary. Considering that this legislation extends certain rights of appeal to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal, I was concerned to see why other areas had been excluded from this appeal procedure.
I shall go on to explain a little more about this matter as it has been referred to me. The concern of the Australian Federation of Air Freight Forwarders is that proposed new section 65A confers substantial discretionary powers on Customs officers but no ready recourse is provided for a review of the exercise of such powers. Member companies of the Federation deal with consignments of air freight that originates in many overseas countries and airports. The capacities of members of the air freight industry to comply with decisions made by Customs officers in relation to Customs requirements under such a section would inevitably vary enormously because the standard and timeliness of data available to industry members vary enormously depending on the exporting freight forwarder, the airline company, the flight itinerary and the standards of documentation at overseas airports of loading. Is it a reasonable requirement, then, for one flight or one category of flight necessarily to prevail across the whole spectrum of aircraft movements?
Basically these organisations consider that it would be unsatisfactory if decisions as to requirements in these matters could be made without their being subject to the discipline of review by a readily accessible independent umpire provided by the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. In other words, these organisations are saying that they want some channel of appeal against decisions taken by Customs officers. They are not debating the Customs officers' right to seek information, whether oral or documentary. I understand that the point they have put to the Minister and to the Government in relation to this matter is that they would like some channel of appeal. All I am asking in the short time available is for the Minister in his reply to explain why he has refused to allow these organisations that appeals process.
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce) (4.35)-In reply to the honourable member for Parramatta (Mr John Brown) and the honourable member for Robertson (Mr Cohen) in relation to what is known as inwards duty-free shopping, I indicate that that matter is currently under the consideration of the Government. It does not just involve the Department of Industry and Commerce, it also involves the Department of Aviation. There are a number of arguments for and against the proposal that visitors or Australians returning to

Australia should be able to purchase duty free at the airport before passing through Customs. I am well aware of the proposition by Qantas Airways Ltd which is to reduce the amount of goods which are brought in on flights. One of the problems would be: At what price would the purchaser pay? Would he pay at the Australian price, or would he get the bottle of whisky at the price that was applicable in the country from which he had just come, whether it be Singapore, Hong Kong or whatever? I invite both honourable members if they would like to pursue the matter to let us have their submissions as the matter is currently under consideration.
I note the matters which have been raised by the honourable member for Robertson on the question of watches and cameras. I will refer it to
the Minister concerned and get back to the honourable member if there is any additional information. It has however been drawn to my attention that Treasury has a very significant interest in this aspect because of the sales tax. On the matter which was raised by the honourable member for Parramatta, I do not know the groups to whom he was referring when he talked of representations. I assure him that at the meeting that took place a fortnight ago, there were persons there who were not part of the main group-if I can use that expression-whose case was presented by Mr Puplick. Mr Puplick was successful on the matters where he was able to persuade, but there were matters on which he was not successful. If after the debate is concluded, the honourable member would like to draw my specific attention to the names of the persons to whom he has referred I will ascertain from within the Department where the submissions are and what the response is.
I would like to respond to the honourable member for Canberra (Mrs Kelly) in some little detail on section 65A of the Customs Act and the Excise Act. I think there is a genuine misunderstanding on the part of those who have approached the honourable member as to what exactly the implications of section 65A are and whether in fact any rights are affected in such a way that it would be appropriate to bring them under the review of the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. The right to require adequate information in relation to a ship, its cargo, voyage, crew, passengers and so on, is not contested on either side of the House. That being the case, there should be no objection to the exercise of that right on the basis that the collector has a discretion as to when-I emphasise this-the information should be given. In other words, if it is not convenient for the information to be given there and then, the collector can say: 'I will postpone

836 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982 Activities ofShip Painters and Dockers' Union

the time when the information should be given'. The requirement for a person to answer questions and produce documents is explicitly limited to the ability of that person to do so at any given time. From the point of view of the Government we say that no onerous, unreasonable or additional burden can be seen as being placed upon persons obliged to tender the information. We would have to argue that no rights could therefore be adversely affected to warrant the attention of the AAT. The proposed provision is seen as working to the advantage of ship operators, a screening of manifests, et cetera, before arrival of a vessel will obviate any need to hold back discharge and dispersal of cargo until after arrival screening is carried out.
In conclusion, the only other point I wish to make is that whilst from time to time one hears criticisms of Customs officers, I think honourable members would be interested to know that the volume of complaints against Customs officers as part of the departmental correspondence is very, very minute. If there is a situation in which an officer does misbehave-I would have to say that is more likely to be the exception than the rulethere are alternatives available to the party aggrieved, other than the Administrative Appeals Tribunal. I mention simply the right to action at common law and the often forgotten but nevertheless fundamental basis of the law in this country, that is, the prerogative writ. I commend the amendments to the House.
