ROYAL COMMISSION ON THE ACTIVITIES OF THE FEDERATED SHIP
PAINTERS AND DOCKERS UNION
Report and Ministerial Statement
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond ValleyMinister for Communications and Minister Assisting the Attorney-General)-For the information of honourable members. I table volume 1 of the fourth interim report of the Royal Commission on the activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union.
Motion (by Mr N. A. Brown)-by leaveagreed to:
That this House, in accordance with the provisions of the Parliamentary Papers Act 1908, authorises the publication of

volume I of the fourth interim report of the Royal Commission on the activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union.
Mr N. A. BROWN-by leave-The volume I have tabled is part of a series of interim reports the Royal Commission has provided since it was established by the Commonwealth and Victorian Governments in September 1980. The first two reports dealt with unfolding investigations and, on the advice of the Royal Commissioner, Mr F. X. Costigan, Q.c., they were not tabled. The third interim report, which dealt largely with fraudulent taxation schemes, was tabled earlier this year. The Government recently received the Commission's fourth interim report, in three volumes. Mr Costigan recommended against tabling the second and third volumes, but saw no objection in regard to volume 1 and envisaged that it would be made public. The Government has given very close consideration to the question of tabling of volume 1. That volume includes findings and observations that make damaging allegations in relation to a number of individuals. Findings and conclusions of a royal commission are matters that need to be taken seriously, though they are not, of course, findings of guilt whether they relate to criminal offences or to matters of discipline. For criminal charges, the guilt or innocence of a person is determined by the Courts; for disciplinary charges, Acts of Parliament provide for decision by appropriate tribunals. Notwithstanding these considerations, the Government has concluded that there is an over riding public interest in the matters canvassed that calls for tabling of all but two paragraphs of that volume. Two paragraphs have been excluded having regard to their contents and to the fact the Commissioner is to make further inquiries into the matter discussed in them.
Matters Relating to the Union, its Members and Others
The report gives substance to the grave concerns that led the Government to set up the Royal Commission. Volume 1 deals with the Commission's knowledge and findings on some aspects of the activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union, its members and persons associated with them. It describes a history of violence, and the ruthless means by which members of the Union have arrogated to themselves a position outside the law. It tells of the Union's ability to provide individuals with a new name and a new identity, and some of the uses that are made of these. The Royal Commission has developed a practice of referring to appropriate law enforcement agencies particular criminal activities as they are identified. Many of the activities described in the report are of direct

Activities a/Ship Painters and Dockers' Union 24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 837

concern to Victoria and its police. The Victorian Government has a copy of the report except for paragraphs 1.13 and 1.14 and chapter 3 which appear only in the report to the Governor General.
The Royal Commission has pointed in another volume to the need for a special group to prepare and conduct the prosecutions necessary to suppress the criminal activity uncovered by its continuing investigations. The Government stands ready to set up such a group. The Attorney General, Senator Durack, will be considering this matter urgently, in consultation with Mr Costigan and the Victorian Government initially.
Action to Progress Taxation Prosecutions
Attention is also being given as a matter of priority to the prosecution of offences associated with bottom-of-the-harbour tax evasion schemes which are referred to in volume I.
In June this year the Treasurer (Mr Howard) and the Attorney-General took steps to obtain advice from the Solicitor-General in regard to the availability of prosecution action in the particular case dealt with in chapter 3 of volume I. The Solicitor-General, in an opinion delivered later in the same month, advised that certain remedies were open.
Advice was also sought from senior counsel in June on possible criminal proceedings in relation to a major promoter in Sydney. A final opinion is expected shortly.
On the particular case referred to in chapter 3 of the report, the Crown Solicitor sent an officer from his central office to Perth to work in association with the Australian Taxation Office to complete a brief to advise on evidence and to draw charges as appropriate. That brief has been delivered to senior counsel.
More generally it has been decided to appoint a special prosecutor to concentrate special effort on action with a view to prosecution of persons who were directly involved in the promotion and execution of bottom-of-the-harbour schemes. The special prosecutor will be assisted by a task force of lawyers, taxation officers and police. There will be liaison with the Royal Commission. Additional staff and resources will be provided as needed. We will be seeking the fullest co-operation from State authorities. The Victorian Government has already proposed a joint effort and the Attorney General will be discussing this matter with them.
Decisions on prosecutions or other proceedings will, of course, be made on the evidence available and on the law applicable. But the Attorney General is firmly of the view that those who were

most directly involved in the promotion and execution of blatantly fraudulent tax evasion schemes should be dealt with when their conduct can be shown to have been in breach of the criminallaw. Where appropriate, civil action for recovery of moneys will be pursued as well.
National Crimes Commission and the Future of the Royal Commission
The Royal Commission's fourth interim report underlines the need which the Government has seen for some time for the establishment of a national crimes commission. Mr Costigan himself has recommended action along these lines.
The Commonwealth envisages a national crimes commission as a standing body, established by legislation, with special powers to investigate and initiate action against serious criminal activity. Such a body would supplement existing law enforcement efforts in areas of need. In particular, a crimes commission could be directed to: Organised crime; white collar crime; and corruption of officials and official bodies.
The primary objective of such a commission would be to bring about prosecutions in appropriate cases. The commission would be involved in such prosecutions rather than recommending prosecutions and leaving action on them to others. It would not produce reports naming individuals as suspected of committing criminal offences.
The Commonwealth is seeking the cooperation of the States. The matter was discussed at the Police Ministers Conference last week and will be discussed further at the Standing Committee of Attorneys-General later this week. The Royal Commission's report is relevant to the deliberations of those meetings.
Mr Costigan refers in the volume that has been tabled to other areas, mentioned in the confidential volumes, that are of major significance and concern to government and community. He says his Commission will be unable to complete its investigations by its present report date of 31 December 1982 and states that continued investigation must take place whether by his Commission or some other body.
The Government would see the proposed national crimes commission as an appropriate body in due course to carryon the work of the Royal Commission. In the meantime, the Government will consult with the Victorian Government on the question of extending Mr Costigan's Commission.

838 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982 Activities ofShip Painters and Dockers' Union

Findings Affecting the Crown Solicitor's Office
Chapter 3 of volume I focuses on an episode of particular concern to the Government. The episode relates to the performance of the Crown Solicitor's Division of the Attorney-General's Department and in particular of the office of the Deputy Crown Solicitor in Perth.
The report recounts the handling by the Crown Solicitor's Office of a case of an alleged fraudulent tax evasion scheme referred to it in 1974 by the Australian Taxation Office in Perth following an investigation by that office. The scheme, of the kind commonly referred to as a bottom-of-theharbour scheme, involved the stripping of company assets so as to cheat the revenue of taxes. The report states that, after counsel's opinion was obtained, the Deputy Crown Solicitor's Office was requested in August 1975 to commence a prosecution.
Mr Costigan reports a history of events and circumstances in the handling of the matter by the Crown Solicitor's office, and of dealings between that office and the Taxation Office. The history reveals inaction and negligence on the part of the Deputy Crown Solicitor's Office in Perth. The problems described are said to go further than the Deputy Crown Solicitor's Office in Perth, and to bear on the control and supervision of the Perth office by the Crown Solicitor's central office in Canberra. The matter concluded with advice from the Crown Solicitor's central office that the evidence was insufficient to establish a criminal conspiracy to defraud the Commonwealth.
The Attorney-General should not be taken as accepting without reservation all the conclusions and views expressed by the Commissioner in relation to individual persons. The Attorney General does not accept the views expressed by the Commissioner in paragraph 3.89 of the report to the effect that a new law was not needed. But the views expressed require action.
Remedial Action in Regard to the Crown Solicitor's Office
The standing of the Crown Solicitor's Office, and the integrity and competence of some of its officers, are brought into question by these matters.The proper course is to deal with the problem quickly and effectively.
A number of officers referred to in the report have retired since the time of their involvement. There is no suggestion that any committed a criminal offence. The Commission has been critical of some officers who remain on the staff. It has expressed conclusions of a very serious character in relation to two officers. One of these officers

was directly involved in the conduct of the particular case referred to by the Commission. Another was not officially involved in it but in the course of its investigation the Commission found some association between this officer and a number of companies which seem to have been involved in bottom-of-the-harbour schemes. Other conduct in which this officer was found to be involved also gives cause for concern.
As a first step, the divisional manager of the Crown Solicitor's Division was sent to Perth to undertake inquiries there and to examine work in the office that was allotted to those officers. The two officers have been suspended from duty pending decisions whether to charge either or both of them with disciplinary offences under the Public Service Act. Appropriate action under that Act is in process.
On the wider questions of organisation, management and control of the Crown Solicitor's Division, the former Crown Solicitor initiated an internal management review in 1978 which culminated in the establishment of a joint management review of the Crown Solicitor's Divsion in 1979. That review was carried out from November 1979 to June 1980. That review, by a team consisting of a private management consultant, a representative of the Public Service Board and a Crown Solicitor's officer, recommended major changes in the management structure. Its recommendations were readily accepted by the Attorney-General's Department. Considerable progress has been made in implementation. One of the major recommendations of the JMR was for the establishment of a management and coordination branch of the Crown Solicitor's Div-
ision headed by a first assistant crown solicitor having a local designation of divisional manager. A major function of this branch is to develop and improve Deputy Crown Solicitor's offices and to co-ordinate, guide and oversight the work of those offices. The three top positions in the branch are filled and working. Other appointments are in train.
There remains, however, a question as to the adequacy of the staff resources of the Crown Solicitor's Division. The Government has asked the Public Service Board to report to it as a matter of urgency on the adequacy of the staffing and resources of the Crown Solicitor's Division. In view of the Royal Commission's findings, however, the Government sees a need for a more particular external examination of the Perth office and its relationship to the Crown Solicitor's central office in Canberra and with the Australian Taxation Office and other client bodies in Perth. The Attorney-General is arranging for such an

Activities a/Ship Painters and Dockers' Union 24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 839

examination to be carried out by the President of the Law Council of Australia or by a suitably qualified person nominated by the President. In brief, the terms of reference of the inquiry will be as follows:
To review the operation of the Deputy Crown Solicitor's Office, Perth, generally, but with particular regard to
(a) the professional performance of the Office;
(b) the adequacy of the management and the effectiveness of work control systems;
(c) the adequacy of numbers of professional and support staff to ensure the timely performance of work at an appropriate level of quality;
(d) the effectiveness of the organisation of the Office for the provision of legal services for the Commonwealth Government;
(e) the effectiveness of supervision of the Office by the Crown Solicitor's central office; and
(f) the effectiveness of procedures in client departments in relation to matters referred to the Office.
Recommendations for Changes in Section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act and the Royal
Commissions Act
Mr Costigan has recommended a number of changes in the secrecy provisions of section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act to allow in particular for more access to and use of Taxation Office information in law enforcement. These recommendations are for changes additional to the amendments to the secrecy provisions made earlier this year. The Government will give early and careful consideration to these recommennations. The Government will also examine carefully the Royal Commission's recommendations for amendments to the Royal Commission's Act to provide additional powers in regard to search warrants and compulsion of witnesses. I present the following paper:
Federated Ship Painters and Dockers' Union-4th Interim Report of the Royal Commission-Volume I-Ministerial Statement, 24 August 1982.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (4.57)-1 do not know whether I can deal with this matter adequately in 16 minutes but I will do my best. The speech made by the Minister for Communications and Minister Assisting the Attorney-General (Mr N. A. Brown) is another part of the serial in respect of tax avoidance in this country. We now have a further report from Mr Costigan, a practising lawyer, who was given the job of investigating certain matters relating to the activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union. Let me say at the onset that I think the matter goes past the activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union. It goes to highly organised crime and to the fact that the taxpayer in this country has been asked to foot the bill for people who are guilty of criminal activities and did not pay their tax. I think it begs the question for the Government to suggest in a few sentences that perhaps all will be well because it will do certain things. It cuts no ice with me to suggest that one of the best things to do is to institute remedial action in the Crown Solicitor's Office. We cannot put all the blame on to the Perth office when we know that the legal opinion given to the Perth office was also given to the Commissioner of Taxation and the Deputy Commissioner of Taxation.
What is this matter about? Is it not about incompetence, ineptitude and what Parliament does when Ministers have to administer laws which they are unwilling or unable to administer? That is the real crux of the matter. We have had nothing but explanations. On this occasion I note again that there will be some suggestion of amending section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act. That provision relates to secrecy. That is not a new matter either. If we refer back to the Australian Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drugs, we will see that three years ago Mr Justice Williams recommended that something be done about making taxation records available in relation to criminal matters. Taxation avoidance is obviously a crime. As a safeguard one needed a judicial warrant.

I

840 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982 Activities o/Ship Painters and Dockers' Union

wish to give high praise to the McCabe-Lafranchi report. It was a Victorian report which the Cain Government tabled, but it was commissioned by the earlier government. That report clearly shows that active criminal elements were involved not in the initiation of tax avoidance but in the final guarantee that nobody would be found. The painters and dockers were involved in the lowest level of that activity. What about the people who earned the money and did not pay tax? Not one of them has been dealt with. What is the excuse? Let me refer to the McCabe-Lafranchi report, which at page 25, states:
. . . the dealer sold on upwards of 3,000 companies to sham directors . . . no prosecution has been instituted by the Australian Taxation Office . . . and . " . only two sale-on transactions have been fully investigated.
Can we say that people are doing their jobs? Can we put the whole blame on the Public Service structure? Surely we would know what was happening in regard to the massive numbers of people we could not find. Surely we would know, as the Treasurer himself admits on page 24 of his statement, about this picture developing. He said that in 1975 he found 729 participants involved in tax avoidance. The next year the figure grew to 2,700 and the following year grew to 10,200. He accepts that there are over 31,000 tax avoidance cases dating from 1975 to 1979. Is it not incredible to think that when one has that matter within one's control, one cannot find out what is wrong?
It took McCabe and Lafranchi to identify some of the matters. It took Mr Costigan, investigating another matter altogether-the Ship Painters and Dockers Union-to advert to the fraud of the Commonwealth. Unless (hose two groups had been set up we would still be walking around in the dark wondering what happened to the tax revenue. Cain, the Premier of Victoria, made the very valid statement that the amount of fraud could be $6 billion. That is no small amount of money. If we were able to offer that amount in tax cuts it would be considerable. The Opposition will not get the benefit of recommendations which, as I understand it, are contained in two other volumes of this report because Mr Costigan considers it is not in the public interest that they be disclosed. I ask the Government to make them available to the Leader of the Opposition.
If one looks at organised crime in this country, one will see that it does not relate only to activities within this country. It involves international efforts; it relates to heroin dealings; and it relates to massive amounts of moneys being transferred out of this country. I understand that that might be the reason why the Government is, at this

stage, unable to do anything about making the parts I have referred to public. Will this matter be solved by establishing a crimes commission? I see nothing wrong with a crimes commission. But we do not want to have a crimes commission that will be bogged down by a rigid framework or whose instructions are so general.
What we need is a crimes commission that has specific instructions, that has accountability that enables it to deal with specific matters that we-the Parliament-determine. We cannot have a rambling commission that can go on and on without us knowing whether it wi1l maintain the interest. It is very clear that practising members of the legal profession are required to identify clearly what has been going wrong. It is very clear that Government Ministers are unable to do it. We must not blame the present Treasurer. The previous Treasurer, the right honourable member for Flinders, Sir Phillip Lynch, must have had some knowledge of these matters which relate to the inability to collect revenue. Surely legal advice ought to have been expected from an Attorney General or Attorneys-General. Surely they know the law. Surely they must have seen some evidence of fraud when they were trying to identify the number of companies that could not be found.
In one case-I think it was Keirad Pty Ltd, formerly Corser Homes, in Western Australia-a liquidator was appointed. I will deal with that matter in a minute. Nothing was ever done; nothing happened. Yet that is one matter in respect of which we are aware of some initiation of action. Why was no action taken in respect of Corser Homes? It appears from Mr Costigan's report that the liquidator got no help at all. The McCabe-Lafranchi report states that the investigation sections were aware that companies were being dumped. The question must be asked: Why was no action taken? Companies, all across Australia-not just in Perth-were being dumped. They were being dumped everywhere and nothing was done. Whatever the position, Mr Costigan states, those responsible for the decision not to prosecute ought to be regarded as at least partly answerable for massive losses of revenue. He goes on to make the point that the failure to prosecute certainly promoted what he calls the nerve of the main promotor. The principle of deterrence was not utilised. According to the McCabe-Lafranchi report and the Victorian Corporate Affairs Office, the Victorian office is still receiving particulars from directors and officers which are false and misleading. Accordingly, the abuse of the philosophy is still continuing. The philosophy is that because of the legislation which

Activities o/Ship Painters and Dockers' Union 24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 841

the Treasurer introduced in 1980, taxation avoidance would be eliminated. In other words, it is still going on and it is flourishing. How stupid it is to see the Treasurer acting like little boy lost saying: 'It has all been fixed up'. It takes the presentation of these reports before one can find out how incompetent and unable the Government is to deal with the matter.
One should look at the sheer facts. I have already pointed out that banks at a little place called Stones Corner in Brisbane in one afternoon dealt with transactions of $30m to $50m, involving perhaps 50 companies which were changing hands. What more evidence does the Government want of conspiring in relation to such sham actions? What was wrong with the law under the Federal Crimes Act which relates to conspiracy to defraud. Surely the Government could have dealt with it using the provisions of this Act. What was wrong with the use of section 231, and others, of the Income Tax Assessment Act which talks about tax avoidance being an offence which certainly renders a person liable to a double penalty.
When one is talking about a conspiracy arrangement and Dorothy Dix letters which are written within the Australian Taxation Office, one notes that McCabe and Lafranchi were unable to get possession of the evidence. What do the people of Australia think when they realise that laws are passed and nobody will implement them despite legal advice that has been given by two top Q.Cs. Mr Brinsden is one. I will not name the other one but I mentioned him the other day. He is a leading Q.c. from Victoria. In 1978, he said: 'You certainly should be prosecuting in these matters'. His opinion was that if a conspiracy to defraud the Commonwealth can be established there is a strong possibility that the agreement being the subject of that conspiracy is itself a nullity. Do we need this present charade which is coming from the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and others that we need to pass retrospective legislation. The Government does not need to do it. It should be prosecuting now. One can set aside every purported transfer pursuant to a sham or fraudulent transaction. The legal opinion on that issue was given as far back as 1978. The point was made that the vendors are still liable. In fact they are liable.
What is this Government about? Is it trying to hoodwink the people of Australia that it never had the power, that it did not know, that it needs to pass more laws when the legal advice and the facts clearly show that it has had the power all the time. The only thing that has been wrong with this Government is its incompetence and inability to accept the law. What value is it to have in this

statement the Attorney-General saying that he does not accept the suggestion in paragraph 389 of the Costigan report. Paragraph 389 of Mr Costigan's report states:
What was worse was that the Crown Solicitor's Office permitted the inference to arise that the existing law was not adequate and that new laws were needed. Thus the Parliament of the Commonwealth of Australia was induced to enact new criminal laws of great breadth and scope when all that was required was the enforcement of the existing laws.
Is it any wonder that the Attorney-General cannot accept that advice? It shoots holes through the present posture of the Government that it needs to pass new laws. Two opinions were given, one by Mr Brinsden who was mentioned, but also by another person. What lawyer worth his salt would not advise that there was a conspiracy to defraud and the Government could surely do something about it. So there is incompetence and there is a failure of ministerial responsibility.
There is the extraordinary claim that action is exempted by section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act. Section 16 of the Income Tax Assessment Act contains certain secrecy provisions. But, if one looks closely at it, one will see that a lot of people are able to get information if the Treasurer permits it. We have this incredible situation, by self interpretation, that the Treasurer can make information available to a number of people but he cannot even get any information himself, not about the detail but about the amount of the fraud. Is it not important that, when Ministers are appointed to proper responsible office, they administer that office? The whole series of events here shows dreadful problems about the inability of a Minister to administer a department, whether it be the Attorney-General's Department or the Taxation Office.
Both the Attorneys-whether they be present or past-certainly should have known what their responsibilities were. The present Treasurer and his predecessor surely should have known what their responsibilities were. What value is it for us to have this nonsense by the Treasurer who, when talking about a prosecution that Mr Brinsden recommended but was not proceeded with for four or five years and which then lapsed because of what was deemed insufficient evidence-it now comes to light that the insufficient evidence was obviously hidden by somebody who might be guilty-made the point that with the benefit of hindsight, of course, such a period seems inordinately long. Nobody in his right mind would make statements like that. Surely the Treasurer could have told us in June that he knew that an opinion had been given and that it was not carried out. He makes no reference to the fact that there was an inability to carry out that opinion.

842 REPRESENTATlVES 24 August 1982 Activities ofShip Painters and Dockers' Union

By way of excuse, the Treasurer makes the point that it is very difficult to establish a charge of conspiracy. He says that conspiracy cases are notoriously difficult to establish and sustain. Nonsense! One can get the facts from McCabe and Lafranchi who interviewed people who admitted that the whole series of transactions were a sham from the word go. There is certainly plenty of evidence from people which clearly indicates, apart from the facts themselves, that there was conspiracy. That is the important part. Why was it not disclosed?
I think it is important that this matter be the subject of investigation. I think it is important that the Prime Minister has a look at the conduct of the departments and the Ministers concerned from the point of view of giving the nation some assurance that we do not need to have a parade of proposed laws here to remedy the defects. The clear evidence was that the defects were always remedial in terms of the advice given by legal opinion. That is the nub of what we are about at the present time. (Extension oftime granted).
In the remaining few minutes let us look at what Mr Costigan has said. He makes the point on page 35 of his report that in the opinion of Mr Brinsden, QC., something should have been done and prosecutions should have been launched. Page 36 of the report states:
Mr Brinsden's opinion was sent to the Deputy Commissioner of Taxation, and then to the Commissioner of Taxation.
One cannot limit it to the Perth office; it had spread past that. I make the point in the same context, now that the Treasurer is here, that it was known also that another opinion was given in Victoria in 1978 on the same issue.
Mr Howard-September 1978.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-The Treasurer admits that he knows of it. I am pleased about that. At page 69 Mr Costigan states:
To assert that there was insufficient evidence to establish an intent to defraud the Commonwealth was contrary to Mr Brinsden's opinion . . .
Yet the Treasurer asserted it in his statement of June this year. Mr Costigan made this point:
To make such a statement without explaining how these responsible and diligent people had been wrong was to display a contemptuous attitude towards them and the Taxation Office, and it ill became the Crown Solicitor's Office.
Why are we getting all this nonsense when Costigan can clearly show quite properly how preposterous it was to suggest that? I have already dealt with the fact-I emphasise this-that there was no need to alter the existing law. The laws were quite adequate. No new laws were needed. Mr Costigan says that what was needed was a

guarantee that the existing laws would be applied. The report states:
To say that the new laws have been effective is not to answer this criticism. The new laws will be effective only if they are enforced.
It is no good talking about having a crimes commission and all these other things unless we can get to the facts. It would not be able to get to the facts. There is nothing in the statement today that would identify these facts. The Government needed to apply the law. It needed a prosecution. Mr Costigan said:
The only reason why the former laws were not effective . . . was because those laws were not enforced. The new law will also be ineffective if it is not enforced.
The answer lies not in new laws but in the enforcement, promptly, of the old.
The criminal proceedings would have had a salutary effect upon the promotion of the fraudulent schemes.
That is the issue. Mr Costigan talked about the unfortunate liquidator-the only one who was ever appointed. He had an horrendous task. Mr Costigan said that after seven years the liquidator appointed by the Government gave up the ghost because he could not get any answers, even to letters. Mr Costigan makes the point:
Given that 7 years had passed without any help from the one party who could assist, the feelings of a liquidator are understandable.
Is it not incredible to think that nobody would know that the liquidator was having trouble, that the liquidator was writing all these letters? He made the point that in those circumstances the failure of the Taxation Office to equip the liquidator with the records explains the situation. We are happy to know, of course, that the liquidator now has the records, having got them in recent days. Mr Costigan made the point that the Taxation Office lives in a Federal system and it is subject to the laws of both the States and the Commonwealth and it should learn to live with them and have regard for its responsibilities. That would apply to Ministers as well.
Talking about the problems of the Perth office, Mr Costigan made the point on page 85 that Canberra was just as involved. Everyone in Canberra had the legal opinions. Why would one blame just some public servant in Perth if that was the issue? On page 90 Mr Costigan talks about the proliferation of these schemes, about how they mushroomed. Mr Costigan made a very valid point when he stated:
Whereas with inflation there has been a great increase-six fold-in the taxable incomes of individuals, there has been only a slight increase in the taxable incomes of private companies.

There is the lesson. There has been a six-fold increase for the pay-as-you-earn taxpayer, who has had to bear this burden, and hardly any in the company structure. Would not any alert Treasurer notice the position? Would he not notice that he was not getting the revenue from the company section? Would he not notice that there was an additional burden on the unfortunate PA YE taxpayer? Is that not clearly a searchlight that he should have noticed and done something? In speeches we have been making here for a long while we have been asking the Treasurer why he is continuing to increase the burden in the light of this evasion, which we can identify as amounting to $6 billion. These facts ought to be highlighted.

SOCIAL WELFARE
Ministerial Statement
Debate resumed from 17 August, on the following paper presented by Mr Wilson:
Social Welfare Objectives, Ministerial Statement, 17 August 1982
and on motion by Sir James Killen:
That the House take note of the paper.
Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (5.21)-ln continuing my remarks on this statement of the other evening I think, perhaps, I should begin with the observation I made the other evening that in looking at this social welfare statement in relation to the Budget we need to observe that nothing concentrates this Government's mind better on the needs of the poor than the approach of an election. It is characteristic of this social welfare statement that we have here a consideration of the poor in this society to an extent that I can remember in no previous Fraser Budget. I suggest that the reasons for that concentration are not all completely admirable. We on this side of the House welcome some of the generosity that has been shown in aspects of the social welfare provision in the Budget. Certainly, there is increased welfare expenditure, but it must be confessed that it is spread fairly thinly. Many of the small presents made in the Budget are merely an acknowledgment of seven years of neglect by the Fraser Government. Indeed, most of the provisions can be summed up as too little and too late.
Problems recognised in the Budget should have been recognised years ago and remedial action should have been provided years ago. Many of

these difficulties needed the provision of regular increments-not a sudden pre-election handout. Many of these problems would have been better dealt with if they had been dealt with over seven years in a remedial manner rather than in this sudden and, in some cases, rather surprising pre-election handout. Let us take an example. The Budget provides a derisory rise of $4 a week for the unemployed 16 and 17 year olds. For seven years there has been no increase. It has stood at $36 since 1975; it has now been raised to $40. It has been frozen, therefore, for seven years at that level. In that period pensions have been lifted 14 times. All I am suggesting is that a government which really cared about these problems would have been doing something over the years to adjust that provision. In those seven years the value of the dollar has been nearly halved, so that even the $40 which will be provided from 1 November is roughly equivalent to $20 in 1975. The $4 increase is, in fact, no compensation whatsoever for that erosion. The figure of $40 a week for a single person is still less than half the poverty line. There seems to be no recognition in the Budget that many of these young people need to live away from home on that $40 a week. The
reasons for them having to live away from home can be catalogued. In many cases, given the nature of poverty and unemployment in our society, these young people come from families which are already very poor and which have difficulties in coping with near adult children. So in many cases they have to leave the family situation and somehow survive on $40 a week, that is, on $5 a day. There are young people in country families who can find employment only by moving away from their family in the country situation and coming to the city to look for jobs. But how can such people survive in our cities today on $5 a day? There does not seem to be any recognition at all of the kind of degradation that these young people will often be thrown into as a result of a search for work with quite inadequate living provisions to sustain them during that search for work. It is perhaps all right if the young person has a complete family backup-family accommodation, family provision and family support. But for many of the young people we are talking about-for many of these 16-year-olds and 17year-olds-there is not that family backup. They do not have family financial resources and they are required to exist entirely on what was $36 a week for seven years and what has now been raised to $40 a week.
Let it be quite clear that if the Government is sincerely concerned, and not simply electorally concerned, with the problems these people face,

844 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Social Welfare

there is a need at least for a living away from home supplement for the young unemployed. The very least we could demand is that for young people who are not backed and supported in a home situation there should be a living away from home supplement. I beg the Government to look at that provision as a possible support for these young people.
Let us take the family allowance as another example of rather belated activity by the Government. From 1976 to 1981-for those five yearsthere was, in fact, no change whatsoever in the family allowance. It simply was provided and it was gradually eroded over those five years. Budgets came and went and no change was made to the family allowance. The value of the family allowance was reduced in those five years by about 60 per cent. Let us be fair to the Government. The 1981 Budget-that is last year's
Budget-gave a substantial rise but only for third and subsequent children. So after last year's Budget there was some improvement in the case of 25 per cent of all families with dependent children. Finally, this year the Budget has looked after the other 75 per cent by providing a rise for first and second children.
But when we look at those figures we find that the total expenditure is $396m short of what it would have been in 1982-83 if there had been proper indexation from 1976. That is, if the family allowance had been properly indexed from the time it was introduced it would be costing the Government-therefore, be paid to familiesnearly $400m a year more than is now provided. What does that mean? In terms of a family with five children that is something like $33.20 a month less or nearly $400 a year less simply because the Government has failed to index this provision. Even though the Government can now talk of the increases it has carried through, those increases still trail behind indexation as, of course, they trail behind indexation over the whole range of social welfare provisions.
Thirdly, when we examine these proposals we find that the unemployed remain the group most discriminated against by this Government. Of all the social security beneficiaries they are the ones who are discriminated against, particularly the adult unemployed without dependants. It is true that the benefit for the adult unemployed without dependants will be raised from I November 1982 from the present $58.10 a week to $64 a week so that it will become half the rate paid to an unemployed couple. This is the third rise in this grant in three years. We appreciate the fact that the Government has begun to take action in this area, but one of the reasons for its taking action is

simply the moral problem that developed in this area. The single rate was less than half the married rate. It encouraged de facto relations amongst young people in order simply to get the married rate and twice-yearly ind9tltion, and the Government's moralistic problems caught up with it. Of course, these are the sorts of things which flow from these forms of payment. It is true that the Government has made an effort to cope with this problem but as the single rate is not indexed and as the employment benefit for married couples is indexed, very rapidly these two will become apart
again and the same problems will arise. That is one form of discrimination.
Another form of discrimination against the unemployed when compared with other pensioners and beneficiaries of the same age and with the same needs is that, firstly, the unemployed are not paid supplementary allowances. They often face the same costs in rent as pensioners in private rental accommodation. Indeed, I can see no difference between the problems faced by poor pensioners and the problems faced by the unemployed in the search for private rental accommodation and the cost of that rental accommodation. In many cases the resources of the unemployed are even more limited than those of pensioners, yet they are denied any rent allowance or supplementary allowance. If the Government is seriously concerned with adequate provision for those who are worse off in our society, there is need for supplementary assistance, that is, rent assistance for the long term unemployed.
The unemployed secure only a health care card. They do not get a pensioner health benefit card as do other pensioner groups. That means that all the unemployed can get is concessional provision on pharmaceuticals. That is, the unemployed are required to pay $2 per prescription whereas a person who has a pensioner health benefit card receives free pharmaceuticals. Again, the income levels to be eligible for health care cards and the income levels to be eligible for pensioner health benefit cards are very similar. Why should they not receive similarity of treatment in relation to pharmaceutical benefits? Why should there be a discrimination between two groups of people who are roughly in the same income situation, as is provided through these provisions. Whilst we appreciate that an effort has been made to provide the unemployed with concessional provision of pharmaceuticals, it still leaves open the question: Why should they not be treated in exactly the same way as our pensioners who have pensioner health benefit cards?
Again we find that the unemployed are discriminated against when compared with other

Social Welfare

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 845

pensioners if we look at fringe benefits. Most of the range of fringe benefits available to pensioners are generally denied to the unemployed. Certainly they are denied by the Commonwealth, although in some cases this is modified by generosity on the part of some States. Examples are hearing aid concessions, motor registration rebates, rate reductions, transport concessions and fuel bill relief. These fringe benefits which are provided for many pensioners just do not apply to the unemployed. They are, as somebody has said, kinds of nonpersons when we come to the fringe benefit area. No pensioner pays tax on spouse or children's allowances but the unemployed are taxed on these allowances. Indeed, an unemployed family which earns nothing during a year but has three or more children will be taxed simply because there are children. I think it must be difficult for any member of the Government to defend this contrast between the provision for pensioners and the provision, in this case, for the long term unemployed.
I simply stress to the Government the importance and urgency of rectifying these anomalies and discriminations. That is becoming even more urgent because of the accelerating rate of unemployment. We now have on record the Treasurer (Mr Howard), the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee) and the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) all indicating that unemployment levels will rise in this society. It is not just a problem of school leavers not being able to get into the work force. Increasingly, families are having to live on the unemployment benefit because their breadwinners are facing redundancy and sackings en masse. It is already agreed by most commentators that the Budget figures on unemployment are fudged. Only a week has passed since the Budget was introduced but it is generally accepted that the figures in relation to the unemployed, and particularly in relation to the cost of the unemployment benefit, are rubbery figures. Indeed, we would argue that those unemployment benefit figures are underestimated by $300m. Already, of course, unemployment has gone through the 400,000 barrier at a time of the year when employment is usually looking up. What sorts of figures will we be faced with in February or March of next year? -1 strongly warn the Government that these anomalies and discriminations in the treatment of the unemployed will become more acute in the coming year.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.

Mr HARRIS (Chisholm) (5.36)-1 welcome the announcements made in the Budget in support of the needy and those important groups in the community whom we ought to support. There are many new initiatives which will be of real help to large numbers in the community. The Budget recognises the needs. It also recognises the views widely held by many Government members. In particular, I am very pleased to see the help provided in the Budget for families and pensioners.
This Budget increases the family allowance for the first two children by 50 per cent. This Government is committed to the family. It is an area we must support. It is a vital part of our community which I hope we would all agree must be given every support and encouragement. The family is worth supporting, not just as a worthwhileindeed vital-institution but also as the best and often the most effective means of dealing with many of the problems which this community faces. The family allowance is a payment of great significance. It recognises the value of the family in this community. It is said that it assists those who have means as well as those who are without means. I suppose that is true. But insofar as it is a direct cash payment it unquestionably supports and helps most those who most need it. It is in fact, a transfer payment from those who do not have children to those who do. It recognises the greater obligation and cost of maintaining children. I am glad to see that in the Budget it is the first and second children who are recognised; it is in respect of those children that the increases relate. The average Australian family, I suppose, these days would be a one or two child family.
Mr McLean-Some have more.
Mr HARRIS-Some have more, that is true, but most of us have two, or, as in my own case, three. The reality and the common experience is that the first child is the most expensive. It is the one that leads to the setting up expenses in the home. The others tend to get a certain measure of hand-me-downs. The birth of the first child is also normally the occasion upon which the wifecertainly these days-ceases employment. It is the moment of greatest need in the family. Therefore, the family allowance ought to be recognised as the allowance which needed increasing. The Budget and the Government have this year done that. I welcome it. I hope that in succeeding years further increases will be weighted in favour of the first and second child as they involve the greatest cost impact and payments in this area, I believe, will do the most good. In addition to the increases in the family allowance, from May 1983 a tax free family income supplement for low income families, apart from pensioners and beneficiaries, of up

846 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Social Welfare

to $10 per week for each dependent child will be available. This measure reflects our concern for low income families who have responsibility for bringing up children. Low income families will also be assisted by a rise in the qualifying limits for
health cards which, as a result of the consumer price index increase and adjustments to the income test for the unemployment benefits, to which they are linked, will go up to $184 a week for a married couple plus $20 for each child in the
family.
One cannot discuss the help given in the Budget to families without referring to the changes in the taxation arrangements. The spouse rebate has been increased by $200, which is a significant help to single income families. There are other measures. The income tax exemption limit has been raised by $400. The standard rate of tax has been reduced from 32 cents in the dollar to 30 cents in the dollar. These measures will have a significant benefit for the average family. For example, the family of a single income earner earning about the average income of $320 a week with two dependent children will be $15 or so better off. A family earning $200 a week will be $24 better off. A family earning $184 a week will be $32 a week better off. That represents an 18.5 per cent increase in disposable income for such a family. Those figures indicate the value and the benefit of this Budget to families and also the weighting given to those who have family responsibilities and those who have average and lower incomes. Of course, all this is before taking into account the new tax rebate of 30 per cent on payments of home loan interest at rates above 10 per cent on loans up to $60,000. Interest rates, it is true, are high. Many average families have to pay significant interest bills and this rebate will be of great benefit to them.
The second area of real benefit I have mentioned is that of the pensioner. I begin by reminding the House that the decisions announced in the Budget complement the important measures first adopted in the 1976 Budget by the Fraser Government to protect and automatically cover against inflation most pensioners and beneficiaries who
receive the basic rate of pension by twice yearly adjustments to pension rates linked to consumer price index increases. It should be noted that this commitment is expensive. The November 1982 and May 1983 increases will cost about $770m in a full year. These regular adjustments are necessary for pensioners to meet inflation, but involve large sums of money. It should be remembered too that as time goes on the number of people receiving the age pension will increase. There are, however, several worthwhile additional measures

which have been announced in this Budget which are of real benefit, I believe, to pensioners. The allowable income limit has been increased from $20 a week to $30 a week for single pensioners which is a 50 per cent increase and from $34.50 a week to $50 a week for couples. The fringe benefit income test has also been increased. The income limit has been raised from $40 a week to $54 a week for a single pensioner and from $48.50 to $90 a week for a couple. There can be little doubt
that each of these measures is timely. The effect of inflation has eroded the value of the previous income limits.
It is sometimes said that affluent people are receiving the pension. If this is so, it is not the result of the previous income limits. People who, in fact, are well off and receive the pension have achieved this by having their means in a form which does
not attract the income test or, to put it another way, to which the income test cannot apply. Whether there are a large number of these people or not, there are people who are entitled to greater assistance and to whom these changes in the means test will apply. People who until now have been receiving, say, $40 a week in private income in addition to the pension have suffered disability or loss. There can be no doubt of that. I am very glad that by these measures we have done something to improve their lot significantly. They will also give recognition to those who have made a contribution to their own retirement and represent some incentive to them to continue to do so. They assist those pensioners who have part time work or some small superannuation policy.
I will give some examples of how this assists pen-
sioners. A single pensioner under 70 years of age, the most numerous class, earning $54 a week private income-income additional to the pensionwill in November be $11.53 a week better off and will also be entitled to fringe benefits. In November somebody with $40 a week private or additional income will be $12.26 a week better off, again with fringe benefits. Somebody receiving or earning $30 a week in addition to the pension will be $12.79 a week better off. That last person, of course, already has the pensioner benefit health card. Anybody can see from these examples that there have been significant advances for the aged and pensioners.
I mention the position of married aged pensioner couples under 70 for they too will benefit significantly from the changes introduced in this Budget. A couple who receive $90 a week additional income will in November be approximately $13 a week better off. Of course, they also now receive the fringe benefit of a health card. A couple with $50 a week additional income will be

Social Welfare

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 847

$14.36 a week better off and will receive the fringe benefit of a heallth card. A couple who are presently receiving $40 a week in additional income will be $15.81 a week better off in November. They, of course, would already be receiving the health benefit card.
There are, of course, other benefits in the Budget for the pensioner. A new tax rebate for pensioners has been introduced. This will be $167 this year and $250 next year. This year the rebate will be reduced by 12.5c for each dollar earned above $5,007. This step, combined with a $400 increase in the tax free threshold, will ensure that pensioners with a pension and no other income will pay no tax at all. The effect of these measures is that a pensioner can have a total income, including a pension, of $5,429 a year-over $100 a week-without having to pay any tax. As I have said, the rebate reduces at the rate of 12.5c, but it does not cut out until income of around $7000. As with the easing of the means test on pensions and on fringe benefits, these steps represent a real improvement for pensioners. They also represent both an incentive and a reward for people to provide, to the extent they are able, for their own retirement.
Further help for the pensioner is also provided in a new three-tiered arrangement for pharmaceutical benefits. Those pensioners who have the health benefit card will continue to receive medicines on the same basis. Of course, following the relaxation of the income test, more pensioners will be entitled to that card. In addition, other pensioners without the card will no longer pay the $3.20 a prescription as this has been reduced to $2 a prescription. This is real help to the pensioner, particularly the aged pensioner, who so frequently calls on medical assistance.
A final consideration with regard to help for the aged is the increase in funds available for aged persons accommodation and elderly citizen's centres. This too has been significantly increased. This Budget represents a package of measures which assist a great many people in the community. As I have said, I am very glad for the help provided to the pensioner. I am very glad for the help provided to the family, the basic and vital unit in our community. There are many other items in the Budget which time will not permit me to mention this evening, including help for the unemployed, the disabled and those people requiring child care services-all of which are worthy of mention. I have no doubt my colleagues will be mentioning them during the forthcoming debate on the Budget.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr SCOTT (Hindmarsh) (5.50)-The ministerial statement canvassed a wide range of matters including the housing industry, unemployment, consumer price index increases, welfare payments, invalid pensions, child services, homeless youth, health care and so on. Petition after petition in this House has sought some relief from the Fraser Government's policies. They have concerned interest rates, child care services, sales tax, survival of Community Youth Support Scheme projects and relief for the unemployed. Speech after speech by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) and members of the Labor Party have sought relief from the economic hardships being imposed by this Government on the people of Australia.
Perhaps the most generous thing we could say of this ministerial statement is that it recognises the issues we on this side of the House have been raising since the Fraser Government was elected and imposed these policies on the people of Australia. The Government recognises the issues. One cannot go on to say that the Government has responded adequately or responsibly to the needs of the nation. there will be jobs for those who want to work. We will abolish the Prices Justification Tribunal. We will establish a sound basis of economic responsibility. There will be no international safaris by members of Parliament.
Those are just some of the promises of the Fraser Government. Others include:
We will maintain Medibank, and ensure that the standard of health care does not decline.
Perhaps the lowest level ever reached in those promises was that made by the present leader of the Government when in 1977 he said:
Unemployment will fall from February and will continue to fall.
Mr Milton-Who said that?

848 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Social Welfare

Mr SCOTT-John Malcolm Fraser, the leader of the present Government. Those promises have been echoed in the community and they will never be believed again. The 1982-83 Budget clearly predicts a massive rise in the number of unemployed. Apart from those currently unemployed, one in every thirty of those now working will be unemployed by July 1983. These are the predictions of the present Budget. The statement of the Minister Assisting the Minister for Social Security (Mr Wilson) totally ignores those infamous promises. Instead, there is a pathetic attempt to apply a coating of sugar to this bitter pill, this 1982-83 Budget. The Minister has said that the Government has recognised the problems in housing. Other speakers, including the shadow Minister, the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren), will clearly expose the failure of the Government either to recognise properly or to deal properly with the problems of the home buyer or of the housing industry.
I will content myself with dealing with the serious problems of both the home buyer and the housing industry in South Australia in particular and the effects on that section of the community. I wish to show the Minister's failure in this state-
ment to understand those problems. It is often said that the housing crisis in Australia is the second greatest social tragedy after unemployment to have developed during the seven years of this Government. That statement is true and will remain so until the present Government is thrown out of office and the underlying ideological assumption which promotes anti-housing policy is dismissed from the domestic political agenda.
At present the bank interest rates for first home loans stand at 13.5 per cent, having risen from 9.5 per cent in March 1980. This represents an increase in money terms of $88 a month for an average $30,000 loan over 25 years. Many borrowers, however, seek credit at building societies. Here the national average charge is between 14 per cent and 16t per cent. In South Australia, the level at present is held at 14+ per cent despite significant pressure from the building societies. It is widely anticipated that Premier Tonkin will buckle under this pressure, and we can expect a rise in the near future. This rise will be of the order of at least I t per cent and probably 2 per cent. Those people forced to go to financial institutions for second mortgage loans are faced with increases in excess of 20 per cent.
First home buyers are finding it increasingly difficult to gain access to traditional forms of borrowing. A typical $30,000, 25-year loan requires a monthly repayment to a savings bank of $350. This requires a household income of at least I \0

per cent of average weekly male earnings. Such a requirement excludes 80 per cent of prospective home buyers. Only one buyer in five can meet such a target. The social cost of this virtually unbridled rate of increase is demonstrated by looking at the total debt connected with a typical $30,000, 25-year loan. In March 1980 the total debt on such a loan at an interest rate of 9t per cent was $78.630. It is now $\04,907. The tight monetary policies of this Government have added $26,277 to the individual debt on an average home loan-and it was done in just two years. This is an increase of just over one-third-33.4 per cent.
It is estimated that in the 1980s, because of natural population increases and the projected migrant intake, 160,000 new dwellings need to be constructed each year. The Fraser Government, far from reaching or even aiming at this necessary target, is backing away from it. In 1980-81, there were 149,000 new commencements. In the financial year just passed the figure was down to less than 130,000. This year it is estimated by the Housing Industry Association that new commencements will amount to only 117,000. This represents a shortfall in one year of 43,000 dwellings and a shortfall in the first three years of the decade of 85,000 dwellings. I seek leave to continue my remarks later.

Mr HAYDEN (Oxley:-Leader of the Opposition) (8.0)-This Budget is a betrayal of the Australian people. The economy is out of control, sliding backwards into depression. Business has run into a brick wall. Our interest rates are the highest in the world. Record numbers of Australians cannot get work. Two million people are living in poverty. Consumer confidence is at its lowest for years. And all the Government can do is panic and run for it. Every year for seven years a Treasurer has stood up in this place like the boy on the burning deck and told us that, appearances aside, the good times were about to roll. This year the Treasurer (Mr Howard) has come into this House waving a white flag of surrender.
The economy will not grow this coming year. Unemployment will rise. Poverty will worsen. Interest rates will stay high. Inflation will increase. And the clowns who are supposed to have

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 849

the economy in hand have decided that it is all too hot to handle. For an hour and a half last Tuesday night the Treasurer talked his way around this point. Not once did he offer a word of apology for it to the people who are going to suffer as a result of the excesses of his Government's economic policies. Every Australian has the right to be outraged that this Government, which has the hide to suggest that it alone knows how to handle the economy, has run away from its obligations to deal fairly and honestly with the Australian people. It has offered a packet of goodies designed to make the bitter economic medicine go down. But the figures on which these goodies are based, as everybody knows, are shaky and shonky.
This Budget is an insult to the hundreds and thousands of Australians who have no jobs. It is an insult to the families and individuals who are struggling to make ends meet. It is an insult to working people who pay higher and higher taxes than any generation in Australia's history. It is an insult to those who expected that this would be the year when the Government finally got off its tail after seven years of windy promises which produced nothing but sustained personal suffering, social division, and economic failure. According to this Budget, the Government has given up all responsibility for seeing our stricken economy through 1982-83 and beyond. Instead, it has produced two myxomatosed rabbits out of its hat, a couple of scapegoats to blame for its failings and some concessions to compensate some Australians for the seven lean years they have been put through.
According to the Government, the reasons why, after seven years of promises, we are actually worse off are the demands of greedy wage earners and the downturn in the world economy. Such hypocrisy is astounding even for this Government. Wages are not just a cost to employers; they are a major source, usually the only source, of spending power by working Australians. Keeping wages down to unreasonable levels will actually destroy consumer demand and continue the paralysis of the economy. This is not going to help either employers or employment. In any case, despite what the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) is saying, wage costs are now lower than they were when he took over. Figures dealing with real labour costs and labour productivity show that since 1975-76 real labour costs grew by II per cent and productivity by 12 percent.
I will not be so unkind as to say that the Prime Minister is tripping over the truth in a characteristic way. I will just say that he has not read his own Budget Papers, where these figures are

published. His complaints about wages are rank with hypocrisy. Wages can be a cause of inflation. So can other economic influences such as government charges for petrol, health insurance and sales taxes on essential commodities. The inflationary king hit has been largely coming from incompetent Government pricing policies. It is nonsense to offer wage restraint by corporate executives and public servants as an example to wage earners. Public servants earning $800 a week will not suffer if their salaries are limited to an increase of 7 per cent. But for an electrician on $250 a week wage rises equivalent to 4 per cent less than the rate of inflation are a disaster.
Let me make another point. The fact that we do not have a systematic wages policy is not the fault of wage earners or their unions. For five years the Government undermined our centralised wage fixing system. It managed finally to -destroy it. It was warned about the consequences by employers and employees alike. It wanted market forces in wage fixing and it got them. Now it is bleating. Wages have to be weighed and planned for in an orderly system in which relevant factors have their place. Wages, non-wage incomes, taxes, social security programs and prices all have a role in economic planning. That is the central point to the arrangment that the Australian Labor Party has reached with the Australian Council of Trade Unions. Until we can get all these relevant factors into a proper rational relationship we will never restore order and predictability to economic management. The prices and incomes policy that we offer will be the point from which the economy will progress in a systematic, non-inflationary fashion.
Mr Howard-Ha, hal
Mr HAYDEN-The people who are experts in failure find that hard to swallow. Look at their record. There is one starting point where we will not make a mistake. We will not do what they are doing. It will be the central part of the planning process which we will set up with the highest possible priority. In that planning process all who have a stake in economic decision makingwill have their place. By that I mean employees, employers, national and State governments. This is why we have been arguing that a prices and incomes policy is crucial to a well-run economy. Countries rated by the World Bank as above us in a per capita income scale, such as West Germany, Austria and some of the Scandinavian countries, consider -a prices and incomes arrangement to be an essential element of economic management. The nearest this Budget comes to effective action is its packet of goodies-the Government's decision to give back some of the money from taxes it has been ripping off us for the past seven years. misled into thinking that it is as much as they deserve.
The sole positive point in the Budget is neither as positive nor as bountiful as the Government says it is. The Prime Minister, of course, calls it 'significant'. That is a word he uses a lot and which he makes mean a lot, depending on the time and the circumstances. He even suggested it would compensate working Australians if they forgo wage justice, but as I have said many times the wise person will watch the Prime Minister's hands, not his mouth. These concessions will cost the Government just over $1900m but the Government will claw back more than half of it through higher indirect taxes and charges, and, what is more, the higher taxes and charges are operating now, but the concessions will have to wait until November at the earliest.
One simple statistic exposes the fraudulent nature of the Government's concessions. Taxes this year, at an estimated 25.7 per cent of gross domestic product, are about the same as last year, and last year was the record holder. How can the public respect a Government which says that it is concerned about people and yet plays such shabby games with them? A patient would be justified in feeling a bit upset with the medic if, as fast as the blood was being pumped into his arm, it was being syphoned out of his leg. That is how many people will feel when they see through this latest government quackery. That is how many people will react when they find out that the greatest benefit of the tax concessions will go, not to the average family the Treasurer keeps talking about, but to above average salary earners. That is how many pensioners will feel when they find out that the concessions promised to them will favour not the battlers the Prime Minister is suddenly worried about but those who have incomes over and above the pension.
The Government will certainly hear more about these matters as time passes. I want to make only one more point about them. This is an example of how far the Government will go to stir expectations at the lowest possible cost to itself. Even here when it deceives working people who have done this Government no harm, it cannot stop itself from using shonky figures. The average family that the Treasurer talks about will be good and mad when it finds that its living standards will not rise by $16 a week because of these concessions; not at all. As the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) illustrated so graphically last week, the typical married couple, with two children, on average weekly earnings will be only $3.90 better off each week. That will buy them four more loaves of bread each week. Big deal! If

the Treasurer cannot visualise that, let me put it in terms that he will understand. His average family will just have enough extra money to buy two more avocados a week. In other words, that is an improvement of one per cent in that family's spending power in a year when inflation is going up by 11 per cent.

Mr HAYDEN~ No, all the jokes are in front of me, I am afraid. This cynical deceit is better than nothing, except that lifting dishonesty to such a high art form corrupts and corrodes the values of the Government just as it undermines and destroys its credibility. It is not what working families deserve after the treatment handed out to them over the past seven years. After all, these are not gifts that the Government is giving out. Families should not have to be grateful for the grudging concessions the Government has allowed in this Budget. This relief is the people's by right; they have earned it. They have paid record amounts of tax for it. What have they got in return?
