I now turn to that part of the Budget that explains how the Government would manage the economy in the foreseeable future if it had not run away to hide in its funkhole. The economic circumstances, said the Treasurer, 'make the framing of this Budget by far the most difficult since the present Government took office'. What'are those circumstances? The Treasurer stated:
The international recession is now hitting us hard. Our overseas markets have shrunk. Economic growth has faltered. Unemployment is rising. Inflation remains far too high. Interest rates are hurting many of us.
I remind the House that all this is after seven years of the Government which said it would bring us prosperity, defeat inflation and provide jobs for all. What future does the Government see? Under its management inflation will remain high. There will be no growth in farm and nonfarm production. Investment in business and

852 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

especially private home building will decline. Personal spending will stagnate. Employment will not grow. Unemployment will rise. Last year's severe balance of payments problems will continue. A current account deficit of $9 billion will need greater inflows of private capital. This will lead to more pressure on the exchange rate. To attract this capital and to keep some of the $10 billion that came here last year, interest rates will have to stay high. These high interest rates have already done immense harm to many Australians. When rates in the United States of America were rising, the Government told us that ours had to rise too because American rates were going up. Now American rates are declining and ours are set to climb again. I repeat: They are the highest in the world according to the respected Syntec International Pty Ltd publication. They are climbing, in fact, right off the top of the latest Syntec graph.
The Government is banking on a contribution of $1.5 billion from the balance of payments-
partly offset by the projected domestic budget surplus-to keep the growth of money supply down to between 9 per cent and 11 per cent. This, together with problems with the balance of payments, will put even more pressure on interest rates. So will the projected increase in inflation. The effect of those higher interest rates will be to close the circle of disaster. It will discourage the already flattened rate of spending by business and consumers.
Sales of motor vehicles-already on the slide according to Martec Pty Ltd, the motor vehicle industry forecasting organisation-are to be hit even harder by the increases in sales taxes and fuel charges. The one-off lift in spending on public housing will not compensate the building industry for the horrendous recession in home construction. The increase in unemployment is expected by the Government to be between 2 per cent and 3 per cent-another 130,000 to 190,000 on top of the 470,000 already out of work.
This is the litany of woe that we are given after seven years of promises. What does this Budget propose should be done about it? Where are the measures that will turn our economy around or even arrest the slide that the Treasurer foresees? The short answer is: The Budget offers nothing. The Government boasts about its planned domestic surplus as though it were going to help. It will actually make things worse. Here is the economy roaring downhill. Everybody involved is screaming for help. What is the Government going to do? It is going to take out of the economy $230m more than it is putting in. It is unbelievable. It is putting one's foot on the accelerator as one approaches the cliff edge.

That is no blueprint for economic change. It is John Howard's black hole. It must be there somewhere but nobody can see it. The only possible relief is the suspicion that the Treasurer's broad estimates are wrong. Lord knows, so many of his projections are rubbery. In fact, they are downright shonky. His version of the effects of his concessions looks pretty elastic. Much of the wherewithal for these concessions is expected to come from the Government's so-called blitz on tax cheating. Commentators describe this proposition as, at best, dubious and, most likely, ludicrous. We call it ridiculous.
The tax system is riddled 'with rorts. The Government boasts of its eradication plans. But the fact is that the tax cheats have increased 2,500 per cent in the first five years of this Government's stewardship. That is just the rorting that we know about. There would be a lot more if the Labor Party had not made it such an issue. The McCabe-Lafranchi report described some hundreds of cases in Melbourne alone. The New South Wales Government is said to have a version of its own which is as shocking as the Victorian report. The Australian Taxation Office is deeply concerned at the spread of devices to cheat on sales taxes.
The Liberal Party in the Cook electorate had its tax avoidance scheme and, for all we know, it is being repeated by the Liberals in other electorates in the Treasurer's bailiwick of New South Wales. The National Party in Queensland was touting its own tax avoidance scheme only a few days ago. People named in the McCabe-Lafranchi report represent this Government on various boards and commissions and within the ranks of its own party. Half the members opposite are determined to stop the Government from doing a proper eradication job, The Premier and the Liberal Party in Western Australia are helping them. Four years ago, the Treasurer promised measures to eradicate evasion through cash payments, especially in the building industry. The measures certainly have appeared for the first time with this Budget. The only reason they are there is because the Government could no longer ignore community pressure and disgust.
Mr Donald Cameron-Are you against those cash payments?
Mr HAYDEN-No, but it does not take me four years to make up my mind, Four years ago the Treasurer announced that he would introduce measures to prevent international tax avoidance through tax havens. The havens remain virtually untouched, safe as ever, four years later. Even the new housing proposals in this Budget seem, on the

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 853

face of it, to provide opportunities to rort the system. How many working Australians who are paying the highest personal taxes in history take the Government at its word? How can they trust the Government to stamp out the tax cheats when the tax cheats are running around its feet like cockroaches? With this record the Government cannot blame us for being quite sceptical.
So how will the Government pay for its concessions before it rips them back? The Budget estimates are so rubbery that they bounce all over the place like a cheque from Keith Compton Gale who is well known to the Prime Minister.
Spending on unemployment benefits is probably understated by $300m. The home interest rate rebate will probably cost $70m more than the estimate. The revenue target of $615m through penalties on late payment of tax could be out by as much as $400m. The health rebate will probable cost about $150m more than is estimated. The revenue from the tobacco excise is said to be understated by about $65m, according to newspaper reports only this morning. These estimates are so thin the Budget will fall apart within months. No wonder the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations (Mr Macphee) does not want to be associated with the Treasury and no wonder the Treasury does not want to be caught too near the Government's estimates. They are so dubious that this Budget is just about irrelevant to any sensible discussion about the state of the economy. This is tragic because the state of the economy-present and potential-is disastrous.
Let me deal with some positive propositions we have. Our plan of rescue in the short term would aim at a prudent level of expansion in the economy. This would be appropriate to the circumstances and genuinely fair to those who will do the work that pulls the economy around. A domestic deficit of $1 billion to $1.5 billion-equivalent to much less than one per cent of gross domestic product-would have major multiplier effects throughout the economy. Within these limits, Labor's program has been carefully assessed for its maximum impact on getting the economy moving and employment rising. It would include an employment policy that would directly boost job opportunities by about 150,000 in the public and private sectors; increased spending on capital works on overdue social and economic infrastructure programs in co-operation with state and local government; a boost to the public housing rental stock and associated community services; and genuine tax cuts and associated measures aimed at making up the decline in family living standards. A Budget deficit could be financed by a net addition to money supply financed by Treasury bills

bought by the Reserve Bank of Australia. We would aim at limiting growth in money supply to between II per cent and 13 per cent. This would be firm enough. It would also accommodate faster growth and ease pressure on interest rates. In association with our 'prices and incomes policy it would do so without adding to inflation.
We totally disagree with the Government's policy of keeping interest rates up to attract capital inflow with which to finance the current account deficit. This pressure on interest rates should be eased in two ways. First, the dollar should be devalued by five per cent-a figure which the markets think is sensible and rational. Second, we should have an overseas borrowing program to maintain adequate foreign reserves. In this way we would establish a floor of confidence under the exchange rate. These borrowings would be temporary, repayments being made as the trade sector gains ground. The employment program is designed to create directly about 150,000 job opportunities. More jobs would result from the increased spending power from these otherwise unemployed people and in the industries supplying the materials for the projects on which they have been employed. Our public housing program alone would involve spending an extra $170m in 1982-83, bringing the base level of Commonwealth funding up to $500m. Every $IOOm spent on the building industry creates 5,000 jobs. Accordingly, our proposal will provide 8,500 new jobs in that area alone. More jobs would come from Labor's proposals for bridging the deposit gap for first home buyers and boosting the flow of affordable housing finance.
Our proposal for a genuine increase in family living standards through tax reform, cheaper health insurance and a new improved retirement scheme would directly bolster consumer spending. The details of these programs will be issued in the near future by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition, the honourable member for Kingsford Smith (Mr Lionel Bowen), the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke), the honourable member for Gellibrand, the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) and other colleagues here and in the other place-the most active and creative front bench this Parliament has seen in years. I notice the Vice-President of the Executive Council (Sir James Killen) applauding. He is the first Minister for nothing we have had in the Parliament. Our proposals envisage non-farm' gross domestic product growing at a rate of 4 per cent in 1983. The confidence that they would stimulate plus our medium term measures for industrial renewal are aimed at putting Australia back on to a

854 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

sustainable annual growth of about 5 per cent per year.
It has to be said that the task of halving unemployment to 4 per cent would be a long and hard one, taking several years. Even so, a growing economy would help lower the cost of expansionary activities. Halving unemployment in that period, for instance, would mean more than $2,000m additional revenue in unemployment benefit saved and taxes paid from the currently unemployed being put back into productive work for Australia. If one thing is needed in this country today it is to put Australia back to work the way it used to work. One thing is certain: We have to get out of this rut into which the Government has dumped the nation.
Mr Anthony---That is what Joh Petersen says.
Mr HAYDEN-The crepuscular mentality of the Deputy Prime Minister is brought to bear again. The no-growth policies of the Government have cost us dearly. In the 10 years to 1975 the annual growth rate averaged 5 per cent. If that had continued since then gross domestic product this year would be $200,000m instead of $160,000m. In other words, 20 per cent of our potential wealth is lying idle this year as a direct consequence of a succession of Government policies. That is a cost equivalent of $50 a head per week or $170 per famiiy per week. No wonder Australia has shot down the World Bank rankings of per capita income. We were the ninth richest nation in the world seven years ago. We are the sixteenth richest now and falling, thanks to the geniuses opposite. The World Bank's rankings, by the way, are dominated by nations with democratic socialist forms of governmentgovernments, that is, which recognise the importance of prices and incomes policies as crucial elements of economic management. West Germany and Austria are obvious examples, both incidentally enjoying much lower unemployment and inflation than we have. Despite the mess created by its inflation first strategy, the Government's deliberate actions have put 2 per cent on inflation each year. Imagine a government that claims to be fighting inflation first adopting policies that increase costs and impose an extra 2 per cent on inflation each year! By these actions I mean petrol and sales taxes, higher excise, more expensive health insurance and the crunch imposed on statutory authorities and State government funds.
The prices and incomes arrangement we have with the trade union movement, as it unfolds through further negotiation, will tolerate none of these stupid contradictions. Its objective is that

growth in wages will not exceed growth in productivity plus inflation and, indeed, will be kept within guidelines at all times dictated by sensible economic management. In return, rational programs will improve the social wage and set up mechanisms to reduce price pressures such as a pricing authority and an effective Trade Practices Commission. We will reduce our over-reliance on inflationary and regressive indirect taxes and government charging practices. Our monetary
and fiscal policies will be meshed into our prices and incomes policy. By the end of 1983 such integrated action would have inflation down to 8 per cent and falling. It would be consistent with, and helpful to, our objective of bringing down the highest interest rates in the world.
I have spent some time detailing this short term rescue plan for a good reason. If it helps the Government out of its present paralysis, it is welcome to try it. This is no time to be exclusiveeven with fools. The country is in too great a mess. If the Government follows the line that Labor has offered tonight it will have our co-operation. But Australia faces longer term developments which, in my view, are not helped by the form that these Budgets take. Every year at this time we are so busy fighting over the details of one year's program that we do not have the time to consider what is ahead of us. The year 2000 will be on us so quickly that none of us can say that we are ready for it. We have to understand what this lack of vision must do to those we seek to lead and to the young people growing up after them. We are beyond the tinkering and fine tuning that go on in our Budget deliberations. Australia needs a new outlook altogether, a search over the horizon for the ideas that will prepare us for a complicated future. This means that we should be approaching present policies and present ways of doing things with fresh and sceptical eyes and a start must be made now. We should, for instance, have a clearer, more objective vision of our place and role in the area in which we live. The careful bromide that we got in the Harries report on Australia and the Third World will not do. I have in mind a much tougher minded and more realistic view-a regional strategy.
It is a fact that the world's most impressive growth rates are taking place in the south, east, South East Asia and the Pacific region. These are areas in which we are still insular aliens and often resented for it. If we want to survive as a community with high living standards, we will have to hook into the economic developments taking place there. These are potential markets not only for our meat and other high cost food where markets with our traditional trading partners are

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 855

declining, but also for our processed metals and manufactures. We have to work out much more carefully than we have up to now the forms in which we relate to these areas economically as well as politically. Closer economic relations with south, east, South East Asian and Pacific countries would bring immense opportunity for our growth as well as theirs. They would also force less structural change on our economy than growth based more narrowly on the export of energy minerals.
This connects with another long range develop-
ment we have only played with so far. I refer to structural change in our manufacturing industry. Industry cannot sustain us using its traditional modes and patterns of production. The Labor Party has been carefully drawing up a framework of policy planning that aims at a much more enabling role for industry by the Government. It should involve a more active Department of Industry and Commerce which would have direct planning links with strategic corporations, for example, and would key them into the planning councils that we will set up. If for no other reason than our proposed regional strategy, protection must be lowered progressively. But we are totally at one with the authoritative view of the Crawford Study Group on Structural Adjustment about this; that is, it cannot be done while unemployment remains a problem. This leads to another long range development for which Australia is unprepared. It cannot be done without effective manpower and capital policies. We argue a great deal over the pros and cons of keeping young people at their studies, to keep them off a chronically stagnant job market. But now is the time we should be planning changes to the structure of these studies so that our young people are educated and prepared for a future that we older people can scarcely imagine.
The Commonwealth and the States argue constantly over who is responsible for further education at a time when 70 per cent of the world's industrial robots are at work 24 hours a day in Japan. We argue about how best we can get industry moving again in the short term, presumably along its traditional path, producing its traditional goods in its traditional ways. But now is the time when we should be preparing the mechanisms which will stimulate research and development, which in turn will fit our technological base for a future which already exists in Europe and Japan. Ideological blinkers ought not to prevent a creative and productive partnership in this area between the public and private sectors.
We have a system of sharing power between the national and State governments that has not

really changed for a generation or more and which is bogged down in the traditional political haggling. In the meantime, our infrastructure is seizing up. It is a victim of the political system, choking like a dinosaur. This is one of the reasons why I have convened a meeting in a few weeks with all Labor Premiers and Labor leaders. In the new year we will have more Premiers at Labor's meeting than the Liberals will be able to claim at any convention of theirs. A start has to be made on a profound problem. If the leadership is not forthcoming from this Government, it will come from us. The Government haggles over details of social and economic programs in a political structure which ignores the new pressures that are already emerging. I am thinking of a whole range of new challenges to the system: The breakdown of the traditional nuclear family, for instance; the demands for devolution; and the growth of corporations into huge bureaucracies, with the familiar bureaucratic inertia and the familiar bureaucratic grasp for power.
While these new ways are ready to swamp us, we are here arguing over whether unions cause inflation and whether young people out of work should or can survive on $40 a week. While the future is rushing towards us the Government is turning away from it. We have only to look at the way in which the Government is organised. The portfolios dealing with science, technology, transport, communications, defence support and the environment are all non-Cabinet portfolios. Their Ministers are outside the centre of power. Their Ministers may well deserve it but the portfolios do not. We will pay dearly one day for this refusal to adapt. More importantly, those who come after us will have to pay for this lack of foresight and leadership.
I have one final point to make. It should be kept in mind while we are discussing the present and future of Australians in this debate.
Mr ColemanYour followers are asleep.
Mr HAYDEN-One scarcely notices the difference. There is a calculating air about our societies these days which is poisoning our country. We see it in the proliferation of tax rorting. People involved in it are honoured and kept on as Government representatives on public bodies. People responsible for it live lives of luxury, untouched by the law, and even lionised as rogue heroes. Occasionally, these rogue heroes get what they deserve. Even then they attract devoted attention on visiting days from one of the highest public figures in the country. We have a Prime Minister who is notorious for his 'life was not meant to be easy' philosophy, yet we have

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

never had a leader who has pampered himself more unashamedly. In his seven years he has given himself more overseas trips than any.other Prime Minister, more than the last five Prime Ministers combined and more than in the 16 years during which Sir Robert Menzies made his regular pilgrimages to Lords and Buckingham Palace. While companies are laying off workers all over the country, the Prime Minister has built up his personal staff to the biggest in our history. It will cost us nearly $800,000 this year-nearly 50 per cent more than last year. While homeless people were scratching through garbage cans for scraps, the Prime Minister happily spent $18m on the pomp and ceremony of the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting last year-$6m more than was planned.
Meanwhile, working Australians soldier on, paying far more tax than is fair. The number of people living in poverty is as high as in the soup kitchen days of the 1930s. Too many families rely on supermarket specials for their food and welfare agencies for their clothes. Too many Australians have no security in their jobs or accommodation. When they ask for a fairer share of the national cake, they get the tripe kicked out of them for being greedy. Task forces of bureaucrats persecute invalid pensioners and lone parents. God help you if you are a Greek pensioner. The dole that allows young people to live for a week in graceless austerity would not buy a lunch for two at the restaurant across the road from this House.
I am not saying that the community leaders in this Parliament are entirely responsible for this atmosphere. I am not saying that they are entirely to blame for the way these double standards have been encouraged. But the tone of our national behaviour is set here in our national Parliament. When it comes to setting Australians an example of how society should operate fairly, the buck stops here. We cannot expect Australians to live in harmony and co-operation when the nation's affairs are conducted as they are by this confrontationist and unbelievable government. If the normal rules of decency are kicked to bits here in this Parliament by the Government, they will not be respected out in the real world. If confrontation, lies, false promises and abuse are the norm on the part of the Government, they will become the norm in the whole community. Let me remind the House that the greatest and most decent leader this country ever had was Ben Chifley. He once said:
There is a cynical belief in many sections of the community that politicians will promise anything in order to gain power. Unfortunately, some politicians will do so. Yet all of us have a great national responsibility. I've never been able to bring myself-in politics or elsewhere-to make rosy promises

which, in my own heart, I knew were not true or could not be given effect.
That was Ben Chifley who might well have won his last election if he had stooped to the attitudes that prevail at the head of national affairs today. This country is going through tough times. There is no point in denying that. Australians will have to work their way through these troubles. We did it after two devastating world wars and a crippling depression, struggling in co-operation and comradeship for national renewal and reconstruction. We owe it to ourselves to restore a nation whose potential rewards are so enormous and to make sure that these rewards are shared out fairly. We owe it to those Australians who have worked so hard before us and to the Australians who are coming after us. This country was once a prosperous, decent and a fair place in which to live. We can make it so again.
We in the Australian Labor Party believe, as Ben Chifley believed, that this national Parliament should be the place from which the leadership should come. We believe this Parliament has the great responsibility to give a lead to the people by being honest with them and treating them with respect. If the Government trusts the people, the people will trust the Government. This is the way Australia will become once more a fair-minded and civilised society; not by the defeatist and small-minded attitudes exemplified by this Budget, but by national leadership which is progressive, forceful and above all, but beyond this Government, honest. We condemn this destructive Budget.
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond ValleyMinister for Communications) (8.48)-That must surely be the most pathetic reply that has ever been delivered in this Parliament to a Budget Speech. The Australian people were quite entitled to hear from the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) as to what his alternative Budget would be but they have heard absolutely nothing to that effect. They have heard no specific proposals with respect to taxation, social welfare and the way in which an alternative government would raise revenue. We heard nothing more than vague, generalised promises and the most enticing promise of all-that is, that the details would come from the most active and creative front bench that this Parliament has seen in years. The front bench of the Australian Labor Party was so active and creative that it spent most of the parliamentary recess undermining the Leader of the Opposition and trying to get rid of him. What an active and creative front bench indeed it was. We have heard from the Leader of the Opposition that there will be a deficit of between $1 billion and $1.5 billion.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 857

There is plenty of scope to manoeuvre there! He said that the deficit could vary by $500m; a variation of a mere half a billion dollars.
Then we heard the same tired old socialist proposals on job creation, the same proposals that Labor put into practice when it was in government between 1972 and 1975. What was the result of those job creation schemes, as it called them? The result was that employment in this country actually declined. On one of the very few occasions in its entire history did employment in this country actually drop. That was the time when the so-called job creation schemes were in full flight. They were an absolute failure because not only did employment drop but also did unemployment double during the time when the Labor Party implemented those programs. They were a failure then and they would be a failure again.
The Australian people are quite entitled to say to themselves tonight, having heard the speech of the Leader of the Opposition, that the Australian Labor Party has no alternative Budget; it has no alternative proposals; it has nothing that can help this country from economic difficulties and troubles; it has nothing that can give the Australian people any confidence whatsoever.
There are two basic principles upon which this Budget is based. The first principle is to create the right foundations for economic growth and particularly for employment growth. The second principle is to provide reasonable social welfare assistance for those who need that assistance and who are unable fully to care for themselves without some assistance from the Government. Let us look at each of those two principles in turn.
With respect to the first principle, there is no doubt that the Treasurer (Mr Howard) summed up the situation accurately and concisely in the Budget Speech when he said:
The plain, inescapable fact is that Australia has priced herself out of many world markets.
For example, last year wage levels in this country grew by some 3 or 4 per cent more than the rate of inflation. By comparison recent wage settlements in West Germany, the United States and the United Kingdom, countries that rank among our major trading partners, have been around or less than the rates of inflation in those countries.
The Treasurer went on to say:
Unless our relative performance is improved, our living standards will suffer.
That is the way the Treasurer put it, accurately and concisely. There is no doubt that the Treasurer was absolutely correct in making those statements. Australia has priced herself out of some world markets and will continue to do so until we come to realise that the cost of our labour is making the goods that we produce in this

country too expensive for them to be sold on world markets. Other countries have woken up to that fact and have adjusted their policies accordingly.
Let me give a clear example, which I observed
myself in Europe earlier this year. In West Germany, the Government, the trade unions and the employers sat down for the annual round of wage fixing discussions. As they approached the problem logically, they started off with an assessment that the likely rate of inflation for the following 12 months would be about 5 per cent. Accordingly, they agreed upon wage increases of 4-!- per cent-in other words, less than the inflation rate. And how logical that is; how sensible it is: What a contrast it is to the way we seem to approach these problems in Australia where the only guiding principle for those seeking wage increases seems to be to squeeze out of the system whatever they can get and to hell with the consequences. Unfortunately, there are too many people in the community who seem to have no regard for the effect on employment of excessive wage increases. Until they come to have that understanding, we will continue to pay ourselves as a nation more than we deserve and more than we are entitled to. Our goods will continue to be more expensive than the world market will bear. We will not be able to expand our markets overseas and our employment numbers will shrink. Those trying to sell into a market, who demand higher prices than the market will pay, will sell nothing. Their sales will shrink; so will the number of people making goods for which there is no market also shrink.
Let me return to my own experiences of what I observed in Europe earlier this year. I attended an Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development conference of employment Ministers and listened for several days to the experiences from their countries. It became very clear indeed that there are two basic things about employment that we can learn and which we need to understand and put into practice. The first is that employers will employ people if it is profitable to do so. We must therefore make our industry more profitable if we expect industry to employ more people in this country. The whole Budget strategy is designed towards that very end and we must have a total commitment from the community in support of that principle. The second thing that I learned is that employers will employ people if those who are offering themselves for employment have something real and substantial to offer-in other words, if they have the skills, ability and training that the employer needs to do the job. Employers will not employ people as an act of charity or an act of grace; they will employ

858 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

people if the people who are offering for employment have the skills that are needed. It is for that reason that this Budget so rightly places so much emphasis on employment and training programs. The Budget should be supported for this reason alone, even without any more justification.
Under the Budget there will be more scope for unemployed young people to obtain the work experience and job skills that they need to make themselves more employable. Expenditure on work experience, for instance, will be increased by 37 per cent. This will allow for more than 74,000 new work experience places. There will also be substantial increases in Commonwealth Rebate for Apprentice Full-time Training rebates to encourage more apprentices to be employed. In total, $84m will be paid out under the CRAFT scheme. This will provide assistance for 111,000 apprentices this year. I cannot emphasise too much the importance of those work experience and training programs. They are absolutely essential if young people are to be given the skills, training and experience that they need to put them in a position where they will be more employable prospects. The Budget should be supported strongly because of this additional emphasis which is placed this year on training, skills acquisition and work experience.
I said that there were two principles upon which the Budget was based. What I have said so far is directed to that first principle. The second principle is equally important. It is to provide social welfare assistance in many forms for those members of the community who are unable to care for themselves and their families fully. Let there be no mistake about it; the Labor Party has no exclusive franchise on social welfare. The present Government, since it came to office, has been generous in its social welfare assistance, and rightly so. Its generosity and understanding of the needs of less fortunate members of the community is continued in this Budget as it has been so amply demonstrated in its previous Budgets. In the first place there is a substantial increase in overall social welfare payments of 15.4 per cent: It is not widely enough understood in the community that the Government is now committed to spending, on social welfare alone, over $13 billion this year. Secondly, there are increases in family allowances. These increases, in effect, amount to 50 per cent in the case of the allowances for the first and second children.
Thirdly there is a new form of assistance to help families that are on very low incomes. A tax free family income supplement of $10 a week will be paid for each dependent child of a low income family not in receipt of social security pensions.

Fourthly, there is a very large increase of 28 per cent in all forms of assistance under the Children's Services Program, including increased grants to family day care schemes and a fee rebate for preschool day care for families in need. Furthermore, pensions and other benefits will continue to be indexed so that those in receipt of them will continue to receive protection against increased costs. There is a further increase in assistance for pensioners who live in rented accommodation. There is a whole range of further and additional forms of assistance for those on unemployment and sickness benefits, for those who are disabled and who need particular help, and for those who are aged or disabled and who need special assistance for accommodation. The details are there for all to see. The Australian community need be in no doubt about one very basic thing, that is, that this Government is a government of compassion, a government that recognises that there are people in ~he community who need help and assistance, and a government that is prepared to provide that assistance and help and to do so in very large and generous measure.
This Budget, therefore, stands on its own merits on those two principles-economic progress and social concern. What is the alternative, such as it is? There is nothing, not a syllable, in the Australian Labor Party's alternative proposals we have heard tonight or read anywhere else that would contribute to solving Australia's economic problems. Those problems would be exacerbated by the Labor Party's alternative, which is the
same old socialist program-more government expenditure, a bigger deficit, the higher interest rates that come in its wake, higher taxes, more types of taxes, and more government control through more government bodies. At least there is one thing we can be clear about, and that is that the Labor Party is proposing an enormous deficit which would bankrupt this country.
Let us look in a little more detail at what the ALP proposes by way of a deficit. We have heard, for instance, the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) advocating a deficit of 4 per cent of gross domestic product. That is about $6.5 billion.
His leader tonight was at least a bit more modest. The deficit he proposed was between $1 billion and $1.5 billion. Honourable members can take their pick. There is plenty of room to manoeuvre. The fact is, and it is a critical fact, that the consequence of that would be devastating so far as inflation is concerned. Additional pressure would be placed on the capital market with a deficit of anything like that size. Inflation would be fuelled and interest rates would undoubtedly increase. The Labor Party has no policy on interest rates, it

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 859

seems, other than a policy that would inevitably increase them. Not only does the Opposition not know where it stands on the deficit but it does not seem to understand the inflationary impact that a larger domestic deficit would have on the economy as a whole. On the other hand, the Government has decided that a modest domestic Budget surplus of $230m would strike the right balance between a positive response to the economic diffi-
culties facing Australia and a responsible approach to economic management.
The Government has also proposed substantial and responsible reductions in personal tax. Let there be no mistake about it, Labor could not possibly finance the expansion of government that inevitably comes in the wake of a socialist government and at the same time reduce taxes. No socialist government has ever been able to do that and none will. A socialist government taxes by stealth, it taxes by inflation, and that is what a Labor Government would do in this country. The runs are on the board for the present Govern-
ment. The cuts in the present Budget are substantial and they are of major significance. They include new tax rebates for home mortgage interest rates over 10 per cent, the large increase in family allowances, and the $200 per annum increase in the spouse rebate where there is a dependent child. All of these will work in combination to make the tax burden on the vast majority of Australian families a much lighter one.
In addition, this Budget contains specific and substantial improvements to the taxation situ-
ation. These taxation and related reforms come on top of the other taxation reforms that have been implemented during the life of this Government, especially the three-step taxation scale. All of those tax reforms would be at risk under a Labor Government.
There can be no tax relief under a Labor
government; it is a contradiction in terms. A Labor government would expand government because it believes that a government can do things better than free individuals can do them. The people will pay for it through higher taxes. Is there any doubt at all that the Labor Party would expand the role of government? There should be no doubt about it. It did exactly that when it was in office and it would do it again if it had half a chance. If honourable members look at what happened at the Labor Party's Federal Conference which has just concluded they will see that the party proposes to create or expand at least 72 new government or semi-government bodies. Among those proposals are the National Energy Commission, the Hydrocarbon Exploration Government Program and the Australian Hydrocarbon

Corporation. We will even be taxed to pay for the national kangaroo monitoring study group. All of that will come on top of the inevitable expansion of government under any socialist government.
There are many proposals approved in the Budget context which are important to Australians and which are often taken for granted. Many of them are in my portfolio. I mention the Remote Area Television Scheme and the extension of the inland radio services to improve communications over very large distances in the open spaces of this country. I mention the expansion of the Australian Broadcasting Commission frequency modulation network and the establishment of a new spirit in the ABC itself. I mention very briefly the expansion of the Special Broadcasting Service and the multicultural television service that is provided by the SBS. I remind Australian people, especially those in ethnic communities, that at the recent Labor Party conference a proposal to abolish multicultural television was very narrowly defeated. These are just examples from my portfolio which show that, in the field of communications, this Government is committed to improving and expanding communications in this country. But far more important than the matters in my portfolio are those matters in the economy as a whole.
The people of this country can be confident that under the present Government our economic problems will be tackled and will be solved. The alternative is disastrous. The alternative of a socialist government in this country would simply spell ruin for the country.

Brown)-who I think is basically more intelligent than he appeared to be tonight but who just acts like a fool and an idiot. That is a part of the reason why not only this country but also democracies in general are in significant trouble. My second pet hate concerns some sections of the media. I will mention that matter in a few minutes.
I wish to make my point very clear. The general proposition that has been put by the media and by a significant number of other people is that this Budget is an election Budget and that the Government is keen on holding an election and has an excellent chance of winning an election during the

860 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

next four months or whatever period is being picked out this year. I think it is extremely difficult, if not impossible, for any democractic government to win anywhere at the present time. I do not care what honourable members opposite say about that. At this point I am not being particularly political. I think it is extremely difficult for a democratic government-it does not matter whether it is the West German Government or a conservative government, such as the Fraser Government or for that matter the Reagan Administration-to win at the present time. I hope that some honourable members opposite who occasionally think about these things are aware of that.
Recently the Australian Financial Review published two or three articles by Professor Groenewegen of Sydney University which dealt with two aspects of taxation reform and the pros and cons of indirect taxation versus direct taxation. I would like to quote somebody who is usually right-me. In a letter I wrote to the Australian Financial Review which was published on 24 June 1982-1 do not think it is a particularly political letter; it is a letter that politicians ought to think about-I said:
Professor Groenewegen's two articles on taxation reform . . . were depressing in their fair summary of the lack of intelligent discussion among politicians on this subject.
I give one partial defence only on behalf of the politicians. Groenewegen himself discussed the pros and cons of different types of taxation without coming to any conclusions.
I applaud this demonstration that very few issues are black and white, but the media do not allow politicians to adopt this rational attitude. Anybody who has doubts or can see two sides of an argument is attacked as vacillating, lacking leadership et cetera.
Even intelligent politicians, e.g. Bill Hayden, who can see both sides of most arguments are forced to behave like shrewd lawyers with a brief.
We had a perfect example of this a few minutes ago, not by the Leader of the Opposition but by the Minister for Communications who followed him in the debate. I continued:
No wonder the (at least temporarily) successful politicians are of the Menzies, Whit lam, Fraser variety, completely convinced that their line at any particular time is the only correct one.
Surely, 'leadership' in an educated democratic society should not exclude rational discussion and the realisation that not all problems are 'soluble'.
Soluble problems occur on the puzzle pages of newspapers. Problems with which we deal, problems in society, are not necessarily easily soluble or soluble at all. My letter continued:
The onus is on the media to give more publicity to discussion and less to confrontation.

Favourable publicity for demonstrations promoting slogans e.g. 'Peace', 'Right to Life', 'No Dams' is in a sense antidemocratic as it encourages those who treat the public as being most simplistic.
I hope that deep down many of the people even on the opposite side of the political fence will agree with me that we are not giving good service to the Australian people and we are not giving good service to democratic societies by behaving in this ridiculous fashion. A few minutes ago the Minister for Communications was behaving quite typically of the worst of these politicians. For example, he absolutely condemned a high deficit. Quite apart from the fact that he finished up by making all kinds of statements which were obviously going to cost lots of money in his own portfolio, he went on to condemn high deficits out of hand. Those honourable members who have been to Japan will know that that country's deficit is approximately 6 per cent of gross domestic product. It is a huge deficit. It is approximately 40 per cent of Japan's Budget. Yet, the inflation rate in Japan is running at 3 per cent compared with about II per cent here. So let us be quite clear: There is no clear cut relationship between the extent of the deficit and inflation. It is quite silly for some people to make absolute statements along those lines.
My own view is that the Government has probably tried to bring in a Budget which it considered at the time it was introduced into this House to be a possible election winner. I do not think it is; I hope it is not. As I said earlier, I do not think the contents of the Budget are particularly relevant or are likely to be relevant during an election campaign; it is the perception that the people out there have of what is happening to their country and that is partly conditioned by the promises of people when in Opposition. All I can say is as far as this Government is concerned is that when it goes down in defeat, no matter when the next election is held, it will deserve to be in that position because when in Opposition it made all kinds of promises. When in Government the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) spoke about life not being meant to be easy. When he was in Opposition he, of course, promised that life could be easy, that it was easy to give all kinds of benefits and do all kinds of things without increasing taxation. We know that is not possible. There is a trade-off in every case when we do things for the population at large.
I think it is important that we, as responsible parliamentarians, continuously point out that there are significant basic differences between the political parties because of the people who are our supporters. The people who support us on our

Appropriation Bill (No. I)
