side are the battlers~the people who are not terribly well off in society. Therefore, we stand for a more equitable distribution of income, for a fairer type of society, for better opportunities and for equal opportunities for everybody in society. I suppose that is the basic difference between us and the people opposite. Honourable members opposite believe that the more worthwhile persons in society are the people who have made it and who have somehow reached the position in which they have a relatively high income, have maybe shown more enterprise~certainly they have more money at their disposal~and are keener to protect their assets, and they will continue to try to appeal to those people. I think the Government is unsuccessful at present mainly because of the level of interest rates. Before I refer to the details in the Budget, a former member of this House, Dr Richie Gun, who some honourable members might remember was the honourable member for Kingston and who was unfortunately defeated at the 1975 elections, sent me a cutting from an editorial in the New York Times of 6 August 1982. I think this editorial, which I received today, is relevant to this issue. President Reagan, no matter what one's views about him may be, has a real hide. He introduced and supported a Budget in the United States which will involve a deficit of between $110 billion and $140 billion in the next United States financial year. I do not criticise him for that I do not know what the best answer is to the United States problems~ but this Budget has a much higher deficit than any other Budget ever introduced in the United States. He followed the introduction of that Budget by proposing a constitutional amendment saying that the Government must balance its Budget. What a hide. The New York Times editorial~I will not read the whole article, but I think some is relevant~states:
What a relief! It took Congress most of 200 years to get over the old-fashioned idea that the Constitution should be kept lean and Hexible. Over the years, 9,400 constitutional amendments have been proposed but only 26 succeeded, and one of those repealed another. Now the Senate, at least, has passed the amendment requiring a balanced budget. With timidity conquered, it's high time to harness the Constitution in other ways as well. .
The editorial points out that there ought to be other amendments to the Constitution. It continues:
For instance, all Americans probably agree that every able bodied person deserves a job. The way to achieve that goal is now obvious.
All we have to do is pass a constitutional amendment that everybody gets a job! The editorial further states:

So far, Congress has meekly limited itself to considering legislation. . . How much mightier to add a Full Employment Amendment to the Constitution.
Then there's the problem of the Post Office. There is no governmental responsibility more clearly Federal, and there's not a voter alive who hasn't cursed the slowness of the mails. With constitutional inhibitions swept away, let Congress adopt another amendment. Section 1 would be framed with classic simplicity: The mails shall be delivered on time. .
Congress could prove its concern about high interest rates by adopting an amendment that ordains low interest rates. Exactly how low could be left to the same unspecified authorities who would define which economic forecasts are to be used in calculating the constitutional budget balance.
These paltry suggestions do not exhaust the possibilities, not if Congress now has the courage to make the Constitution truly weighty. There is rich grist for other amendments on national defence, safe streets and the nuclear family, not to mention piety in public places.
I think that is ridiculous behaviour on the part of the United States Government. But that is exactly the way this Government is behaving. Before I look at the Budget in more detail I shall make a general point. We have been told that the average single income family~there is some dispute about the amount involved~will benefit in that next financial year it will pay about $4 a week less in taxation. Of that amount $2.50 will be wiped out two months before the benefit comes in. From the beginning of next month $2.50 will be added to the cost of basic health insurance. That will not leave very much out of that $4 Budget benefit.
I suggest to the House that certain propositions which have been put are untrue. One o(the propositions is that this Budget will help the battlers. It will not help the battlers. A certain amount of redistribution is involved in the Budget. Basically, it is a redistribution from the very high income earner to the high-middle class income earner. Let me illustrate what has been proposed in this Budget. The priority which the Government has accepted is to help those people who are at work, who pay taxes and who receive reasonable wage increases. The Government has decided that it cannot afford to help the jobless or the soon-to-be jobless. I think that is one of the most important points which will help the Opposition win the next election. We will not win just because 8 per cent or 9 per cent of the people are out of work but because a large proportion of the 90 per cent or more who are at work are scared~they are justifiably scared~that they may lose their jobs.
I think basically the Budget is aimed heavily at benefiting the upper-middle class earners and the better off elderly. I am not saying that those benefits should not have been introduced. But there are certain measures in the Budget which assist the poor, the unemployed, the low income family and the hard pressed elderly but they are relatively token measures compared with the

862 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

generosity of the measures which are intended for the upper-middle class swinging voter. The total cost, for example, of the new family income supplement which will pay $10 a week for each child to low income employed families will be $3.Sm this financial year and only $30m in a full financial year. Obviously, this is an excellent proposal. It will apply only to very few people; otherwise it would cost much more than that.
I ask honourable members to compare this $3.5m to be spent this financial year on benefits for people who really need it with the SO per cent increase in family allowances for first and second children which takes effect in November. Family allowances are neither taxed nor means tested so they go to the wealthy as well as to the poor. That increase will cost $2S0m this financial year and $37Sm in a full year. We are paying $3.Sm this year to the people who are really poor~that is where the money is really required for the kids~ yet we are paying $2S0m, about 70 times as much, to people who, in many instances, do not need an extra $I.SO a week in their non-means-tested familyallowances.
Similarly there is no housing benefit at all, for those people in the community who rent accommodation apart from the odd pensioner whose benefit has been increased from $8 to $10 a week. This is a minor benefit compared with the benefits for people who have a $60,000 mortgage on a house. As we all know, it has been declared by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) that people will be entitled to switch their mortgages around and, therefore, have subsidies for things apart from housing.
Indirect taxation has already increased. It increased on the day the Budget was brought down, 17 August, whilst the other benefits will not take effect for some considerable time. The benefit paid to unemployed people under the age of 18 will go up by $4 from $36 to $40 a week. This is an II per cent increase after seven years of no increase at all. Similarly, the increase for the single unemployed does not get them up to the sort of benefit that they ought to be getting or need. The people who are getting the biggest proportional tax savings are those earning between $340 and $460 a week. When one considers that average weekly earnings are now just under $330 a week and that roughly 60 per cent of people earn less than the average it is clear that the tax cut was hardly aimed at providing the greatest benefit for the battler. As I said earlier, in fact the Budget redistributes income from high income earners to upper middle income earners.
In addition to the matter I have just dealt with I would like to refer to two other aspects of the

Budget, one of which is the question of the economy not being properly controlled. We do not have accurate statistics. It is very difficult to say exactly what is happening. To some extent the Treasury is failing. One day last week the overnight interest rate for money was at the rate of 100 per cent per annum. Admittedly this was for only one night but people who invested money for that night~from three o'clock one afternoon to lunch time the next day~were paid at a rate equal to 100 per cent per annum because there was such a shortage of money. There has been very little discussion on this topic. My interpretation about why there was such a shortage of money~whilst a number of factors may have been involved~is that people are withdrawing
money from Australia at present because of a continuing devaluation of our currency.
In the Government's control of the economy it has to try to establish more facts. It has failed to establish many facts. Dr McCawley, from the Research School of Pacific Studies at the Australian
National University, has suggested in a note to me that the facts are very vague. If honourable members look at the last issue of the quarterly estimates of national income and expenditure published on 17 August~last week~they will see that the measured growth rates of GDP varied
dramatically depending on where one places the statistical discrepancy. That is clearly stated at the beginning of that monograph. A completely different estimate is given depending on whether one calculates the statistical discrepancy on the expenditure side or on the income side of the account. If one looks at it from the expenditure based proposition it is quite clear that GDP changes are terrible at present whilst if one looks at it from the income based proposition then things may be improving.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)~ Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr SHIPTON (Higgins) (9.27)~The reply of the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) to the Budget tonight offered Australia no specific alternative Budget. It was a bits and pieces reply; it was an exaggeration and an overstatement. The leader was a prophet of doom and disaster. He talked of panic, shakiness and shonkiness. It was a negative reply and it offered no solutions for Australia's economic difficulties. The Leader of the Opposition continually talked the economy down. His speech lacked professionalism. Honourable members opposite know I am telling the truth. It was a very disappointing effort from a former Treasurer and a man who aspires to be Prime Minister of Australia.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 863

Let us look at the statement of the Leader of the Opposition. His Budget strategy boils down to doubling the Government's deficit, the increase to be fully financed by printing money. He talks of using the Treasury Bills and the money supply combination to do this. That is in his speech. He talks of a net addition to the money supply. We will be back to the Whitlam years. The economic package of the Leader of the Opposition is a prescription for more, not less, inflation; for less, not more, growth; for more, not less, unemployment; and for higher, rather than lower, interest rates. The limited budget figuring in the Leader of the Opposition's speech suggests an additional $3 billion in expenditure, half of which would have to be financed by an increase in personal taxes which, combined with the Australian Labor Party's desire to shift from direct to indirect taxation, implies a significant increase in the income tax take. Under the Hayden proposals income tax would need to go up substantially. Personal taxes would rise. The criticisms by the Leader of the Opposition of the Budget figuring of the Government are unsubstantiated and without foundation. The attack he made on the Government's record on tax avoidance is unfair. No government has done more than the present Government to stamp out tax avoidance. The Labor Party, when it had its opportunity in government, did nothing.
Let us look at some of the Leader of the Opposition's statements. He said that unemployment benefits were understated in the Budget by $300m. That assumes something like an additional 130,000 recipients of unemployment benefit but no evidence was presented by the Leader of the Opposition for this estimate. The Government's estimate of 395,000 unemployed has stood the test of public debate. The Leader of the Opposition said that the home interest rebate would cost an additional $70m. He provided no evidence for this estimate and his assumption implies close to a 50 per cent understatement on our estimate. The Leader of the Opposition said that $615m through penalties on late payment was underestimated by $400m. But the Budget estimate is based on over $2.1 billion income tax overdue as at 30 June 1982.
Mr Young-Come on, Roger, shake it up.
Mr SHIPTON-The honourable member for Port Adelaide should listen; he might learn something if he listens. His leaderI think he thinks he is his leader-said that the health rebate was underestimated by $150m. There is no basis in the Leader of the opposition's speech for that estimate. I think that one of the most interesting things to come out of the Leader of the Opposition's speech is a comparison of labour costs and

productivity. The Leader of the Opposition attempted in his speech to make much of the point that since 1975-76 real labour costs grew by II per cent and productivity grew by 12 per cent. He tried to argue that real labour costs are not really a problem. However, if you include the Labor years in government and take 1972-73 as a base-that is what the Leader of the Opposition failed to do-real labour costs grew by 26 per cent and productivity grew by 19 per cent. Clearly, the labor cost increases were concentrated in the Labor Government years and the productivity increases were concentrated in the Liberal-National Country Party coalition years.
The Budget presented by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) a week ago is a realistic Budget for the times. The Treasurer points out that the international recession is now hitting us hard, our overseas markets have shrunk, economic growth has faltered, unemployment is rising and inflation remains far too high. Interest rates are hurting many of us. The Government is to be congratulated for features of the Budget such as financial relief for families in the form of increased family allowance, personal tax reductions, interest relief for people paying off their homes and the introduction of a new family income supplement to help certain low income families. Others who benefit from the Budget are pensioners and unemployed people.
I particularly welcome what the Government has done to provide personal taxation relief and I support the increases in indirect taxation and the slight broadening of the indirect tax base. These tax measures will provide assistance in the short term in the financial year of the Budget. But in the long run, for the years ahead, changing tax scales in one Budget is only tinkering with the controls of an existing tax system. We still need complete reform of the tax system. This is a matter of urgency. The burden of direct tax through income tax at the end of the Budget year will still be too great. This Budget makes a start in this direction but we must relieve the long term tax burden of the pay-as-you-earn wage and salary earner. A new, more equitable tax system which removes the incentive to avoid tax and which encourages people to work hard is the only long term answer for personal taxation in this country. The more we put off this reform the greater the problem becomes.
I particularly welcome the start of taxation relief, made in the Budget, with the $1,000 rebate on dividend income received by individuals. This move should encourage individual investors to invest in Australian companies listed on stock

864 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

exchanges and give individuals confidence to invest in shares in Australian companies without the discrimination they previously suffered through double taxation. This rebate is a start in the right direction of encouraging individual Australians to participate in the free enterprise system by the ownership of shares in Australian corporations. I look forward to the amount of the rebate being increased in future Budgets. Similarly, the announcement of the employee share acquisition schemes in the Budget which will give employees an incentive to invest in companies which employ them is welcome.
I turn now to indirect taxes. The Government's widening and deepening of the indirect tax base through sales tax is welcome and sensible. However, I think we lost an opportunity in the Budget to impose a retail sales tax to shift the burden of collection to the final point of sale. It is unfair and inequitable that it is the responsibility of the wholesaler and the manufacturer to act as a tax collector for the Government. The point at which the sales tax should be paid, I believe, is the retail level. Wholesalers and manufacturers have to pay the existing sales tax within 21 days but they themselves do not get paid for 60, 90 or 120 days and have to finance these payments of tax to the Commissioner of Taxation. This adds to their costs and is in itself inflationary. It produces also a distorted effect on the manufacturing sector where manufacturers are now producing and dealing in goods that attract the lower rates of sales tax because the burden of financing those
payments in payment of the sales tax is less. I believe that in return for the tax relief in this Budget retailers would have accepted the equity of a retail point of sale tax. I hope that in the future that may come about.
From a strict monetarist point of view the Budget could be criticised for being too expansionary and not having a smaller deficit. Strict monetarists would say that is too generous in terms of welfare payments and tax concessions. However, it does hit the right note of expansion needed at this time in the economy. The Government-as evidenced by the size of the deficit-is helping to alleviate areas of hardship by making money available in the economy through increased unemployment benefit payments and welfare spending. The Australian economy is, and has been, out of kilter with the other major Western economies. Unemployment and interest rates in Australia have lagged behind those in overseas countries. In the past unemployment in Australia and certainly deficits have been lower than in other major Western economies. There is now a potential for our unemployment

rate and level of inflation to increase and current interest rate levels to remain where they are while our trading partners' levels go in the other direction. Many economists and political commentators hold this view. However, time alone will tell. I consider that this Budget could turn out to be one of the most innovative of any in the Western world today.
Monetarist theory is to control the economy by contracting the money supply to defeat inflation and then to emerge with a healthy economy. The United Kingdom with monetarist budget policies has got the inflation rate down and is starting to get modest growth but at the price of very high unemployment. In the United States of America President Reagan has now increased taxes and reversed his previous economic strategies because the United States' deficit was so high. This Australian Budget sets out to alleviate problems in the short term. This is no doubt predicated on the hope of an upturn in the United States' economy. Whilst this has not yet occurred there is a definite trend in the United States to lower interest rates caused by a fall-off in consumer demand. We have seen in the last few days Wall Street stock exchange rallies. I believe that if this stock market trend continues it could, in fact, create the right psychological climate of confidence needed in the United States to create a consumer-led demand investment recovery. Let us all hope that this
occurs. If it does-there is hope that it will-this Budget in Australia is just right for the times. The only real risk is that it could be said to be too inflationary. However, if wage demands reach reasonable proportions-I believe that potential exists-I do not consider that the increases in indirect tax in this Budget will add substantially to inflation. It needs to be said that Australia's deficit is not high by world standards. It is a quarter to
one-third of that of the United States, on a comparable basis, and less than that of Canada, the United Kingdom, Germany and Japan. The Australian Government, which has generally been applying monetarist economic policies, has not abandoned them in this mildly expansionary Budget, whose effect is to increase transfer payments and the potential amount of disposable income available to consumers in difficult economic times.
Welfare recipients and taxpayers alike benefit by increased disposable income. There is a risk that such increases to individuals will flow into savings rather than into the economy through purchases. However, the Budget does create its own climate of confidence, which should give those with additional disposable income confidence to invest in Australia. I believe that we will

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 865

see these transfer payments and this increased disposable income spent in the economy.
Let us look now at the subject of wage restraint. Unreasonable wage demands and demands for shorter working hours are totally irresponsible in the current economic climate. Union demands are eroding the standard of living of all Australians. In a recent report to my electorate, I said: 'Tax cuts are a vital element of wages policy and must be accepted by the Australian Council of Trade Unions as a trade-off for wage restraint'. Wage restraint is a key to the success of the Budget. The current economic climate and state of the economy is starting to inhibit some union claims. The regrettable lay-offs and retrenchments are having an effect on some claims. However, it is now, as never before, time for the ACTU to be responsible to all Australians. The Budget's generous tax reductions and concessions, increased family allowances, spouse rebates and home loan interest rebate must be accepted by the union movement in lieu of wage increases, and wage demands must be reduced accordingly. The Budget forms, and is the basis of, a claim for wage restraint by the Government. The union movement must take this into account and moderate its demands.
The Labor Party's response to the Budget has really been a non-event. I have the feeling that it caught the Opposition completely by surprise. The Australian Labor Party really believes that it is a good Budget, but for it to say so would incur the wrath of the unions. The Labor Party cannot upset the unions because the union movement and the Labor Party are intertwined. The Labor Party in this House is the parliamentary wing of the union movement. Because the Budget is contrary to trade union policy and the ALP is controlled by the unions, the Labor Party must disagree totally with the Budget. It is a sad day that, because of this, the ALP is locked into the adversary system and locked into a knee jerk automatic rejection of the Budget.
The ALP's proposed pact with the ACTU is a sham. Under it a Labor government would support full wage indexation, tax relief and higher welfare spending in return for wage moderation for the unions. This pact would be suicide for Australia. It would create enormous inflation but, above all, it would put up labour costs to such an extent that today's unemployment figures would seem minuscule.
Let us look to the future. In supporting the Budget, I must say that I consider that all governments must look to the longer term future of Australia. At a time when the Western economies are contracting, we need to think harder than ever

of the future of our way of life. The prediction that unemployment will rise, with the high levels of unemployment already existing in Western economies, is a very serious problem facing all Western societies generally. Quite rightly society today has given individuals high expectations but regrettably it can no longer fulfill many of them.
Unemployment is a major sociological and political problem and something that governments, employers and unions alike must tackle. Unions are primarily concerned with those who have jobs and seem to care very little about those who have not. For unions to pursue shorter hours and wage claims at this time is the height of selfishness. The current shorter working hours claims by blue collar workers in Queensland can only result in more people being thrown out of work in that State. I know of no other Western economy in which shorter working hours are being pursued. What is it about Australians that causes them to do this to themselves? It is time that the unions put Australia first and put a shoulder behind the wheel to get this country on its feet. Unions, like the rest of us, should look at the strength of our economy and build on those strengths. They should look at new options for job creation and investment rather than destroying what we have.
In Australia today we need a new and innovative major research program to be taken into the problems facing society-to examine particularly the job creation potential of new technology and of changing lifestyles. Such a research program would be best carried out by a new independent think-tank or institute using the best brains available from government, universities and the private sector. It should be in part government funded and its program should study technological, sociological and economic trends. The special Bicentenary Road Development Program in the Budget establishes goals to be achieved by our bicentennial year 1988. We all need to be thinking of the positive things we can do to improve our society for a better Australia in 1988. The 1988 bicentennial year provides us with a bench-mark to consider the nature of Australian society for the rest of this century.
We need to examine what is happening to our social and economic system. So few of us seem to be looking to the future. It was refreshing to see Mr B. A. Santamaria's thoughtful forward looking views in today's Australian. He refers to the contemporary historian Fernand Braudel's analysis of society. Braudel refers to a crisis of structures. He and Mr Santamaria believe that the size of economic units, whether companies or unions, and the size of governments and cities create insoluble problems. Braudel says that our social

866 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

and economic system will have to be reshaped. I do not know whether that is quite right, but what I do know and what is certain is that society is changing and will have to be reshaped. It is our challenge to see that it is reshaped in the right direction. Now, more than ever, is the time for us to be thinking ahead and looking where new job opportunities and industries are. When we identify them, they may need particular government support, based on their employment possibilities,
to get them off the ground. I support the Government's Budget.
Mr ARMITAGE (Chifley) (9.47)-Tonight we heard the Leader of the Opposition (Mr
Hayden) make what was probably his finest speech. It was a speech which outlined an alternative policy, a policy to bring Australia back to economic health and back from the precipice it now stands on as a result of seven years of this Government's economic policies. Is it any wonder
that in the latest opinion polls the Leader of the Opposition has an approval rating of 59 per cent compared with the rating for the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) of 41 per cent? Honourable members opposite must realise that we sincerely hope there will be an early election. On the basis of these opinion polls, we would absolutely eat it. The Leader of the Opposition called the Budget a give and take Budget. If one looks closely into it, there is no doubt that that is what it is. It gives with one hand and it takes with the other. It gives income tax cuts on the one hand and increases sales tax and other indirect taxes on the other. It is a dishonest Budget brought in by a dishonest Government.
I want to touch on one or two of the issues which are very important and which affect this Budget. Today we have very high interest rates which are being falsely held up owing to the dependence by the Government on overseas capital to meet the balance of payments deficit. The generallevel of interest rates in Australia today is the highest in the world. Interest rates in Australia are 2t per cent to 3 per cent higher than they are in any other country. The facts are that this country is living beyond its means. It is exporting less than it is importing with the result that last financial year we had this massive balance of payments deficit of over $9 billion. It was $9 billion, not $9m. I submit, as I have been submitting for some considerable time, that there should be an appreciable devaluation of our currency to make our exports more competitive on world markets and to reduce imports. That is the only answer unless we are going to continue in this vicious circle of having to attract overseas capital to this country and having to hold up interest rates at massive

levels in order to meet a balance of payments deficit.
Unfortunately, the Government is a disciple of a discredited economist, Milton Friedman. It has pursued Friedman's monetarist policies over the last seven years with the result that our economy is now on its knees. I would like to quote to the House parts of an article in the Weekend Australian of 15 and 16 August which contains an interview with John Kenneth Galbraith, who would be the most respected economist still alive. I make the point that this was before the agreement between the Australian Labor Party and the Australian Council of Trade Unions on a new prices and incomes policy. That is very important when reading this article. The headline states: 'Monetarism is not the answer to economic woes, says Galbraith', and the article begins:
With the US economy nearing crisis, economist John Kenneth Galbraith argues that only income policies based on price and wage controls can now ensure the survival of Western economies.
Galbraith said that in an interview with the West German weekly news magazine Der Spiegel. The Australian article goes on:
President Reagan's economic policy-
I mention President Reagan because his Administration, this Government and the Thatcher Government in the United Kingdom are all following the same monetarist policies as laid down by Milton Friedman-
is pushing the United States into a crisis with high deficits, rocketing interest rates and unemployment because it is based on the false premise of stimulating growth by aiding the wealthy at the expense of low-income Americans . . .
He warns that only adoption of a comprehensive incomes policy with wage and price guidelines can cure the West's deep-rooted economic ills.
That is precisely the policy which was outlined here tonight by the Leader of the Opposition. The article continues:
The . . . former Harvard University Professor says monetarist Nobel prize-winning economist, Milton Friedman, who inspired the Reagan administration's economic policy, was an 18th century economist, though in several Western countries governments have espoused his call for a reduction of the monetary mass, less welfare spending and his supplyside theories.
I interpose to say that the policies of Milton Friedman were the policies followed during the latter part of the 19th century, right through the 20th century to the mid-l 930s, and took the world into two depressions. It was not until Lord Keynes's policies were followed that the world was able to come out of the Great Depression of the 1930s. The article continues:
A firm believer in the need for State intervention in economic life, Mr Galbraith says: 'I believe 1 am in a luckier situation than my colleague Friedman. My name is mentioned in

relation with the past, but with a past that was successful. He stands, on the contrary, for an unsuccessful present.
Milton Friedman's theory, monetarism, bases the management of our complex modern economy on a single tool: monetary policy.
He says it is wrong to throw on the scrap heap the theory of demand management based on principles enunciated by the late British economist (Lord) John Maynard Keynes, during the pre-war depression.
') consider my economic ideas as fully timely. It would be wrong to throwaway the principle of State-controlled demand management. But the Keynes version, tailored for the Great Depression of the 1930s, is no longer adapted to our times. Keynes himself would be the last man to claim so. His main achievement was that he came out for a policy that suited his time'.

Instead of that inflation in Australia, as I said, is actually higher than it is amongst our major trading partners, and it is rising. Let us look at the unemployment rate. It stands at 7 per cent.

Mrs CHILD (Henty) (lO.Q)-In a statement made by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) on a review of housing policy some months ago, he had this to say:

868 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Adjournment

Private home ownership is an important element in the cohesiveness of Australian families, which is fundamental to the strength of our society.
His paper then went on to outline what initiatives the Government would take to assist home buyers. He gave very little time in his statement to the home renter, except for what he said on page 5. There he identified one of the problems with housing as being the increased rental payments for some persons in rental accommodation. It is mainly about those in rental accommodation that I wish to speak, because this matter has been totally neglected in the Budget-except for some provision for some pensioners.
Because of this concentration on the needs of home buyers, the difficulties of both private and public tenants have been largely ignored. The trends within the housing sector should be seen in a broader context-a massive shortage of public housing and a dramatically shrinking private rental sector, together with a serious decline in the housing industry. It is essential in any discussion on housing that it is recognised that the housing sector has been permitted to operate on the basis of a fundamental inequity-a substantial proportion of the population has been denied any access to home ownership and has been forced to live in an unregulated private rental sector or grossly inadequate public rental sector.
Social workers, industry groups and other concerned parties have estimated that over 100,000 families are homeless in Australia. Another quarter of a million are making do in makeshift or mobile homes, and more than 80,000 are waiting for public housing. None of these things is happening by chance, nor are they beyond control. They are inevitable outcomes of basic changes to the nation's economy which this Government's policies are designed to achieve. A fundamental right of all Australians should be that they are adequately fed, clothed and housed-and yet in Australia poverty is rampant, and worsening. As Professor Henderson said in the Age on 20 April:
There is no doubt that the Australian Government does not have an effective system of income security. It leaks like a sieve and people who fall through it depend on voluntary agencies having enough money to assist them.
People on low incomes, on pensions and on unemployment benefit are increasingly disadvantaged by the increasing high rents they are forced to pay. The Government's housing policy is an example of the neglect of the poor and needy by the Government's economic policies. It has been produced as a major contribution towards enhancing high private ownership. Not only will it not achieve this objective, but also its effect will

be to exclude even more Australians from owning or renting a home.
The cost of housing finance has increased dramatically in recent years. In 1968 bank interest rates were 5 per cent. They now range from about 11.5 per cent to 13.5 per cent-probably even higher-while building society rates are around 15 per cent. Not only has finance for home purchase become more expensive, but also it has grown scarcer. Funds for housing from traditional sources are drying up, a trend reflected in the dramatic decline in new dwellings being built. August 1981 figures show a decline of 12 per cent in the rate of new dwelling commencements from August 1980 figures, and dwelling approvals again declined in the December 1981 quarter, and again this year. The building industry is on its knees.
All this is taking its toll of the low income home purchaser, and despite statements by the Treasurer, there is an increase in mortgage defaults. Figures from the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation, the largest mortgage insurer, show an increase in the level of mortgage default claims, expressed as a percentage of total premiums, from 4 per cent in 1970-71 to a staggering 35 per cent in 1979-80-a savage indictment of the Government's housing policy. Of course, interest rates are not the only factor in the increasing level of mortgage default. Continually high levels of unemployment and the ever increasing cost of living also play a major role.
However, the housing crisis is not only about reduced access to home ownership. Because the housing finance sector is being squeezed by government economic policy, tenants in both public and private rental are being hit as much as, if not more than, owners or aspiring owners. If the current squeeze on housing finance is continued, two major trends in the private rental market can be expected to worsen. Interest rate increases are forcing private landlords to increase rates to maintain returns on their investment. Further, as other forms of investment become more attractive we will see a decreased investment in the housing sector and a further decline in the supply of private rental housing. The growing shortage of rental stock leaves tenants fair game for increasing rents. Those in Victoria are experiencing serious difficulties following almost three decades of Liberal government. Successive Liberal governments in Victoria have so decimated the housing industry that only 3 per cent of all-
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.

Mr RUDDOCK (Dundas) (IO.S)-In view of
the fact that the honourable member for Henty (Mrs Child), who has just resumed her seat, canvassed some matters which directly impinge upon the reputation of this Government as a government that has compassion and cares for the needs of people, I take the opportunity to highlight some of the initiatives that have recently been announced by the Government as a demonstration of its compassion-and its care for fellow human beings. I know it is always easy to talk about issues in such a way as to portray one's opponents as people without care or compassion and to argue that one is the only person who has a monopoly on conscience and concern. For that reason, I occasionally like to indicate that I do not think that anybody in this place is entitled to say that other honourable members are not aware of the needs of people. I think members are confronted with the needs of the people more frequently than most other people.
Honourable members, no matter on which side of the House they sit, develop an appreciation of those needs. The difference is very often in the way in which they believe that more meaningful results can be achieved. Useful gains in productivity can lead to a situation in which a great deal more can be done for all people. That is certainly a view held strongly on this side of the House. I am not suggesting that it is not a view held by those on the other side of the House, but I sometimes have my doubts. The assumption seems at times to be that there is, without increased productivity, a bottomless pit into which governments can delve to obtain the necessary resources to meet the needs which we appreciate. The recent Budget is a demonstration of the Government's capacity to recognise particular problems which have become more pressing. I identify one with which I have been associated and which I am very pleased has been achieved in this Budget. I refer to the grants that will be now available in the form of a mobility allowance to severely handicapped people.
Mr Milton-It is not enough.
Mr RUDDOCK-It might be argued that it is not enough. I am pleased that the principle has been recognised, and recognised by this Government. I am very proud to be associated with the initiative and I know that many organisations interested in the needs of handicapped people very much welcome the initiative although they too would argue that it might not be as generous as it would be if we were able to write a blank cheque. An organisation in New South Wales, the Association of Quadriplegics and Paraplegics, led by Dr John Grant, a very noted neurosurgeon, has

been actively campaigning for this allowance for some time. Its research worker, David Kerr, a lad who lives in my area, has spent a great deal oftime putting proposals before government and before members of parliament. Before the last Budget that organisation wrote to all members of parliament in an effort to raise the interest of members in this subject. Before the consideration of this Budget. I was pleased to arrange for members of that organisation to see the Government members back bench committee that had an interest in these questions and to meet with those members and put their views fully before them. I was also able to assist, as no doubt others who were aware of their need did, in bringing those views to the attention of the Ministry.
I want to let honourable members know something of the nature of this allowance because it is important to recognise that, whilst assistance was provided before in the form of reductions in sales tax to enable handicapped people to purchase a motor vehicle, not all handicapped people are able to purchase a motor vehicle or to avail themselves of a motor vehicle to assist them in making their way to their place of employment. This allowance will assist them directly if it is necessary for them to take a taxi, to use a bus designed especially for handicapped people or to use public transport, albeit with difficulty. I think it is a measure of this Government's compassion and concern that it has identified this group of people and that for the first time an allowance will be available to people who are severely handicapped. Of course, if others want to look at the allowance at some future time it can always be reviewed and adjusted upwards.

Mr FREE (Macquarie) (10.10)-1 know that in the past week honourable members on both sides of the House have been particularly interested in gauging public reaction to the Budget. Tonight I wish to refer to two reactions which concern me and which ought to concern the House. The first concerns an invalid pensioner who came to see me yesterday. He has a dependent wife and has no income apart from the married rate of pension. They are paying off a home. Because their incomes are below the tax threshold they do not pay tax. Because they do not pay tax they will get no relief through the taxation system for interest paid on the mortgage.
I wish to set out the details of their position. I have looked at their papers. In the financial year

870 REPRESENTATIVES 24 Aug~st 1982

Adjournment

1981-82 they paid $1,501.20 to the lending authority in monthly instalments, which were $120 at the start of the year, increasing to $125.60 by the end of the year. Over that year the principal of the loan was reduced by $212.78. An amount of $1,288.42 was paid as interest at a rate of 14.5 per cent. It appears to me that the interest paid over the Government's 10 per cent limit was $345.22. If these people were better off, if they received additional income to take them over the tax threshold, they would become eligible for the tax rebate scheme and would receive a rebate of 30 per cent of that $345.22 that they paid in interest, which amounts to $103.56. That would reduce their monthly repayments by $8.63, an amount which would mean quite a deal to people in that situation. As it is, because their income is so small, they will get nothing. Surely this is an anomaly. Surely it is an anomaly that people in better circumstances than this couple can, under this new scheme, obtain relief from rising interest rates through the tax system but that these people, battling to pay off their home on the married rate of pension, get no relief. Why is it? It is because they are worse off. It is an anomaly which I hope the Minister for Communications (Mr N. A. Brown), who is in the House, and the Treasurer (Mr Howard) will look at with some sympathy.

Dear Mr Free,

Please Mr Free, show us the way.

Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (IO.14)-Last week, because of a turnaround by the Australian Democrats in the Senate, the Senate passed the Government's superphosphate and nitrogenous fertiliser subsidy legislation. They had previously opposed it. As the representative of a rural electorate I had been deeply concerned at this antifarmer approach. However, as I inquired further into this matter it became obvious to me that the welfare of my farmer constituents was academic

to the position taken by the Democrats in opposing this subsidy. All previous fertiliser legislation
had provided for the payment of a bounty to the superphosphate manufacturers. This was notwithstanding the publicity that inferred that this bounty was a subsidy to farmers. That the payment was truly a bounty to the manufacturer was supported by payment being restricted to superphosphate of Australian manufacture. Further, sea transportation of rock phosphate to Australia is reserved for the Australian National Line, increasing the cost of the finished product substantially.
The legislation which has just passed the Senate made the payment truly a subsidy. The legislation said to users: 'Here is a sum of money paid to recognise your disability of large fertiliser costs. You may spend it in the most competitive market'. It was the conversion of the assistance from bounty to manufacturer to subsidy to consumer that enraged the Australian Democrats. Why was that so? I choose to believe that they were led to this position by their spokesman on such maters, Senator John R. Siddons. Senator Siddons has a spare time job as Chairman of Directors of Siddons Industries Ltd, which manufactures the Sidchrome spanner. This 'non-adjustable wrench' is in receipt of substantial bounty protection. In 1980-81 the Australian Government paid bounties totalling $367,224 in this area. Siddons
Industries received all but $378.84 of that money. The shock that went through the board room of Siddons Industries at the thought of a government saying to our farmers: 'Here is, say, $5 each; go buy your spanners in the best market', is easy to consider. This unreasonable reaction by Siddons and its top executive was a clear message to my constituents to buy someone else's spanners in future. I hope that they do so. The significant message to me in this exercise of personal survival is to question the integrity of a party that can support such parochial actions after campaigning under the slogan 'We'll keep the bastards honest'.

Mr KENT (Hotham) (10.19)-1 should like tonight to voice my concern and the concern of my electors about the way in which the Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) and his Department treat many of our war veterans. Although this matter has been raised many times in this House, the Minister for Veterans' Affairs remains as intransigent as his Department. Furthermore, the Minister has accused me, in a letter and in the local Press, of seeking to make political capital because of my efforts to achieve a long denied benefit for one of my constituents. I am raising this matter to let honourable members judge for themselves.
In April this year a constituent of mine, a World War II veteran, came to me complaining about undue delays regarding his total and permanent incapacity pension. The veteran, who, by the

way, is a very ill elderly gentleman, was recommended by his repatriation medical officer for the TPI pension in December 1980. For various reasons the Department equivocated on his application until he eventually asked for my intervention to help bring his case to a conclusion. I promptly wrote to the Deputy Commissioner of the Department of Veterans' Affairs pointing out the inordinate delay in this case, indicating that such delays may worsen the veteran's health and requesting an urgent resolution of the matter. As there were further delays, I wrote repeatedly to the Deputy Commissioner as well as to the Minister. Knowing the slow reflexes of the Minister and his Department, I also issued a Press statement to hasten their knee-jerk and help my constituent and, indeed, the many other veterans that suffer from the Department's delaying tactics. I quote from the Moorabbin Standard News of 28 June 1982:
Mr Lewis Kent, MHR for Hotham. has issued a warning to war veterans this week that rampant bureaucracy in the Veterans' Affairs Department is causing outrageous delays in having pension claims settled.
"I have contacted the Minister for Veterans' Affairs to complain about the number of my constituents who have had their cases pigeon-holed." he told The Standard.
Mr Kent said he was advised that approximately 800 claims for pensions had been put aside by the Department pending the outcome of cases in the Federal Court.
"Constituents have told me that some claims have been pending for 18 months," he said.
"The right of one veteran to take his grievance to court should not mean that hundreds of others have to suffer," added Mr Kent.
"It is sheer spite by the department to hold up entitlements to these aged people who fought for their country," he said.
"Most veterans are anxious to have their claims settled and the intransigence of the Department is aggravating their anxiety and ill-helath.
"One constituent I spoke to has a serious heart condition and is on oxygen for 20 hours a day. In this case, and in many others, the Department may succeed where the Japanese failed and bring about his death," Mr Kent concluded.
I am proud to advise the House that my intervention was successful and that the Minister advised me in a letter dated 17 August that my constituent's pension had been increased to the special TPI rate, with effect from 15 July. In the same letter, however, the Minister accused me of being 'more concerned to seek to make political capital than to ascertain the facts'. Let me relate the facts of a couple of other cases to the House.
The widow of a World War II veteran recently contacted me about her husband's 20-year battle with the Department to have his hearing loss attributed to his war service. Ironically, his disability was finally accepted but he died before ever receiving a hearing aid. Another veteran on

872 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Adjournment

whose behalf I have also written to the Minister has complained that his appeal in April 1982 was adjourned so that the Department could obtain further medical evidence. The appointment with the doctor was made for August 1982. This is a delay of four months, merely for a medical appointment. I could go on but I will simply say that I wish that the Minister and his Department would perceive their job as one of trying to help veterans receive their just entitlements instead of endlessly procrastinating and obstructing and denying claims.
Mr MILTON (La Trobe) (10.24)-There are four main themes to the nuclear debate which constantly recur and which cannot be explained away by the propagandists and apologists for the nuclear industry. First and foremost is the poor economic future which is facing the industry as a result of the public repudiation of nuclear power stations. Secondly, there is the complete failure of scientists and engineers to develop any satisfactory method of disposing of radioactive waste. Thirdly there is the vulnerability of nuclear power
stations to a multiplicity of accidents involving the release of dangerous radioactivity and, finally, there is the increasing number of nuclear weapons which are multiplying, not only in the military arsenals of the super-powers but also, more frighteningly, in the military arsenals of minor powers
such as Israel and Pakistan.
The continued profitability of the uranium
mining industry depends upon the economic viability of nuclear power generation around the world and the plain fact is that nuclear power has never been economic. It has always been subsidised by governments. The costs of research and development, of the treatment and disposal of waste, the decommissioning of nuclear reactors, such as Three Mile Island, and the supervision of the international and national nuclear fuel cycle are not included in the price of the electricity delivered to the householder.
Governments also grant generous depreciation and investment allowances and special credit and insurance facilities in support of the nuclear industry. In the specific case of the uranium mining industry in Australia the net benefit to the Australian community is likely to very low when the costs of Government concessions to the industry are taken into account. In addition, the costs of the infrastructure for many mining ventures in the form of railways, roads, water supplies and sewerage are provided by the Government. When all of these economic factors are taken into account, economists have estimated that nuclear generated electricity would cost between three and seven times as much as it does at present. I turn now to

the disposal of nuclear waste. We can all recall the triumphant announcement by various leading scientists that the problem has been solved. None of these statements has turned out to be true and no country in the world has been able to find a way of disposing of high level radioactive waste from its nuclear power programs. We have all heard with horror of the wholesale dumping of low radioactive waste in the Pacific Ocean by the Japanese. The canisters containing such waste eventually leak into the ocean with unforeseen long term effects on the oceanic life forms. The disposal of high level radioactive waste poses even
greater hazards and proposals that it be contained in glass, deposited in deep mine shafts or rocketed into space have not been found to be scientifically or economically feasible. Even the synthetic rock concept development by Professor Ringwood at the Australian National University is not yet proven to be more than a laboratory exercise and the cost of the process may add too much to the already high operating costs of nuclear power stations. Reprocessing of the spent fuel is of course another possibility if one is prepared to use unlimited resources in producing plutonium for
nuclear weapons. The economics of reprocessing can be justified only as part of a military capability. I doubt that the Australian people at least would be prepared to foot the bill. At present in the United States of America the spent fuel is stored in ponds at nuclear reactor sites. If no practical solution to the disposal problem is found quickly nuclear power plants in the United States of America will have to close down as the storage ponds become choked with radioactive waste.
The third aspect of the nuclear debate which cannot be ignored by the apologists is the vulnerability of nuclear power stations to accidents. The nuclear industry likes to describe them as incidents but let us not be fooled by newspeak; they are accidents and serious ones at that. These accidents occur not only in power stations but also in uranium mines and in military aircraft, ships and military establishments. The details of many of these accidents have been kept under wraps by the American Government because of its policy that it will neither confirm nor deny the presence of nuclear weapons or components on its military fleets of ships and aircraft. Finally, there is the increasing proliferation of nuclear weapons, the existence of which pose an ever increasing threat to the survival of the human race. No one can doubt that the threat of a nuclear war has prevented the outbreak of a conventional weapons war between the two superpowers, but at what cost? We are constantly on a knife-edge of uncertainty with the ever present possibility

Papers

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 873

that psychologically disturbed world leaders will press the button that will unleash hordes of nuclear warhead rockets. No one nation can win such a war. It is quite conceivable that hundreds of millions of people would die completely unaware that a miclear war had commenced. With intercontinental bombers and ballistic missiles, hydrogen and neutron bombs, multiple warheads, long range cruise missiles, anti-ballistic missiles and multiple independently targeted warheads the variety and extent of the new generation of long range nuclear weapons is staggering in both its complexity and its cost. We must also not forget that with the development of military satellites we are faced with another expensive and dangerous leap into a new dimension of nuclear weapons development. I have brought this serious issue to the attention of the Parliament tonight because quite obviously the Government and others seem to think that the Australian Labor Party has forgotten these matters.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
House adjourned at 10.29 p.m.

The following answers to questions were circulated:

VIP Aircraft: Costs
Question No. 3758)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 16 February 1982:
(I) Was there an under-expenditure of $527 757 in Division 504, 'Conveyance of Governor-General, Ministers of State and Others by RAAF and Department of Transport Aircraft', in the Department of Prime Minister and Cabinet appropriation in 1980-81.
(2) Was there, in the same period, (a) 302 hours of 'special purpose flying' charged to his Department and (b) 202 hours credited to Division 504 because of an over-estimate by his Department the previous year.
(3) Is the average hourly flying rate for the 3 types of aircraft used by the VIP fleet (No. 34 Squadron) $1045; ifso, is it a fact that the 504 credited hours referred to in part (2) would have cost $526,680; if not, what is the cost of these 504 hours.
(4) How much VIP flying time has been regularly charged to his Department (a) in each of the years 1976-77 to 1979-80 and (b) in 1981-82 to the date of the preparation of the answer to this question.
(5) Is it specified procedure that RAAF pilots maintain their requisite number of flying hours in the carriage of VIPs; if so, where and when was this stipulated.

(a) an estimate of 320 hours for RAAF hours underflown was credited to Department of Prime Minister and Cabinet, and
(b) 202 hours were credited because of an over-estimate by Department of Defence in the previous year.
(3) In view of the considerable difference in the operating costs of the three aircraft types the use of an average rate in calculations is not appropriate; recoveries are based on the hours flown by, and operating costs of each aircraft type.
The two components (202 hours and 302 hours) of the 504 hours referred to in the honourable member's question relate to different financial years operations, consequently it is not correct to cost the aggregate as shown.
A credit of $231 ,441 based on April 1980 rates was allowed for the 202 hours over-estimated by Defence in 1979-80, and $276,005 for the 302 hours credited in 1980-81. The balance of the under-expenditure resulted from special purpose flying hours being lower than hours on which estimates were based, partly offset by increased flying hour costs and incidental charges.
(4) (a) The flying hours credited to Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet and hence borne by the Department of Defence for the financial years 1976-77 to 1979-80 were:

Financial year

Flying hours

(6) What consideration has been given to RAAF pilots of No. 34 Squadron maintaining their flying hours in ways such .as flights for coastal surveillance, fisheries protection, aerial fire fighting, resources reconnaissance, aerial ambulance and similar purposes.
(7) Is the crediting of costs of the VIP flights to his Department an attempt to hide the real costs of those flights; if not, what is the reason for this practice.
Mr SincIair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) In his statement to the House of Representatives on 24 October 1967 (Hansard pages 2149 and 2150) the then Prime Minister (Mr Holt) advised that charges to be made against appropriations of the Prime Minister's Department for VIP use of 34 Squadron aircraft would be calculated on a marginal or extra cost basis.
The formula for calculating these costs included provision that where the annual service flying hours fall short of the estimated number of flying hours which the squadron would have flown as an integral part of the RAAF without any VIP flight responsibilities, the cost of the shortfall would be charged against the normal activities of the squadron and the amount otherwise attributable to VIP flights reduced accordingly. This formula has been followed since 1967.
As annual accounts are closed prior to 30 June the claim on the Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet for the final quarter is based on estimates, with necessary adjustments being effected in the claim for the following quarter. Adjustment can be either a debit or a credit to the Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet.
In accordance with the formula outlined above, for the 1980-81 financial year:

1976-77

336

1977-78

239

1978-79

234

1979-80

317

Flying hours

1972-73

267

1973-74

236

1974-75

401

1975-76

116

(b) The flying hours to be credited to Division 504 are determined when the final quarter claim for the financial year is being prepared; therefore no credits have been allowed to date for the 1981-82 financial year.
(5) RAAF pilots have no such requirement. Pilot currency requirements are fulfilled during any operational or training flight even if the aircraft is empty.
(6) The RAAF pilots in 34 Squadron, maintain their flying hours with their current duties. To use the Squadron for coastal surveillance, fisheries protection and aerial ambulance would be a very uneconomical use of its resources. Aerial firefighting and resources reconnaissance require specialised aircraft not flown by 34 Squadron.
(7) No. As indicated in the answer to (2) above, there has been no change to the charging formula since 1967.
New Airport Site in South Australia
(Question No. 3825)

24 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 875

(I) Has his attention been drawn to references by an officer of the former Department of Transport to conflict between State and Commonwealth methods of land acquisition, as they affect the selection of a new airport site in South Australia, at the public hearing of the Public Works Committee in Adelaide on 3 December 1981, if so, can he state what is the nature of any conflict.

Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I understand that the problems referred to by the honourable member relate not to conflict between the Commonwealth and the State but to the limitations on each of their respective methods of land acquisition. I am informed that, in the context of securing a new airport site for Adelaide, a major factor in determining the role of the Commonwealth Government is the limits that exist with respect to the Commonwealth's ability to compulsorily acquire land for airport purposes in advance of development. Further, existing State and Local Government legislation limits the degree to which an airport site and its surrounds can be reserved and protected without purchase by the developing authority.
(2) Yes. Preliminary discussions have been held between the Commonwealth and State Governments through the South Australian Airfields Committee. I am advised that the State Government has commissioned consultants to examine various aspects of ownership transfer.
(3) There are currently no specific proposals before Government for the sale of facilities at Adelaide Airport. However, the general issue of future ownership of airports and airport terminals has been referred to an Interdepartmental Committee and will be addressed by Government when it considers the Committee's report.
(4) No.
(5) No.
(6) An Interdepartmental Committee has been studying various aspects of major airport development. Decisions are yet to be taken on the matters to which the honourable member refers. The Government decided that to meet the needs of the people of Adelaide for direct access to international flights it should proceed with the development of an international terminal using the recently established terminal at Townsville as a basis for design.

"MAS 'Melbourne'
(Question No. 4246)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 23 March 1982:
(I) What was the cost of maintaining the aircraft carrier HMAS Melbourne in service 1980-81.
(2) What is the estimated yearly cost of operation of HMAS Melbourne in the role allocated to it after the acquisition of HMS Invincible.
Mr SincIair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Details of actual costs incurred on individual units in service are not held in my Department. Assessments are made based on the apportionment of total operating expenditures for all activities. On this basis the direct operating cost of HMAS Melbourne in 1980-81, including repair costs, was approximately $28m.
(2) HMAS Melbourne has been placed in contingent reserve as a non-operational unit, at a direct cost of $182 000. Following the British Government's decision to withdraw its offer to sell HMS Invincible, the Government is now reexamining the options available to Australia to meet the needs that earlier led the Government to decide to buy HMS Invincible. Depending on the outcome of these considerations there may be a need for interim measures such as re-commissioning HMAS Melbourne.

Medical Benefits for Health Screening Services
(Question No. 4322)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
(I) Has the Minister or the Department of Health released any new guidelines on payment of medical benefits for health screening services as they relate to periodic screening for sexually transmitted diseases (STD) associated with occupation, for example, prostitution.
(2) Are any health funds refusing to pay benefits for these services; if so, what is (a) the reason for the policy change and (b) the implications of this on the spread of STD.
Mr Carlton-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No.
(2) Yes. (a) There has been no recent change in policy. The Health Insurance Act was amended on I July 1978 (by adding section 19 (5)) so as to prevent the payment ofmedical benefits for health screening services. It was considered inappropriate that the Medical Benefits Scheme should bear the cost of medical examinations or tests not reasonably required for the treatment of a specific medical condition of the

patient. On this basis, the routine periodic screening of prostitutes does not attract medical benefits. (b) The nonavailability of medical benefits should not have adverse implications concerning the spread of STD. The State and Territory public health authorities provide services to carry out tests to detect the presence of STD. These services are provided without charge. Government responsibility for public health controls, including the spread of STD, rests with the States and the Territories. They have expressed the view that all STD testing should be carried out by the State/Territorial public health services to enable those authorities to be informed fully of the nature and extent of STD in the community. This would improve their ability to deal with the spread of STD in a co-ordinated and systematic manner.
HMS 'Invincible'
(Question No. 4346)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
(I) What are the new arrangements for payment for the purchase of HMS Invincible.
(2) Does the allocation of $81,000,000 in the supplementary estimates mean that the payment program set out in his initial announcement is no longer valid.
(3) [n view of the current operational status of HMS Invincible, will advance payments be made prior to the vessel's return to the United Kingdom, or withdrawal from combat duties.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) On 25 February 1982 my predecessor announced to the House that subject to negotiation of details the Government had decided to purchase HMS Invincible at the total project cost of $478m. It was not expected then that any payments would be made in the financial year 1981-82.
(2) Subsequent negotiations with the United Kingdom Government indicated that an initial payment by Australia could be required on or before 9 July 1982, and provision of an amount of $81.6m was included in the Additional Estimates for this purpose.
(3) The sales Agreement was not formalised and no payments were made to the United Kingdom government in respect of the purchase ofHMS Invincible.
