Operation of Visual Display Units: Department of Transport and Construction
(Question No. 4427)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Transport and Construction, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
In respect of the Minister's Department and statutory authorities within his jurisdiction. (a) what steps have been taken to make operators of visual display units and screen based equipment aware of the need for a sufficient break from operating this equipment, having regard to the nature of the task and the need for involvement in substantially different activities during the break, as per Public Service Board memorandum 81/418 entitled 'Operation of Visual Display Units and other Screen Based Equipment', (b) what percentage of their working day (i) are operators expected to spend on operating duties and (ii) do operators spend on other duties and (c) how many keystrokes per 7 hour 21 minute day (i) are operators expected to complete and (ii) do operators actually complete.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) Within the Department and statutory authorities under my jurisdiction, Public Service Board memorandum 81/418 has been circulated to all Word Processing and Computing Section Managers. Data Processing and Word Processing Operators are aware of the need for sufficient breaks during the operation of visual display units. Although there is no fixed departmental policy on time period between breaks, procedures are in operation to ensure that adequate breaks are taken.
(b) (i) In each of the regional offices of the Department and in the statutory authorities for which I am regponsible (Snowy Mountains Engineering Corporation, Australian National Railways, Australian National Line) the percentage of the working day that designated operators of Screen Based Equipment spend at their screens varies between 50 per cent and lOOper cent, and averages around 75 per cent.
(ii) As a result the time spent by Screen Based Operators on other duties averages 25 per cent.
(c) (i) The amount of keystrokes that operators are expected to complete is based on the Public Service minimum qualification of 55,000 keystrokes per day.
(ii) Actual completion of the keystrokes depends on the proportion of the day spent in each office on keyboard activities but is in line with the Public Service standard rate.
Qantas Airways Ltd
(Question No. 4490)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Aviation, upon notice, on 28 April 1982:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to an article in the Australian of 27 April 1982 reporting a recommendation from aviation consultants that Air New Zealand cut staff almost 30 per cent to United States standards, put staff and union representatives on the board of directors to minimise industrial disturbance and investigate partnership with the Australian Government or an Australian airline.

(2) Will he investigate the economics of similar steps for Qantas Airways Ltd, possibly in conjunction with Air Niugini and other Pacific island airlines.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) It would be difficult to translate the consultant's recommendations regarding airline staffing and industrial matters directly into Australian conditions where there is a different social and industrial relations framework. Qantas recently commissioned a comprehensive review of certairi aspects of the Company's organisation by management consultants. 1 understand that Qantas has implemented those recommendations which are intended to strengthen the Company's marketing drive, improve efficiency and provide for the future with planned development of staff.
With regard to any partnership with, or merger of, Qantas and other international airlines it would be inappropriate for Australia to take any initiatives that might be seen by other countries of the region as an attempt by Australia to take over their airlines. The Government, however, would give consideration to any approaches those countries might make on the matters suggested.
Department of Veterans' Affairs: Mr J. Evans
(Question No. 4577)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 5 May 1982:
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the evidence given to the Senate Standing Committee on Science and the Environment on I April 1982 by Mr John Evans of Melbourne, wherein Mr Evans made statements regarding his dealings with the Department of Veterans' Affairs, the Minister, and his predecessor, and sought a public apology from the Minister.
(2) What steps has the Minister taken as a result of Mr Evans' evidence.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) Yes.
(2) 1 wrote to Mr Evans on 28 April 1982 detailing my position in relation to the evidence given by him. The following is the text of the letter:
'I have just seen that part of your Submission to the Senate Standing Committee on Science and the Environment in which you made certain statements about the nature of contact between us. The thrust of your remarks as reported later in the media is that lowe you an apology for my recent action in regard to the so-called third draft of the Fleming/Evans paper.
I have no wish to exacerbate what appears to have generated a situation of aggravation between us. However, 1 do want you to understand that 1 see no need to apologise for any of my actions in this matter.
On the night of 10 March 1982, when you rang me in a state of some concern that 1 had tabled the document, 1 went over with you the reasons for my so doing. I take the opportunity here to outline to you again those reasons. First, there was a need to counter media claims which had again arisen around that time that the particular information in this document (whatever draft) was 'explosive', 'sensitive' and for such

878 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Answers to Questions

reasons it was being 'suppressed' by the Government. Secondly, with the exception of some lesser items in the document, all of the references had been made available in the Department's bibliography for departmental contact medical officers which contains some 780 references. More important than either of these reasons is the fact that this document, or at least the second draft of it, had been tabled on 24 March 1981 in the Senate.
It was therefore almost 12 months after the tabling by Senator Grimes of the second draft and a media revival of interest (which 1had thought to have died) particularly in the 'third' draft that I took the opportunity to table it. There are several points in relation to this which ought also to be made. The first is that in no significant way does draft 'three' differ from that tabled by Senator Grimes a year ago. The second is that, as I did prior to the tabling in 1981 by Senator Grimes, I indicated I was unclear as to the 'ownership' of the information contained in the document and referred to in your role in its preparation. I am satisfied that my action has at least clarified your position and that of the Department in relation to the attribution of the material.
I am puzzled as to why, when 12 months earlier you made no objection to the actions of Senator Grimes, you now so vehemently object to my virtually repeating his action. I simply do not understand why you have acted in what is clearly a contradictory manner in respect of the two occasions but repeat my observation to you on the evening of 10th March 1982, that, with the tabling of the document on 24 March 1981, I was and still am of the view that the matter is now finally put to rest.
I intend to send a copy of this note to the Chairman of the Senate Standing Committee on Science and the Environment and, beyond that, consider the matter closed'.
Priorities for Ethnic Communities
(Question No. 4612)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 6 May 1982.
(1) Has his attention been drawn to the views of Richard Rodriguez on page 92 of Time, 19 April 1982
(2) Is it a fact that the interests of migrants do not necessarily coincide with those professional ethnics who wish to maintain a reservoir of non-English speaking, culturally distinct communities.
Mr Hodges-The answer to the honourable member's question is as foilows:
(1) Yes.
(2) Yes. I should also add that the Australian Government is committed to the preservation and development of a culturally diversified but socially cohesive Australian society, free of racial tensions and offering equality of opportunity to all those living here. The policy recognises the rights of persons to foster and preserve their cultural heritages and languages. However, this does not imply support for the formation of enclaves which limit contact with mainstream society and institutions. The Government recognises the importance of proficiency in English for all Australians to facilitate communications and equality of opportunity. Significant resources are being outlayed to provide English language learning opportunities for persons unable to speak English proficiently.

Department of Transport Reorganisation
(Question No. 4616)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Aviation, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
With reference to the recent statement about the proposed reorganisation of the Department of Transport and the divorcing of the aviation divisions from the surface transport divisions, (a) do any of the additional estimates of expenditure take into account the extra costs related to this exercise; if so, in what section of the additional estimates of expe:Jditure are these costs contained, if not, what additional costs are to be incurred by the departmental reorganisation, in particular,
(i) what is the quantum of such costs,
(ii) is the proposed aviation division to be established in separate and distinct offices from the surface transport divisions and
(iii) is the continuing process of compulsory staff transfers from Central Office, Melbourne to Canberra to continue for the aviation division,
(b) in relation to administiative responsibility, does the Secretary of the Department continue to exercise control, real or implied, over the officers employed in the proposed aviation division and
(c) as statements on the proposed new aviation division indicate that there is to be a decrease in second division positions, but an increase in salaries of those second division positions retained, what second division positions are to
(i) be elimimated,
(ii) be retained and
(ii) in relation to part (c) (ii),what is
(A) the existing salary and classification of the second division positions and
(B) the proposed salary after the reorganisation of the new aviation division.
Mr Fife-The response to the honourable member's questions is as follows:
(a) (i) and (ii) Owing to the Government's recent initiative to change existing administrative arrangements including the establishment of a separate Department of Aviation, these questions are not relevant.
(a) (iii) The program for transfer of staff from Central Office Melbourne to Canberra will be unchanged by the new arrangements.
(b) Owing to changed administrative arrangements this question is no longer relevant.
(c) (i), (ii) and (iii) Statements relating to a decrease in the number of second division positions referred to the former Department of Transport as a whole and not to the former Office of Civil Aviation.
The reorganisation of the former Department of Transport involved considerable rationalisation of functions and reorganisation of Divisions and Branches. Given the changed administrative arrangements which involved creation of the separate Department of Aviation, it would not be meaningful to attempt to identify positions in terms of those that were preexisting and those that are new.

Indian Ocean: Soviet Naval Presence
(Question No. 4641)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 6 May 1982:
Further to my question No. 2193 regarding the continued build-up of a Soviet naval presence in the Indian Ocean region,
(a) what is the extent of the naval facility presently available to Soviet ships at Maputo in Mozambique and has that facility recently been greatly expanded,
(b) what degree of access to workshops and rail communications do the Soviets enjoy at Beira and Nacala in Mozambique and are these workshops, which originally were established by the Portuguese, capable of producing fuel tankers for the rail-system and thereby supplementing the re-supply of Soviet vessels,
(c) what naval facilities are presently available to Soviet vessels in Angola,
(d) is the Soviet naval facility at Aden in The People's Democratic of Yemen, along with the facility already available on the island of Socotra, being expanded; if so, to what extent and what are the likely strategic implications of this expansion for the region as a whole,
(e) is the Soviet naval repair and supply facility on the island of Dahlak in Ethiopia also being upgraded,
(f) is the Soviet Union presently constructing a new naval base at the Bay of At Turbah on the South Yemen mainland near the island of Perim and what strategic significance would such a base have in controlling access to the Red Sea,
(g) in the light of this rapid build-up of a permanent Soviet naval presence in the region,
(i) what are the immediate strategic implications for the non-Marxist governments in the area,
(ii) what are the long-term strategic implications for those countries which must rely upon unrestricted lines of communications through the Indian Ocean-Red Sea region,
(iii) to what extent are lines of communication in the Atlantic made vulnerable by the presence of expanding Soviet bases on the East African coast, and
(iv) what are the implications of a Soviet control over access to the Red Sea and the Gulf of Aden,
(h) have the British any naval bases or communication centres at all on the east or west of the South African coast or off-shore, and
(j) what countries have naval facilities in:
(i) the Indian Ocean,
(ii) the Gulf of Aden, and
(iii) the Red Sea.
Mr SincIair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) The port of Maputo is capable of accommodating, and providing standard port services for, submarines and surface combatants to cruiser size. Its capability was enhanced by the arrival of a Soviet-built KLAIPEDA-class dry-dock in late 1981. Port activities are supported by Soviet technicians and harb,our pilots.
(b) Information is not available to confirm Soviet naval use of workshops or rail communications at Beira or Nacala.

880 REPRESENTATIVES 24 August 1982

Answers to Questions

(c) Port facilities at Luanda and Mocamedes are known to include berthing, bunkering and water. A radio communication station at Luanda reported to be operate by the Soviet Fisheries Directorate has also been established in recent years.
(d) to (f) A floating dock has been moored at Dahlak Island several years and a large pier and other facilities constructed there. Information is not available to confirm significant development for Soviet naval purposes there or at Aden, Socotra or at Turbah.
(g) The Soviet naval presence in the Indian Ocean is drawn from the Soviet Pacific and other fleets. It makes extensive use of afloat support, thereby limiting dependence on access to shore facilities within the region. There is no evidence of an increase in the level of presence in the last two years.
(h) to (j) Virtually all littoral countries have ports and facilities which can be used by naval forces. Both US and British forces have access to the facilities constructed by the United States on the British island of Diego Garcia. The US Navy also uses Bahrain as the principal port of call for its standing Middle East Force. The Soviet Union makes frequent use of Dahlak Island, Aden and the Socotra anchorage. French naval forces continue to make use of facilities at Djibouti, as well as of those in the French possession of Reunion.

(Question No. 4670)

What contribution does the Commonwealth make to the

River Murray Commission's Water Quality Monitoring

Program.

Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
